The Red Page
TO SHAKSPEARE AFTER 300 YEARS.
Bright, baffling Soul, least cdpturable of
themes,
Thou, who display'dst a life of common-
place,
Leaving no intimate word or personal
trace
Of high design outside the artistry
Of thy penned dreams,
Still shalt remain at heart unreal eternally.
Through human orbits tliy discourse to-day,
Despite thy formal pilgrimage, throbs on
In harmonies that cow Oblivion,
And, lihe the wind, with all-uncared effect
Maintain a sway
Not fore-desired, in trades unchosen and
unchecked.
And yet, at thy last breath, with mindless
note
The borough clocks as usual tongued the
hour,
The Avon just as always glassed the
tower,
Thy age was published on thy passing-bell
But in due rote
With other native men's accorded a like
knell.
And at the strokes some townsman (met,
maybe,
And thereon queried by some squire's
good dame
Driving ii* shopward) may have given thy
name.
With, “Yes, a tv or thy man and t vell-to-do;
Though, as for me,
I knew him but by just a neighbor's nod,
'tis true.
faith, few knew him much here, save by
word,
He having elsewhere led his busier life;
Though to be sure he left with us his
wife."
— “Ah, one of the tradesmen's sons, I now
recall.. ..
Witty, I've heard....
We did not know him.... Well, good-day.
Death comes to all."
So —like a strange bright-pinioned bird we
find
To mingle with the barndoor brood awhile,
Then vanish from their homely domicile —
Into man's poesy, we wot not whence,
Flew thy strange mind,
Lodged there a radiant guest, and sped for
ever thence.
—Thomas Hardy, in “The Book of Homage”
(Oxfoi’d University Press).
Reconstructing the Old Firm
Since 1!)10 a band of Imperialistic ideal-
ists lias been investigating the business
methods of the Old Firm of Bull, Anzac and
Co. The firm has been prospering in a
muddle-headed way, without articles of in-
corporation and without any clear ideals
before it; arid the business relations between
the senior partner and the junior partners
have never been even defined. Apparently
the senior partner has been content to let the
junior partners manage, or mismanage, the
branch offices as they liked, and the branch
offices have been too casual to attempt any
control of the general management. For in-
stance, the junior partners have allowed the
autocratic and somewhat crusty old gent to
commit the Firm to disputes with trade
rivals that threaten the disruption of the
Firm. A quarterly magazine, The Round
Table, was established by these investi-
gators who have local centres in all the
branches of the Firm ; and in it they pub-
lished interim reports of their discussions.
Now a comprehensive report of the problem
is" published, under the editorship of Lionel
Curtis, entitled The Problem of the Common-
wealth (Macmillan ;* Is. 6d.) ; also, the first
part of the preliminary investigation of the
historical origin of the Firm, The Common-
wealth of Nations (Macmillan; Gs.), under
the same editorship.
The latter is subtitled “An inquiry into the
nature of citizenship in the British Empire,
and into the mutual relations of the several
communities thereof.” This comprehensive
volume of over 700 pages indicates the
immensity of the work undertaken by the
groups: it is a lucid and tremendous founda-
tion of their argument; and it comprises
merely the first of three volumes contem-
plated. Whatever may be thought of the con-
clusions reached in the smaller volume, there
can be no doubt of the ponderous thorough-
ness with which the ground for their survey
has been explored. The main argument is
Within their own territorial limits the
Dominions control their own affairs; but in
the general government of the Empire they
exercise no voice whatever, while the Im-
perial Government has no power of com-
manding their resources for the mainte-
nance of the whole. Can the Empire continue
in this anomalous condition, or is it to
develop into the structure of a State or be
smashed up into a number of States? “No
question more momentous has ever been pro-
posed, for upon its solution depends the
stability, not merely of this Empire itself,
but of the whole structure of the world’s
society through which it extends.” How are
the Dominions to assume a genuine respon-
sibility for the greatest of all public in-
terests, those which determine the issues of
peace or war? And though this volume
closes merely with that query, the ground
leading up to the question has been covered
from the beginnings of history ; while a re-
markable feature of the volume (and the
next) is the series of diagrams and maps
that at a glance elucidate the main elements
of the problem.
The Problem of the Commonwealth is a
much less forbidding volume. It is devoted to
one single aspect of the Imperial problem,
that raised by the question how a British
citizen in the Dominions can acquire the
same control of foreign policy as one domi-
ciled in the British Isles. The careful
method of joint action is embodied, too, in
this volume. A draft report was prepared
and circulated throughout the groups,
with the result that the criticism it re-
ceived materially altered the final text. But,
for an Australian, there is one omission that
leaves the problem, for us, still unsettled.
“No attempt could be made within the com-
pass of this short report to discuss in detail
the position of India and the great Depend-
encies of the Commonwealth (the Empire).”
Nor, it will be noticed, has the. question of
India yet found place in the first part of the
large volume. Lionel Curtis is at present
on a pilgrimage throughout the Dominions
seeking the discussion of his project; but, in
the view of this page, he misinterprets his
mission. His aim should chiefly be to dis-
cover not how the Dominions will receive his
suggestion, but what sort of blokes the in-
habitants of the Dominions actually are. He
ought to try to discover what Australians,
for instance, think of party government —the
permanence of which he takes for granted—-
and what they think of India. Leaving India
out of his grandiose project is like trying to
produce the play of Othello with lago left out.
There is a hole in his beautiful and
logically-constructed scheme, through which
a large quantity of water might leak
into places where it is not particularly
wanted. Lionel Curtis may have a solution
of this question in his sleeve. It is up to
him to produce it before he leaves these
shores.
The issue put before us is whether the
Dominions are to become independent re-
publics, or whether this world-wide Com-
monwealth is destined to stand more closely
united. But it is open to question whether
we are to be compelled to chose either of the
alternatives, whether there is no middle way,
whether, indepd, this forcing of the question
upon us may not, in itself, compel us, against
our national will, to choose the first alter-
native. Than which this page could conceive
no greater tragedy.
J*
The Problem of the Commonwealth, apart
from its vital omission, is a lucid piece of
compact and logical argument. It is com-
mended to every thoughtful reader in Aus-
tralasia. The author insists that the burden
of Imperial defence must be recognised as a
first charge upon the revenues of all the
States, not only in war, but in peace. But
Lionel Curtis acknowledges that there can
be no compulsion upon us to become English-
men. “The whole system of the Empire is
based upon the assumption that the
Dominions are each free to develop a
nationalism of their own as distinct from
English nationalism as English nationalism
itself is distinct from that of the Americans.
....Any attempt to x’egulate the social con-
ditions of the Dominions and the United
Kingdom, as those of America are regulated
from Washington, would mean infinite mis-
chief to all these countries.” Plence the
Dominions must control their own tariffs
and their immigration from first to last. In
fact, the principle has been finally admitted
of leaving self-governing colonies to assume
whatever powers they might finally insist on
taking. “The Canadian, Australian and
South African will grow less like each other
and not more so. The spread of the British
Commonwealth over so large a share of the
vacant territories of the world has not
meant, and cannot mean, the spread of the
British nation. It has already resulted in
the production of a number of new nations
and of national types which are destined
in the future to become as clearly marked in
their difference from each other as English-
men and Americans now are.” And it is this
congeries of differentiating nations that The
Round Tabi.e wishes to bind now with an
unbreakable compact.
The object of this inquiry is to discover
the most moderate measure of change
necessary to effect the control by the
Dominions of the Ministers who handle their
foreign affairs. And Lionel Curtis points
out that there is no inherent impossibility of
England sharing that control with the
Dominions, since she has already shared her
original responsibility with Scotland and
with Ireland. Any Cabinet which controls
foreign affairs must also control the naval
and military forces that are required. And
that Cabinet must be responsible to only one
parliament. Hence there must be two
separate electorates as well as two separate
parliaments and cabinets, one for Imperial
affairs and one for the local affairs of each
of the Dominions and of the United King-
dom. The English Parliament must resign
to the Imperial Parliament all except its
local affairs. It would thus have the same
.status as the Federal Parliament com-
pared with the proposed Imperial Parlia-
ment. England would therefore be reduced
to the status of a Dominion. “The Imperial
Government must have no more to do with
exclusively British affairs than it now has
with the national affairs of the several
Dominions.” But if we desire a voice in the
control of foreign affairs we must face the
fact that we will have to provide our share
for Imperial defence, and give that Imperial
Parliament the right to put the bailiff in
until we pay up. The author draws up a
table showing how much eacli Dominiqn
would have to pay proportionately, after de-
ductions had been made for the value of its
local defence, military and naval. He
examines thoroughly the constitution of his
proposed Imperial Parliament, of which the
whole Empire would be direct electors.
But India and the other Dependencies
would for the present remain in the care ot
the local English Parliament. The author
remarks that in the Dominions, “the very
idea of one people governing another is con-
trary to their traditions.” Neither Australia
nor India would consent without vigorous
protest to interfere in each other’s govern-
ment, the one to share in the-government of
India and the other to be ruled by a Cabinet
one, at least, of which would be an Australian.
Yet, according to the author, “a British citi-'
zen in the Dominions cannot be made respon-
sible for the foreign affairs of the Common-
wealth without also becoming responsible
for the government of its subject peoples and
sharing in the long and difficult task of train-
ing those peoples to govern themselves.” vv e
must wait for the second and third parts ot
The Commonwealth of Natio7is before we get
this most important problem constructively
treated. Till then we must perforce suspend
judgment.
War
At the outbreak of the war F. A. Wile,
author of Men Around the Kaiser, was the
Berlin correspondent of London Daily
Mail, and also of the New York Times.
He was promptly arrested as a spy; and
escaped only through the services of the good-
natured United States Ambassador. With
considerable journalistic facility and some
shrewdness, in The Assault (Heinemann,
Dymock’s Library), he tells of the
development of the great tragedy as he was
privileged to see it in Berlin. He points
with amazement to “the little army of emi-
nent and expert ‘investigators’ who honored
England with their company on the very
threshold of hostilities.” In June came
Krupp, with his chief technical expert. He
was shown over practically all of England’s
greatest weapons-of-war works. Krupp
hurried back to report, not at Essen, but at
Kiel —to the Kaiser and Tirpitz. In July
Dernburg came over to investigate the
British commercial situation. Then came
Ballin, the German shipping king; and that
remarkable Englishman, Haldane, whose
“spiritual home is Germany,” arranged for
his friend Ballin to meet a few friends at
Haldane’s home. Finally Prince Henry,
Grand-Admiral and brother of the Kaiser,
arrived —a week before the declaration of
hostilities. He called on the K_flg. Louis of
Battenburg and Winston Churchill. ...and
doubtless others. The colossal cheek of it.
And the infantile naiveness of the English !
This absolutely vouched for incident ought
to convince any readers wdio have not heard
hints that the Kaiser was forced to sign
the mobilisation order that meant war. “On
August 1, 1914, the wife of the Chief of
the Great German General Staff visited
a friend of hers. She was in a state
of obvious mental excitement. ‘Ach, what
a day I’ve been through !’ she began. ‘My
•husband came home just before I left. Dog-
tired, he threw himself on to the couch, a
total wreck, explaining to me that he had
finally accomplished the three days’ hardest
work' he had ever done in his whole life —
he had helped to induce the Kaiser to sign
the mobilisation order !’ ”
“The Kaiser was constitutionally addicted
to swaggering war talk, but he preferred the
bark to the bite. He likes his job. Like
Roosevelt, he has a ‘perfectly corking time’
wielding the sceptre. He does not want to
lose his throne In war Holienzollern rule
is in perpetual peril.” Wile asserts that
the real stage-managers of the war were
not Nietzsche, Treitzschke or Bernliardi (of
the latter the German public had hardly
heard until he was made known to his own
country by the prominence given him by the
Allied press) ; but Tirpitz, the Crown
Prince, Ivoester of the German Navy
League, Iveim of the German Army League,
Reventlow, who “hated England, France
and Russia with a ferocity delightful to
behold,” Di*. Hammann, chief of the German
Press Bureau, and, finally, the 60,000 offi-
cers of the German Army. As showing the
Hun’s preparedness, Wile states that though
the Kaiser went ostentatiously away for his
annual holiday cruise on July 6, four days
before German officers in German South-
West Africa were told that they might go
home on special leave at once. These offi-
cers reached Germany early in August, to
find orders awaiting them to join their
regiments. Austria had acted on the same
early date. And on July 18 the Hun press
solemnly announced that a squadron of the
German Fleet was to be sent to an English
port in August to return the Kiel visit by
British warships! Evidently the Hun has
a sense of humor. Unfortunately the Fleet
has not kept its appointment.
Wile was in an excellent position to see
the cranking up of the German Juggernaut.
“I always knew it was a magnificently well-
greased machine, geared for the maximum
of silence; but.... it almost seemed as if
the army had orders to rush to the firing-
line in gum-shoes and that everything on
wheels had rubber tyres.” Wile’s adven-
tures are neither exciting nor important, and
his impressions of England at war, of which
the rest of the book is made up-, are merely
bright journalistic views of facts that are
now stale history.
The Wrack of the Storm, by Maurice
Maeterlinck (Methuen, per Sydney Book
Club), shows the genial author, who
hitherto had cursed no man,” uttering
“words of hatred and malediction. ’ It is
to be remembered that Maeterlinck is a
Belgian. But there is much else beside his
conviction that “the German, from one end
of his country to the other, stands revealed
as a beast of prey, which the firm will of
our planet finally repudiates,” and his in-
sistence upon pitilessness against Germany
as a measure of organic defence. It seems
a grave oversight that his suggestion for
“hostage cities” was not adopted at the
beginning of the war. That is, that the
Allies should have named certain German
cities which would be answerable, stone for
stone, for the Belgian cities in German
power. “If Brussels should be destroyed,
then Berlin should be razed to the ground.
If Antwerp were devastated, Hamburg
would disappear.” In view of the fact
that these Belgian cities are known to be
already mined, there is yet time for that
just threat.
The amazing recrudescence of universal
heroism in civilised man forms the subject of
another thoughtful essay. Maeterlinck studies
Homer’s heroes, and finds them poor things
as compared with a Poilu or an Anzac. Those
ancient heroes “have a wholesome dread or
being wounded, and an ingenuous and mani-
fest fear of death. Their mighty conflicts
are declamatory, but not so very bloody; they-
infliet more noise than pain upon their adver-
saries. .. .Their defensive weapons are
greatly superior to their arms of offence;
and death is an unusual, unforeseen and
alihost indecorous event which throws the
ranks into disorder and mhst. often puts a
stop to the combat or provokes a headlong
flight that seems quite natural. As for the
wounds, these are enumerated, and described,
sung and deplored as so many remarkable
phenomena. The most discreditable routs,
the most shameful panics are frequent; and
the old poet relates them without condemn-
ing them, as ordinary incidents.... inevitable
in any warfare.” Even in later centuries the
fiercest hand-to-hand encounters of trained
mercenaries left but few dead ; and wars were
reduced to a few pitched battles, after which
the men went home. But in modern warfare
death, invisible, is ever threatening, and the
nerves of modern man must be strained for
years to the utmost pitch of heroism. The
amazing thing that emerges from this war is
that civilised man, in laying aside his primi-
tive brutality, has not lost, indeed has marvel-
lously added to, his manly qualities. In
“The Dead Who Do Not Die” and in “The
Life of the Dead” Maeterlinck proffers com-
fort and assurance to those who have lost
their own in this war; and in another essay
he affirms his conviction that through genuine
mediums information can be obtained, by
“psychometry,” of the absent ones at the
Front. But he does not supply us with the
names and addresses of those genuine
mediums. And he is somewhat disappointed
because of this overwhelming catastrophe,
looming so tremendous and so near, none of
the psychic prophets gave us any definite
authenticated hint.
«
Recent shilling reprints include Belloc’s
Pongo and the Ball, Mrs. George Wemyss'
People of Popham, and Robert W.
Chambers’ fantasy, The King in Yellow,
than which this now horribly popular
author has never done better work. It is
a painful thought that the author of such
an original and charming work could grow
up into a best-seller—but then this page
remembers quite a nice kid who grew up
irfto a literary critic.
“Copabella”: Re the Grundyish criticism of The
Superior Race. There is something very artificial
about the conventional attitude to the discussion of
sex-matters. In Oliphant’s play—which, judging- by
the prologue, he himself regarded with a certain
amount of awed seriousness—the author merely made
his men characters say on the stage and before an
assortment of females an ordinary-enough conversa-
tion between average men. (Ordinary as regards
-subject, I mean; few would get down to the inti-
mate personal details, perhaps. It wouldn't be
cricket.) The same thing, too, is.quite an ordinary
occurrence between .man and wife, between wife and
wife, and between matron and spinster. Many a
good married-lady story l hear through the wife, and
many another I send along to Smith’s wife
Jones’s wife through mine. She knows, and Smith'
knows, and Mrs. Smith knows, and all know that all
the rest know, yet not one of the four would dream
of mentioning even a hint before the other three.
It wouldn’t be “nice.” Reporting a closed-door and
court-cleared police-court case the other day I even
heard a bunch of leggy flappers prattle of their
innocent, researches into the fascinating science —
and jolly well “up” in it they were, too. So
much for prudery. ,
The root of all salacity and most immorality is
this pretence of mystery and reticence in sex
matters. Strip it away and all desire in the so-
callcd innocent (of which there aren’t any over 10
years old) to probe into the half-known will flee. If
a process that is no more mysterious than the action
of saliva on food, or the laying of an egg by a hen,
were treated as casually as these phenomena there
wouldn’t be the crowd of youths and flappers gazing,
drip-tongued, into-picture post-card shops or playing
“so-far-and-no-father” in the Fitzroy Gardens after
the pictures are out.
As for the morals, newspaper and social immo-
rality is always a matter of race propagation and its
processes. If a man and a woman are attracted to
one another sufficiently to flout the convenances, why
shouldn’t they come together respectably while the
attraction is at its best? I’m sure better-grade
children would result to the nation than are bred
perfunctorily, and generally by miscalculation, by
two people who have long since lost through
familiarity all sense of attraction for one another.
The curse of the whole thing is that publications of
social standing, and thinking men and women, burke
the problem by a conspiracy of silence—-leaving it to
the tender mercies of Mrs. Grundy (of the Argus
staff) on the one hand and Truth on the other.

WINCHESTER
Cartridges
For Rifles, Revolvers or Pistols
The most accurate shooting rifle, revolver
or pistol made will do unsatisfactory work
unless the cartridges used in it are as accurate
as the arm. For that reason it is important
when buying to get cartridges that have an
established reputation for quality. Win-
chester —the w brand of cartridges in all
calibers from .22 to .50 are unexcelled for
accuracy, penetration and uniform shooting
qualities. They cost no more than inferior
makes and are for sale everywhere. If you
want to get the most satisfactory results with
your rifle, revolver or pistol, always use
Winchester ammunition. Do not simply
ask for “some cartridges” when you buy,
but be sure to specify Winchester make
and look for the bold on the box.

IMPORTANT.—LOOK TO YOUR TEETH.
There are no teeth like those furnished by Nature. For service, comfort and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be compared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to preserve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap and water are useful, but somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttlefish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are to.o severe on the enamel.

the breath, harden the gums and disinfect the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free 1/3. Free sample posted. ROCKE, TOMPSITT & CO., Melbourne.

be taken some mild yet penetrating antisep-
tic. The proper one to take is contained
in De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder Pills.

der, and wherever it touches it washes away
all sediment and bad secretions, and makes
the kidneys and bladder perfectly healthy.

and Bladder Pills at his chemist’s —all chem-
ists sell them—and you will, within a very

few hours, know positively that the seat of

to rheumatism, gout, gravel, backache, tired
feeling, dropsy, Bright’s disease, constipa-

der, or other symptoms of urinary trouble.
To cure rheumatism or any other form of
kidney trouble you must positively get rid of

when to take risks, banks on his
superior nerve to pull him through.
And if by diily habit and by simple
precautions he has kept his body fit
and vigorous always, you may be sure
his nerve will not fail him in any crisis.
Such men succeed where others dare
not even try to succeed.

ful of Kruschen Salts
in a tumbler of hot
water every morning
before rising clears the
head, invigorates and
cleanses the system

better —and rise refreshed ; you eat better —and
assimilate all the nourishment that food can give;
you work better —and enjoy your work. You
can tax your energies to the uttermost and take
pleasure in the effort if only you keep your body fit
while your brain works. The Kruschen habit makes you fit and
keeps you fit —good health becomes an unconscious habit of life.

VAROIUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Night.
Within the trodden circle of the camp
Women, the mothers of the unborn, girls
Small-breasted, wild of hair and lean of limb,
Men strong and sleek as beasts, full fed as
beasts,
And dogs that still were cringing, hand-
reared wolves,
Lay round the rose-red ash in sleep, or
cocked
A prickt wolf-ear, as far across the night
A loosened stone turned ’neath the roebuck’s
hoof
As with a shadowy following hind he stole
Down the long deer path to the drinking
pool—■
A still black mirror, where a mirrored star
Was broken into troubled silver lines
By the slipped stone. The roebuck stamped
and stood
A moment on the margin quivering,
Fearing the narrow, emerald eyes of Death —
The Watcher by the Ford who had wrought
fear,
Unreasoning, saving fear into the race.
The buck stamped once, drank silently, and
stole
In silence up the way that he had come.
Without the trodden circle of the camp
Ringed with great trunks. Fear sat and
walled them in ;
Grass whispered, shadows herded < and drew
close.
All things that know the dark came forth,
and eyes
From rock-clefts tempted, wandered bodiless.
The Night was evil, for the Night was Death.
N.S.W.
DULCIE DEAMER.
Wellington Lights.
The high hills of Wellington are like a
balustrade
That the winds walk over, and the tired dim
sun ;
And the weary little city is drowsing in the
shade,
And the Harbor Lights of Wellington are
waking one by one.
The harbor-tide of Wellington is laving pier
and bay,
And wrinkles are upon it, and many a flow-
ing fold;
And the visage of the waters is very drawn
and grey,
For the harbor-tide of Wellington is very,
very old.
But the young lights, the bright lights are
wonderfully gay,
Dancing from the shadows, and twinkling
here and there;
Like little eyes which watch the tardy pass-
ing of the day;
Like golden slippers flashing on a dim, dark
stair.
And the lights upon the steamers a-dream
beside the pier!
Red lights and green lights on many an idle
boat
Beam through the dim shades marvellously
clear
And blithe as the singing from a deep sea
throat!
Oh, some are ruddy rich lights, and some are
sere and dim;
But each finds an echo in the dark, still
wave,
And each wakes my heart to an old-time
hymn,
And the Harbor Lights of Wellington are
notes upon the stave.
And the night streets of Wellington are
leading from the sea,
Twining in and winding out like little
yellow ropes;
And the street lights of Wellington are very
dear to me,
And the street lights of Wellington go whirl-
ing up the slopes.
Amethysts and moonstones and flakes of
polished jade
Blended together with an old-world charm ;
The city lights of Wellington are showing
’mid the shade,
A glowing heap of jewels on a negro’s palm.
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
Green and Silver.
Rain on the leaves that stir and sigh
Murmurs a fairy lullaby,
Wherein light pinions lift and beat,
And patter tiny, joyous feet,
And elfin voices chirp and cry.
All fairyland seems sweeping nigh,
Stringing the glimmering shafts that fly,
When silver showers fragrant, fleet,
Rain on the leaves.
Clear as wee bells that chime on high
Airily ’twixt the earth and sky,
The tinkling drops ring sharply sweet
On weary branches sere with heat,
And quivering globes of crystal lie—
Rain on the leaves.
N.S.W.
SYD. C.
The Music of the Moon.
I, who, awake, have never heard
The magic nightingale on earth,
This eve, in dream, beheld the moon
Lift, like a bell, its round white girth
All garlanded with cherubim;
And, from its mouth, there came a'stream
Of such sweet singing that I sank
Faint on my pillow ; while a gleam
Of lovely splendor o’er the night
Bloomed, as a flower, in the bell,
And showed the swaying tongue within,
The old-world Grecian Philomel.
VICTORIA.
HUGH McCRAE.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 28, 1916.
WATCHING THE CRUCIFIXION.
We have grown accustomed to the statement
that this is a war of attrition, and, accept-
ing the dictum, we note with growing pride
the seeming exhaustion of Germany and of
the Prussian who is the poison in the sting
of the Teuton peoples. What we do not
note with equal care is the tremendous price
that is being paid for the advantage gained.
The Allies must win, whether Australia
shares the burden to the end or side-steps in
the hour of supreme trial; but in that vic-
tory the tinest thing in all Europe—perhaps
in all the world since the first notes of
human liberty were sounded in Athens may
die in the cause of uncomprehending man-
kind. To put the matter in all its terrible
plainness, the nations are now witnessing a
second crucifixion, and the figure upon the
cross is the figure of French Liberty.
Throughout the awful months in which
frantic and reckless Germany has been put-
ting out a mad strength that has for its
chief inspiration a growing despair of final
victory, France —the same heroic France
that sounded the trumpet-peal of modern
freedom and broke the shows and shams of
things with the lightning-stroke of truth
has been standing in the place of death,
defending the whole world’s honor, and
bleeding, bleeding, bleeding from a million
cruel wounds. This is no figure of speech,
no exaggeration of fact, but the plain, cold
bitter truth. England has suffered too.
Australia has known wounds and can boast
of battle scars. But the fact remains that,
■without France, all the rest would have been
of little avail to arrest the triumphant
march of Prussianism.
* * * *
That Germany can never overthrow
France is a truth which requires large quali-
fication. The splendid soldiers who kept
Verdun will light on while there is
one left to hold a rifle —so much admits
of no doubt. But even with Germany
crushed and trodden under foot in a
world’s contempt, the moral victory may
rest with Prussianism if the last terrible
price be paid for the shadow of a freedom
which is only the freedom of a nation’s
.grave. The spectacle of a democratic nation
in arms against the invader has been one to
quicken every pulse among the onlookers;
but if, when the final cost is worked out, it
is found that the heroic citizens of that
nation paid too big a price for all that has
been gained, Prussianism will have won. In
a word, if the manhood of younger France
is bled to exhaustion, nations may have a
shuddering pride in French achievement,
but never dare to emulate such a people’s
glory. And so the Prussian may be ham-
mered to a standstill and Kaiserism crushed
like the toad it is, while democracy proves
too ideal and impracticable, and something
bitterly like Prussianism wins a triumph
over the souls of men.
* * * *
Of course it is possible to take a large
philosophic view of things and talk about
the blood of the martyrs being a great sow-
ing for harvests to come, or wrap our
pleasant diplomatic robes about us, like the
cautious Caiaphas, and say that it is ex-
pedient for one nation to die for the peoples
that the whole world may not perish; but all
this is baulking the real hurdle and side-step-
ping the plain, inexorable truth that our own
small punch, well-directed, may save France
from the agony of a splendidly barren vic-
tory. Democracy, like humanity, is in a fine
and real sense one and indivisible. You
cannot smash free institutions in one place,
or allow them to be smashed, and hope to
see them flourish in another. England is
herself less free because she denies Ireland
her liberty. The war in North America
between North and South, had the Southern
idea prevailed, would have sapped the very
foundations of all democratic liberty. Negro
slavery and a full white man’s franchise
are unthinkable together. These are mere
axioms, denied by none except the people
who because of their habit of wearing their
brains in their stomachs are incapable of
understanding the meaning of sober, citizen
freedom; but the statement of them has a
direct and vital bearing upon the present
war crisis. Should France be crucified to
the death in the cause of human liberty and
the idea that is the keynote of Prussianism
be triumphant even after the smashing of
Prussian aims, Australia will suffer with
the rest. Man the world over will be a
little less man than he was before and a
little more a mere cog in the arbitrarily-
driven machine which Germany mistook for
God. And once the torches of democratic
liberty begin to go out we may drift any-
where in the darkness.
* * * *
The first call of the war was to save Bel-
gium. Belgium has gone under. The second
call was to save Servia. Servia, too, is trod-
den under foot. The third call and the
greatest is to save France—-heroic France,
the saviour of all men, who is now stretched
on the cross for Democracy’s world-wide sins.
Australia cannot stand by as if the matter
were of no immediate concern. The blood
of France is being shed drop by drop, and,
in no figure of rhetoric, but in cold, bitter
fact, it is being shed for us. Our answer to
the Referendum question should show what
we think about these matters. But the
duty of the private citizen is not the be-all
and end-all of things. In the national seri-
ousness which the great rescue demands all
petty considerations should be put on one
side. The leaders of the party in power in
the Commonwealth, though some of them
may be in earnest about conscription, have
utterly failed to satisfy the Australian public
with their handling of military matters. Let
them, if they desire to play their part
honestly, associate with themselves the
strongest and most capable men on this con-
tinent for the sane and sympathetic manage-
ment of our great, final national effort. It
is no fusion of the old sort that is wanted,
no rump of broken parties masquerading as
a new party. Let the very word party stand
for treason while this thing is being done,
and let our politicians show that they can
live and act in a season of dead earnestness
without the atmosphere of petty jealousy
and deception and intrigue in which they
have hitherto wrapped themselves. If there
is a call to sacrifice ringing in the ears of
Australian citizens at large, there should be
at least a call to ordinary decency tingling
in the consciences of those who are called
political leaders.
[Written to express the views of The Bulletin,
within the limitations prescribed under the War
Precautions Act, by S. H. Prior, D. M. Wright and
P. J. Kirby, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
LABOR AND THE CLEAN-UP.
In spite of all the prevailing doubts about
everything except the sunset, there is one
sure thing: when the war does end there is
going to be a tremendous clean-up. In a
rough, disordered way the job of tackling the
untidiness has, in Australia, commenced ; for
though the uproar and confusion that are
raging in the vicinity of Hughes turn for
the moment on conscription, these pictur-
esque happenings have a close political re-
lationship to the strategical retirement of A.
Fisher, the political funeral of Scaddan, the
twistings of Holman and the comedy-in-the-
making at Brisbane. The truth is that the
Labor organisation was shaky before the war
started; that under the stress of war it has
got shakier; and that a re-alignment of
parties and policies is inevitable. That the
result will be for the benefit of Australia’s
political health scarcely admits of doubt. In
particular it is well that Labor should find
out what it is and where it is. And it is
good that Australia should again get the
lesson it is now being taught—that Party
Government is an evil and vicious thing,
destructive to the nation and demoralising
even to parties themselves.
The Bulletin does not even suspect that
the clean-up can be handled by any party
such as the country now knows. It is quite
persuaded that the mess made in the past by
Party Government when handling simple,
primary things will be a worse mess when
the animal commences to handle more diffi-
cult and complex problems. That the Labor
party as it stands to-day is as little to be
depended upon as a similar mob of Liberals
is as obvious as O’Malley’s hat. This is
the only possible conclusion for the outsider
to gather from the condition of internal
strife and public helplessness revealed by the
party with increasing emphasis from year to
year. On its own confessions it is divided
in principle, intention and ambition. Many
of its political representatives are the scoff
and jest of themselves, the objects of ridicule
and distrust by their followers. And it. is all
this because the Party disease has riddled
members through and through, and Expedi-
ency has made them its victims. When Aus-
tralia had war forced upon her, almost the
first act of the National Parliament was to
bolt into the bush and shirk every responsi-
bility that a Parliament owes to the people.
That evasion was a degrading confession of
failure. It is one that a Labor party deserv-
ing the name would have repudiated any con-
nection with. Instead of that it instigated
this almost unparalleled disregard of duty.
Looking at it now, how many.citizens of Aus-
tralia are there ready to believe that Labor
as a political party can clean-up for Aus-
tralia before it has had a thorough wash itself
—before it has found out what it wants to
do, how it is going to set to work, and where
it is trying to get to?
Cleansed and in its right mind, free from
the platform-breakers and miscellaneous
deadbeats dossing about the premises, there
would be an important, useful part for
Labor to play in the great job to come.
One of its most immediate tasks is to treat
the nation’s affairs as national affairs
instead of as the sport of warring place-
hunters, and to turn the national . cash-
box into something better than an instru-
ment of public demoralisation. But Labor
can never do that any more than the Lib-
erals can if it goes on obsessed by the notion
that machine-made victories at the polls
represent final victory. These are more
often but the first stages of that attrition
which ends in death. A continuation of the
Labor movement in partnership with Min-
istries which do nothing but squander the
public estate, blow off hot air and strangle
Labor principles would be a calamity to
Australia hardly less stupendous than war.
A Labor movement reduced to nothing but
the struggle to make the Legislature a
place for the worship of the Party joss—
robbed of constructive ability through hav-
ing forgotten what it was going to build
can do nothing for Australia except help to
tie it up in a knot. Labor knows this as
well as The Bulletin. It knows that it
has made a false start, that . most of its
gods have feet of clay. And it is because
it knows this that it is turning itself inside
out and upside down. This paper hopes
that all this will do Labor a lot of good, and
that Australia when next asked to support
Labor policies will be? able to understand
what those policies are instead of hearing
nothing but conflicting yells and seeing
Labor practice and Labor profession con-
tinuously in the Divorce Court. Does Labor
want to look forward to joining in the clean-
up with certainty of doing further harm to
itself, surrendering its critical usefulness
and steering all over the shop? The indica-
tions are that it does not; but the sooner
it settles the matter the better for the Com-
monwealth and for Labor. The clean-up is
going to be a big contract. And when it
starts there will not be any spare time to
sit down for a smoke-oh or watch a party
organisation twisting its leaders’ necks.
ONE BRAIN ONE VOTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The recent rush of people who didn’t know
an enrolment form from a tea-wrapper to
see if their names were on the Federal roll
doesn’t indicate interest in the Referendum
so much as the dread of a heavy fine for not
trying to look like a citizen. What humbug
the whole thing is, and what a farce is made of
our democracy by these obligations forced upon
people with nothing but hair under their
hats! The question whether the imbecile
should have the franchise or whether it
should be confined to those suspected of being
sane, recently raised in the N. S. Wales
Assembly, would sound like a jest in any
place where democratic freedom was under-
stood ; but in Australia it is a cold factor
actually influencing the balance of parties,
and to be taken account of by the various
machines which keep the country’s intelli-
gence from finding expression. There is
nothing Party hates like intelligence, and
jealous eyes are cast even on the occupants of
the padded cell when polling-day comes
round. To rope in a mob of people who
have votes and get them to put an abso-
lutely meaningless mark on the ballot-paper
is the one thing desirable in our “informed”
and “advanced” politics. As- a result we
get politicians and statesmen of the kind we
do —a kind entirely in keeping with the high
level of political thought behind the ballot.
And when Australia appears by electoral
representation before the world as a thing
blatant, empty and oozing shop-worn plati-
tudes, men wonder and talk about the failure
of democracy. As a plain fact, among the
many strongest forces working against
democracy and subverting it is the legisla-
tion which forces the indifferent and non-
intelligent to be citizens against their will.
Greece tried it ages ago in Athens with a
painted rope and failed; Rome. scorned
any notion of the kind and, in spite of an
over-complicated civic machinery, achieved a
lot. Australia muddles back upon the trail
of burst political devices and flounders to
despair among the mess of wreckage.
* * * *
The whole idea of democracy is that the
intelligence of the community shall rule. To
attempt to force citizenship upon those who
don’t care whether they have votes or not and
don’t know what those votes mean in any
case is clearly to defeat the democratic idea.
Any number of people who now vote at elec-
tions ought in the interests of public safe y
to be disfranchised at once. Their votes
merely swamp the national intelligence ana
turn the representative system into a hiss-
ing and -a swear-word. To make elec-
toral enrolment compulsory is absurd ; to
make the right to vote too easy to obtain
is foolish; to ensure that only those who
know the meaning of their political act
shall have any say in the management ot
the country’s affairs is sane, wise and some
guarantee of progress. The old system
which gave the rule to those with property
qualifications was no worse than the present
one which gives the main say to those witii
no qualifications at all. It may not be
possible ot practicable to fix a high standard
of qualification for citizenship, but some
standard there should be and that standarc
should be based on intelligence alone, lliata
great number of the citizens of the Common-
wealth are quite without political intelligence
is a fact which nobody could have the haicli
hood to dispute; and to eliminate the names
of all these from the electoral rolls is abso-
lutely necessary in the interests of decent
government and reform. This, of course, is
no class matter. Keen political intelligence
is found at wharf and mine and washtub,
while some of the least intelligent of all
voters reside in handsome suburban homes
and fence their inanity with diamonds, lhe
weeding-out process should apply all along
the line and be employed without mercy for
caste or creed, but with some decent sym-
pathy and a real desire to place what was
best in the community on top.
* * * *
To achieve such a reform need be a matter
of no overwhelming difficulty. A small exami-
nation of an oral kind conducted by a
Board answerable to. Parliament, but not
to any Government or party, is all that is
required. The test questions would merely
aim at discovering that the candidates had
a reasonable knowledge of the possible effects
of their votes. It is safe to say that thou-
sands of men and women would fail in that
test, including men of vast waistcoat, women
who live at Darling Point and Toorak, some
members of the Legislature and a host ot
the right-thinking of all political parties;
but the country would be the better for it
and the real democracy would come to its
own. Party organisations which now talk
about educating the people in politics, and
mean thereby roping them in to vote for the
right-colored candidate, would have to get
at the job of education in earnest. . The
voter’s right would be a certificate of citizen-
ship ; and as time went on the examination
could be advanced until the holding of the
thing became a matter of pride and a guaran-
tee of achievement. Then the politician
would alter the form of his rhetoric to meet
the altered shape of the thinking around
him, and a vast heap of things that lumber
Australia’s path could be taken straight away
to the destructor. Nor would there be the
slightest chance that the disfranchised might
rise in revolt against such a system. All the
men and women who desired citizenship
would eagerly strive to get it, and the rest
would be impotent as angry pumpkins or
maddened horse-radish.
* * * *
The old theory of the early democrats that
the best way to make a man was to think
him one, and treat him as one, has had a long
trial in Australia and hasn’t made good.
The alleged citizen who isn’t a citizen and
can’t be made one is as smugly complacent
as a wowser who assures you that his soul
is soundly sayed ; and there is no possibility
of progress while he thinks himself all that
he isn’t. In Australia to-day there are
people still living in the Stone Age, others
who have reached the Bronze Age, and a
few that have almost tracked back to the
Golden Age that was before either. To give
the squalid savage a vote —whether that
savage stays at the Hotel Australia or lumps
on the wharf —is plain insanity. It means
that the whole thought of the country is
delayed while the laggards in the intellec-
tual race are catching up ; and, as they don’t
even try to catch up, the waiting is likely
to be as long as the contemplative pauses
of a suburban train. The Stone Age man
is supposed to have taken about 50,000 years
to reach the Age of Bronze, and, though you
may hurry him up by catching him young
and sending him to a State school, he is still
a slow mover and a dead load for the com-
munity to carry. Why are we not honest
about these things? The brain of the coun-
try is neither in its feet nor in its stomach,
and by persistently keeping up the pretence
that a clod is a potential sage we do tre-
mendous injury to the clod himself and sub-
vert the whole force and meaning of
democracy. Most of Australia is intelli-
gent ; why must the hand of heavyweight
stupidity be forever at her throat?
RIMU.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“Neutrals” and the Referendum.
Two years ago, when certain Hun oracles in
U.S.A. and elsewhere were stating
would happen to Europe after the Kaiser
had conquered it, they invariably promised
that Sweden should benefit by the redistribu-
tion of territory. Sweden was to have
Finland. A profoundly unenlightened public
naturally wondered what Sweden was doing
to deserve this future reward ; but the cen-
sorship suppressed all pungent comment on
the subject. The publication of the Allies’
recent Note to Sweden lifts this veil of
official mystery. It came from France, and
is refreshingly candid. After detailing the
Allies’ causes for complaint the Note
declares: —•
Sweden’s attitude towards one group of belli-
gerents is incompatible with fair and impartial
neutrality.
To which the Swedish Government sent a
saucy Germanised reply. Looking back two
years we can now see that the British
Empire at large owed to the censorship its
ignorance of the full strength of the German
menace. Germany and Austria were gener-


sn-noi!i to be a pair of overbearing
asbbucklers attacking humanity with the
.Vmpathies of all the other Powers arrayed
..f ainS i Even Turkey was represented
tu. f n h s )norable neutral, and all of Greece
lat mattered was with us. In short, the cen-
sorship kept the British Empire in a fool’s
paradise as regards its military require-
ments and the dangers which threatened it.
To-day we know what Turkey did and how
the case stands between Greece and Ger-
many and Sweden and Germany. And we
know, too., that Norway’s leading journal
has frankly announced that all Scandinavia
will meet Britain in “joint fellowship.”
So. the Allies still stand menaced by
nations that would too obviously be pre-
pared to throw in their lot with Germany
it they could be given a little more
encouragement to believe that the German
will win. They could not possibly get bet-
ter encouragement than an assurance that
our people are sick of the war, and are not
prepared to guarantee reinforcements. This
referendum of ours will be the first direct
appeal made to people of the British Empire
on the subject. The answer will actually be
the answer only of Australia; but unques-
tionably it will be accepted as revealing the
minds of British people everywhere. What
use Germany will make of it if Australia
says “No” is too painful to think about.

A Senator’s Premature Incitement to
Australia to Maffick.
According to Senator Findley, the German
is “already beaten,” and the right thing for
Australia to do is to knock off beating him
some more, leave the job to the conscript
armies of various countries, and with
£75,000,000 thus “saved” proceed to “build
strategical railways, construct thousands of
aeroplanes, provide up-to-date arsenals, and
honeycomb Australia with stations for the
fleet.” Findley is entitled to his opinion
about Germany being “beaten” in any legiti-
mate sense of the word ; but it is one that
fills this paper with a great yearning to
know where the evidence is. Taken in the
broadest sense it is clear enough that German
aims have been thwarted; but to use the
word “beaten” in the way Findley does is
wildly absurd. Between stopping the Ger-
man from conquering any more territories
and inducing him to remove from the terri-
tories he has already conquered and now
occupies there is a vast difference. And
between that and pushing him back on to his
own dunghill and jumning on top of him
there , there is a vaster difference still. Even
when he has been pushed across the Rhine
he may not be beaten. The German is in
France; and the French are not beaten.
He is in Servia; and the Servians are not
whipoed yet by a long way. The Allies are
not in Germany or anywhere near it. They
are only on the fringe of Austria. It is both
foolish and dangerous, Jffien, to talk of the
German armies being licked or the German
spirit broken. The Russian does not think
that either has happened, obviously. Nor
does the Frenchman or the Britisher or the
Australian who is battling with him. They
may think they are going to beat the enemy;
but Findley can be dead sure they would
stop thinking that way if they heard their
Governments remarking that it was all over
and that the time was ripe to “save” money
by getting rid of it on something besides re-
inforcements. Germany will be beaten, but
only on two conditions—that the Allies stick
together and that their armies are not per-
mitted to dwindle away. And even with
these conditions fulfilled the contract is a
mighty one.

within the limitations prescribed under the War
Precautions Act, by S. H. Puior. D. M. Wright and
F. J. Kirby, 214 George-street, Sydney.]

Pozieres.
Quite plainly the same old stupid business
is still going in—everything we do is a
success, and everything the other side does
is a failure. The truth—the truth that
we ought to know ; the truth that we must
know, unless our referendums and elections
are to be worse than a farce —this is still
suppressed, or glozed over. Pozieres was,
according to the cables, a glorious victory.
The anti-conscriptionists said that we had
the enemy beaten —he was on the run; what
need was there for any such new and untried
effort as conscription? Letters by the mail
tell quite another story. Even the usually-
colorless official “Orders” put a greyer com-
plexion upon the affair. The Commander-
in-Chief (General llaig) sent this message
through General Plumer, and it was in a
Special Divisional Order of the Divi-
sion on July 22 :
Please convey to the troops engaged my appre-
ciation of their gallant effort and of the careful and
thorough preparations made for it. I wish them to
realise that their enterprise has not been by any
means in rain, and that the gallantry with which
they carried out the attack is fully recognised.
General Plumer added his “Hooray !” to this
condolence; and General Birdwood “con-
gratulated the Division, not only on their
gallantry, but on .the fact that they are the
first Australian Division to receive such
recognition in France.” This Divi-
sion was hammered together in Egypt out
of all sorts of odds and ends. Though
most of the officers were drafted from other
Divisions, the is nevertheless the infant
of the Australian army in the field. In the
midst of the gloom it is at least good to
know that Australia did not exhaust its
gallantry when it sent off the earlier lots
that proved themselves at Gallipoli.

Wills and Widows, with a P.S. on the
Tote.
When Frank O’Neill, bookmaker, died a
few months ago, it was found that he had left
behind him £20,000 and a widow. Under
his will there was cash for various persons,
but not a bean for the woman. He left her
penniless. She challenged the will on the
ground of testamentary incapacity. Now,
testamentary incapacity is always a difficult
thing to prove; and she could not prove it.
All that she could prove was that.the man’s
personal habits and conversation were like
concentrated samples from 15 manure fac-
tories. It made Justice Street talk like
this:—
The circumstances of the case afford a potent
weapon in the hands of those who believed that
legislative restrictions should be imposed upon the '
absolute freedom which the law hitherto permitted to
an individual to disregard entirely all moral obliga-
tions in disposing of his property by will so long as
he possessed what was called a sound and disposing
mind. Unpleasant as they were, the facts of the
testator’s career were also instructive from other
Points. of view. Of humble origin, of little or no
education, familiar probably with only one book, and
that his betting book, as coarse in his language as
in his nature, devoid apparently of most if not all
of the principles and moral restraints by which self-
respecting and clean-living people were influenced,
openly and unashamedly unfaithful to his wife and
to his matrimonial obligations, he nevertheless
managed, in pursuit of his chosen calling, to accumu-
late a fortune of upwards of £20,000 before drink-
ing himself to death at the early age of 42 or 43.
,o piovision in his will of any kind was made for
iw « e ' ™ ere was nothing Whatever to suggest
that Mrs O’Neill was not a good wife to her hus-
band until compelled to leave him, and nothing
which had been disclosed afforded any moral justi-
fication for his action in not merely leaving her
nothing by his will, but in taking away from her
in'his'lifetime. W . hlCh had " lade for her
And then, having shown that the testator
was a pervert, his Honor could find no justi-
ficatmn in law for disturbing the will. The
TimV il . a<l , beßn a ‘‘“ark” and a “spoil-sport.”
That is to say, she had attempted to come
«nd W twi hei ’ l ins, ? ;nKI . and his bestialities;
and the law stands aside and allows him to
punish her for it. Happily such a case can
hardly occur again in N. S. Wales. For
very tardily the Legislature has just passed
a measure putting upon such testators the
restrictions to which the Judge referred.
Indeed, recognising its own neglect, it made
the measure retrospectiveso O’Neill may
be beaten on the post. Apart, how-
ever, from the rights of the wife, the case
has, as the Court said, instructive features.
In particular, that such a man should be
able to accumulate £20.000 in the way he
did is a dreadful reflection on the intelli-
gence of society; and what sort of a business
the bookmaker’s is can best be judged by the
fact that apparently no man can be too foul
to succeed at it. On the contrary, it looks
as though foulness is rather a qualification
than otherwise. And it is in defence of
such people and such a “profession” that
the wowser takes his stand against the clean,
honest tote!
Cutting the Ground from Under Our
Feet.
There is about to be established on the
foreshores of Port Ivembla (N.S.W.), at an
initial cost of about £lOO,OOO, a plant for
the manufacture of copper goods. The neces-
sary equipment has been ordered and pre-
liminary work is in hand. The raw material
will be obtained from the Electrolytic
Refining and Smelting Co. near by, and an
abundance of fuel is available in the coal
seams a couple of miles , away. Prior to the
war the copper production of the Common-
wealth was at the rate of about 44,000 tons
per annum. This production, however,
never greatly benefited Australia. Foreigners
swooped down and reaped whatever harvest
was to be made from the conversion of this
raw material into implements of utility—
and destruction. German buyers in parti-
cular were always prepared to take as much
copper as they could get in any shape or
form—ore, “blister” or refined. For many
years they were buying “blister” from Mt.
Morgan and paying for it as such, and all
the time were leaching from it valuable
quantities of gold and silver by electrolysis.
Eventually the secret leaked out and led to
the erection of the big refining w T orks at
Port Kembla. Even after the establishment
of these works the Germans could offer
higher prices for the refined copper than
Great Britain or any other nation. The
fact that they were prepared to pay these
high prices, in addition to freight, and then
return us the finished article at a reason-
able cost, was not attributable so much to
cheap labor as to efficient machinery, the
application of scientific methods, national
bonuses, tariffs and subsidies, and plenty of
energy. With these things we can have the
same industries. Without them we shall
remain hewers of wood and drawers of
water; and because our supplies are by no
means inexhaustible we cannot be hewers of
wood and drawers of water for ever. So far
“the solution of the metal problem” has, at
the best, consisted of arrangements for smelt-
ing and refining our ores either in Aus-
tralia or in Britain. That is only the
beginning of wisdom. We need to use our
metals —not merely provide other people
with the raw materials for their industries.
Better in the long run for Australia not to
“solve” our metal problem at present if it
means merely robbing the country of the
material which will be indispensable for
great manufacturing industries by and bye.

True to its policy of doing the things it
wildly condemned in other people, the Hol-
man Government has plunged up to its neck
in the repurchase of land for settlement, and
proposes to employ the debenture as the
medium of exchange. The lOU is to be
payable in 10 years, and will be a cheerful
item in the Kid’s inheritance. Of course, the
transaction represents borrowing just as
definitely as if a loan was floated and cash
instead of scraps of paper was handed over
to the landowners. The latest list of estates
to. be “resumed” numbers seven and com-
prises 111,178 acres. Out of this area it is
estimated that 221 farms can be carved. The
price per acre has not been mentioned. Per-
haps it doesn’t matter. But whether the
price be £1 or £5 The Bulletin protests
against these transactions and against the
whole system of repurchase. The conversion
of existing boomed land values into public
debt is a scandal. It means robbery of the
State and of the people. State land pur-
chase has been a gross failure in the past,
not only in N. S. Wales but in Bananaland,
Victoria and Maoriland. It settles nothing.
It is merely an expensive way of perpetuat-
ing abuses, making settlement more difficult
and creating further embarrassments. To
imagine that the settlement of Australian
lands and the building up of a rural popula-
tion can proceed on such a basis as this is
to conceive the country shouldering a burden
of debt which would make the war-game look
as cheap by comparison as 2d.

assaulted by seVen Governments at this
rate; but Heaven is not open to be inter-

The very fact that the censorship has now
been relaxed for nearly a fortnight with no
evil results is proof that its exercise outside
the military sphere was wanton and treason-
able to the Australian democracy; and
it is to Pearce, the very small man
behind a very big gun, that the public
finger points as the one who, without
either authority or reason, denied to Aus-
tralia the right of free speech. For him to
say that if certain Germans, now behind
barbed wire, had been at large the publica-
tion of the matters referred to by Ferkicks
and others in the Senate might have been
dangerous, is simply to draw a herring
across the trail. Most of the matters which
were censored had no connection with the
war, but some of them had an intimate and
close connection with Pearce and his dis-
qualifications, and his muddling, and the
muddling of his officers, and the pulling of
social and political strings. These are the
things that this man and his officers cen-
sored ; and the time will come when they will
have to answer to Australia for it.
“Blackbutt” : The timber trade of Mel-
bourne and suburbs is neatly apportioned
between a limited number of wholesale and
retail traders. The retailers are supplied
with Australian hardwood by the sawmillers
and with imported softwood by the timber-
brokers. There is a retailers’ association,
with a wholesalers’ (mostly softwoods) and
a sawmillers’ (hardwoods) association as
well. Believing that there are already
enough timber-merchants at the game, the
retailers (1) frown on any broker or saw-
miller who supplies any merchant outside
the association; and (2) give the glassy eye
to the citizen who tries to start a new
timber-yard and applies in the ordinary
course of business for admittance to the
association. The result is that there is
nowadays no known instance in which an
outside-the-ass. timber-merchant lis being
supplied by a broker with softwood at whole-
sale rates; and the only way such a mer-
chant can supply his customers is by buying
his softwood retail. As for the sawmillers,
their number in Victoria is less than it
used to be, for various reasons; but in spite
of the retailers’ decree at least one Mel-
bourne yardsman, not in the association,
has been able to get supplies of hardwood
and is doing a thriving trade at cut rates.
These times, deeply hurt in its tenderest
feelings, the ass. is holding almost hourly
meetings to devise a means of causing this
hardwoodsman’s business to drop dead for
lack of supplies; and it is hoped that every
sawmiller in the State will come to an hon-
orable understanding with the ass. and agree
not to deliver a foot of the hardwood except
to the hon. understanders —under pain of
boycotting by the combined retailers. Still,
perish the thought that the price of Aus-
tralian hardwood, which has trebled during
the past few years, is at its present high
level owing to any but “natural” causes !
At last Stinking Fish is getting it in the
neck. Besides the reputation the Anzacs
have gained as soldiers of much higher
intelligence than the English variety, Eng-
land is struck by the great superiority of
the material that their uniforms are made
of,. and by the fit and general get-up of
their clothes. British critics point out that
the material in an Australian private’s uni-
form is better than in that of an English
officer, while, in addition to Billjim’s
superior physique, he is sprucer—a fact that
is a good deal resented by the envious Mr.
Thomas Atkins, who sneers about boot
polishing and such-like performances. It
is of course rough on Thomas Atkins,
who is exactly what he has been made by a
country whose idol has been cheapness, even
when cheapness meant a Chinese in place of
a Britisher. But it is even rougher on the
anti-Australian press and politicians who
have so conscientiously dwelt on the theory
that we should never attempt to do anything
that England hadn’t done first, and even
then could never hope to do it half as well.

David Low’s caricature of Marshall
Lyle, “still after 20 years known as the
lawyer who defended Deeming,” starts
Lyle talking about the case:—
I appeared at the coroner’s inquest, but withdrew
from the defence (so-called) when the case began
in the Supreme Court. The Judge had refused me an
adjournment of the trial. I wanted an adjournment
because papers necessary to the defence were
coming oversea. These papers arrived only after the
execution. I withdrew as solicitor, but the two
barristers who appeared as counsel would not sup-
port me by withdrawing with me, although I with-
drew my instructions from them. The .Judge, being
■put in a fix through my withdrawal, asked the two
barristers to appear for Deeming without instruc-
tions. They did so— Alfred Deaiiin and W. P.
Forlonge. As they were without instructions their
appearance as defenders was somewhat of a farce. I
had no sympathy for them, as their spineless refusal
to support me for the adjournment was in roy
opinion inexcusable, because in common fairness the
prisoner was entitled to have his defence fully pre-
pared. The Judge had no excuse to be in~a hurry,
and Deakin was too courteous altogether. Life's
battles are not won by courtesy to anybody, not even
to Judges. Judges are very human. Under these
circumstances no defence was possible. I don!t call
a few questions from counsel and a little rhetoric
from Deakin and no evidence a defence in a capital
case. I don’t think Deakin’s affability had any
good effect. I am sure it only assisted injustice.
My application was genuine and honest and perfectly
truthful, and, not being granted, I took the only
course by retiring from the case. It was Deakin
and Forlonge who “defended” Deeming.
Although, then, I did not defend Deeming at his
trial I tried get, for the prisoner a fair trial. I
was satisfied as to his guilt assuming he was sane,
but from the information given to me I became sus-
picious that all was not right mentally. My sus-
picions increased when I received cables from the
lunatic asylum he had escaped from seven years
before, the reports of his treatment as a child at a
home ior mentally-afflicted children, and numer-
ous police reports confirming his previous insanity
from England. 1 was also made aware of a conspiracy
amongst some of the police in Melbourne to suppress
evidence of the prisoner’s epilepsy, and this police
conspiracy was entirely successful, being supported by
perjury in court. There was wilful and corrupt perjury
committed in Dbeming’s case. Deakin and Forlonge
Knew nothing about this, as they had no instructions.
The case as outlined by the Crown was wonderfully
wrong. Even the date of the murder at Windsor
The Singing Army.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It’s the spell that spurs the feet when
marching palls,
It’s the charm that covers league on muddy
league,
It’s the trimming that we wear with over-
alls,
When we’re slopping with the bucket at
fatigue;
It’s the prayer that makes the soldier’s
spirit strong,
And the amulet he wears upon his breast —-
Just to scoff at dull endeavor with a song,
For in song the soldier’s soul is manifest.
It was “Desert Sands” we sang when we
were green
(Oh, Mena sands were anything but cold!)
When the Bedouin and craftier Cairene
Were wise with wiles of ages manifold ;
We sang and swore and sinned with such
as these,
Till we countered other ways and other men
On one small neck of land between the seas
(We taught the world the songs we chanted
then !).
The words are mostly shaped by limping
chance,
And the jolting tune may rattle like a ’bus,
But it trumpets scorn of Time and Circum-
stance,
As we tramp the greasy road with Orpheus;
It links the breaking squadron under fire,
If pulls the cursing column from the rut,
It voices scorn of Death and vain desire,
To the Colonel or the regimental slut.
And it’s “Take Me Back to Dixie” here in
France.
(Oh, there never was a Dixie like the
South !)
And the music is the ghost of old Romance,
And the memory of kisses on the mouth ;
It is loss and gain and torment, it is truth,
It is wine unto the flesh and spirit spent,
The seer whose counsel stands forever sooth,
And the whip whose thong can drive
environment.
We’ve mocked with tune the tepid Turkish
night,
Rouen has echoed back “Australia Fair,”
We’ve yelled the songs of Brisbane and the
Bight
Along the pitted streets of Pozibres;
Their voice is wrath and mirth and cleanly
hate,
Forgetfulness and keen remembrance both,
They are bombs we lift and fling at froward
Fate
(And we fuse them with a hot Australian
oath!).
And it’s “Boys of Anzac” when we leap the
bags
(Oh, that’s their barrage-fire that drops
behind !),
Athwart the gaps their flattened wire sags,
The shouting charge spills forward, battle-
blind !
And when to blackened vines and ragged
walls,
We win through splintered steel and shrink-
ing flesh,
Ihe swaddies drop to breathe, the trumpet
calls,
And we sing them up to dig the line afresh.
They are tunes that know no glib harmonic
rule,
And their ancestry concerns us not at all,
For we’ve conned them in a kindergarten-
school,
And we’ve hummed them in a Sydney music-
hall,
They’ve the loneliness that draws the soul
apart,
And the shame that bids the husky laughter
rise
With a jest for something tightened round
the heart,
And a blasphemy for tears within the eyes.
And it’s “Break the News to Mother” when
we drop
(Oh, gently in the jumble where you
tread !),
When earth and Heaven roar and reel and
stop.
And the dirty khaki blots with cleaner red ;
Though the bugles blare their grieving to
the sky,
And the fulsome ink of sorrow choke the
pen,
The song we taught you shall you know us
by,
Who were singers of the Melody of Men !
N.S.W.
J. ALEX, ALLAN.
