was wrong by a week. Emily Mather was killed a
week before the time alleged by the Crown. It was
perfectly clear that her husband killed her. But the
real facts of the case made it a murder without pre-
meditation.
I say now, after 20 years and more, that the
Deeming trial remains, and will for ever remain, as
a scandal in Australian judicial history. The
prisoner was not allowed a fair trial. Journalistic
mendacity assumed enormous proportions and in-
flamed the public so much that it was quite impossi-
ble to get a fair trial. I read lies in the papers
every day and many journalistic forgeries. The
verdict of the jury to the effect that the prisoner
was sane was (with my information) manifestly
false. Yet they did not care how false their verdict
was. The Deeming case followed very venerable
British legal precedents. It was conducted exactly
like the witchcraft trials in the days of the Stuart
Kings of England, and with as little justice, as little
reason and as little sense of fairness to the prisoner.
There was no desire to ascertain the real truth.
There was just the mob’s desire to kill the witch or
epileptic. And the mob was the Melbourne public.
It was more powerful than justice. Was it not
better that the epileptic should be killed, guilty or
not guilty, they said. No, I do not think might is
right. In that respect I differed from the people
of Melbourne in the Deeming case. I have had to
suffer for this. The Victorian State Government,
although I was assigned as solicitor to defend the
prisoner, did me out of my expenses in acting for
it, and left me stranded, knowing that I could not
force them to pay me, as the juries were so in-
flamed. Ministers laughed as they told me to bring
an action to recover my pocket expenses. I was for
weeks the recipient of hundreds of threatening letters,
which filled a sack, and I was boycotted in every
quarter in Melbourne. I found a revolver good com-
pany. Yet I have lived to find an eminent English
Judge endorse my stand for justice to epileptics, as
European scientists have done for years.
Freedom of Speech.
This appeal for freedom of speech, signed
by Holman, Hall, Griffith, Ashford,
Black and Hoyle, State Ministers; by
W. G. Spence, president of the A.W.U.;
by J. C. Watson, ex-leader of the Common-
wealth party; and by J. D. Fitzgerald,
R. D. Meagher and H. Lamond, ex-presi-
dents of the P.L.L., is addressed to members
of leagues and trades unions in N. S. Wales:
The action of the executive of the N. S. Wales
P.L.L. in expelling the Prime Minister from the
movement, and placing the Premier, other members
of the Ministry, Labor members of Parliament, and
certain members of the party under a ban, strikes a
vital blow at the basic principle of the movement.
The question of compulsory military service outside
Australia has been submitted for decision to the
people of Australia by means of the referendum.
Upon this subject there is such a division of opinion
within the Labor movement that only by the exer-
cise of the utmost forbearance can disastrous dis-
ruption be averted. If in a spirit of intolerant sup-
pression of freedom the movement is now split, we
shall place the industrial and political enemies of
Labor in control, although at the end of the war the
workers will be more than ever in need of sympa-
thetic treatment.
The first essential of the preservation of the Labor
movement is that during the Referendum campaign
the question at issue should be openly, fully and
fearlessly discussed.
Many of those who are urging compulsory service
to-day do so on the ground that if compulsory ser-
vice is not adopted in order to bring about speedy
victory, conscription in a still more hateful form will
be forced upon us by the events of the next few
years. This view may be right or wrong, but,
whether right or wrong, the workers of the State are
entitled to have it fully and clearly explained to
them by all who hold it.
We claim that Labor men who share this view
must be set free to put it fully and clearly before
their brother workers without being penalised within
the movement to which they are attached. If their
view is wrong, it can be defeated by the force of
argument and can be voted down at the ballot box.
If it is right, untold mischief will he done to the
future interests of the workers unless it is thoroughly
discussed and considered.
Recognising that Germany is yet undefeated, and
believing that the enemy has immense resources still
at her command, we hold personally to the view:—
(1) That if the Allies fail to obtain complete vic-
tory the declaration of peace will be followed by
prolonged preparation for another war. During this
period conscription and all its attendant evils will be
unavoidable. .
(2) That even if the Allies are victorious, but the
British Empire as a whole has not done its share, the
needs and claims of Australia will not be safeguarded
in the settlement which will then be made.
(3) That it is, therefore, essential that the great
effort made by Australia should be maintained at full
strength.
Whether you endorse this view or not, we claim
the right of Labor men to put our opinions before
other Labor men in the interests of Labor. We claim
our right to remain in the movement to which we
have devoted so much public effort, and in which we
have obtained positions of responsibility. Our offence
in the eyes of the P.L.L. executive is our claim for
freedom of speech, and we protest to trades unionists
and Labor men and women generally against this
outrage upon the basic principle of the Labor move-
ment.
The Bulletin associates itself entirely with
that appeal. The present assault upon free
speech is an assault upon the principle of
the referendum. A referendum is an appeal
from parties to the people; and theorganisa-
tion which attempts to destroy this quality
in a referendum is an enemy of the
democracy. This is not the doing of the
rank and*file of the unionists of N. S. Wales.
It is the doing of a handful of men, the
bosses of the movement. That is the point
the rank and file —the great, mostly-silent
body of unionists —have to remember. It
is an attack, not upon Hughes, not upon
lloeman, not upon Spence, but upon them-
selves. They are the' people whose right to
govern themselves is now being undermined.
No democrat should therefore be in any
doubt as to the side he should take in connec-
tion with this lamentable attempt to exercise
tyranny and destroy the foundations of the
referendum.
♦
The Everlasting Battle and the Sugges-
tion that We Should Knock Off.
Most of the great battles that decided big
things in Europe lasted only a few hours.
The usual thing was to commence in the
morning and start looking for the commis-
sariat in the evening. Sometimes the fray
was over before lunch, and on these occa-
sions the cinematograph division was able
before nightfall to send home complete films
ot the combat. But in these latter days
things are different. Viewed broadly, the
current unpleasantness has been a single
battle in which various engagements stand
out as details. Fighting commenced before
Verdun eight months ago and is still in pro-
gress. The Russians got their second wind
in Galicia when they were four months
younger and have been sticking to it ever
since. The proceedings on the Somme are
three months old and there is every indica-
tion that they are going to survive the
dangerous period of infancy. In fact, one
gigantic battle has been raging since August,
1914; and it will go on until something
begins to crumple up. Then, and not till
then, will the next act in the tragedy com-
mence. Meanwhile the armies in the field
are not so much agitated about the com-
missariat department’s address as about the
ammunition waggons being kept full and the
arrival of reinforcements. Unless there are
reinforcements such fighting as is now in
progress would be mere futility; and the
points for the Australian citizen to become
familiar with are that big holes have been
knocked in the Australian regiments, and
that the battle has not been won and cannot
be unless the Allies make good their wastage.
The rock-bottom problem is whether the
Australian forces shall be the first to wither
before the enemy. The Bulletin says the
answer should be “No.” The opponents of
compulsory service say “Yes” —that if there
is to be victory it should be won for Aus-
tralia by others. They offer no alternative
to service; they simply say that Australia
has done enough and should quit, leaving
its sons and brothers and old mates in the
trenches to take their chance and hack
themselves out if they can. If that is Aus-
tralia’s way, then this paper has made a
mistake and doesn’t know Australia.
[Written to express the views of The Bulletin,
within the limitations prescribed under the War
Precautions Act, by S. H. Prior, D. M. Wright and
F. J. Kirby, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
Dr. A. A. Martin, who died of wounds in
France, was one of the two or three great
surgeons of Maoriland. Native-born about
40 years ago, the son of a railway guard,
Martin first earned a crust in the State Life
Insurance Office. In 1894 he went to the
Edinburgh medical school, and brilliantly
Qualified. Later, he
served through the
Boer War, returned
to M.L., and settled
in the North Island.
The romance of his
knife brought pil-
grims to his operating
shrine from all over
the unstable isles. He
was attending a con-
ference in Aberdeen
when Sheol over-
flowed, and he hurried
to the Front just
after the Mons wrench.
For his strenuousness
in the Battle of the
Marne he was men-
tioned in despatches.
Invalided to England
he wrote “A Surgeon
in Khaki,” and was
one of the first to
boldly disclose Brit-
ain’s tragic unpre-
paredness. Returning
to M.L. in 1915, he
resumed private prac-
tice, but felt the call
again in a few
months, and finally figured in the push on
the Somme. He’s in good company.
Exit at last, after a long, lingering illness,
William Bickford, head of the South Aus.
manufacturing druggist firm. His father
was the first chemist in Adelaide, but died
young, and the sons, William and Harry,
when old enough to take charge, gave up
the shop and built up a big wholesale busi-
ness. William found time also for station-
owning and a bit of speculating. He was
75 when the end came, but had been for
several years dead to the world.

The Gonsalez Co. gave us “Aida” at Syd-
ney Opera House on Monday evening and
dressed it better than other performances
had led us to expect. As “Aida” is essen-
tially a spectacular pageant into which Verdi
flung- a blaze of orchestration, the scenery is
important; and except for the alleged burial
of Aida and her lover, everything was at
least passable. But the great climax of the
opera—the death-scene and song in the
cellar below the temple—was a failure. Tenor
Dagradi, discovered alone in a comfortable
dugout—the stage, undisguised, making the
foreground—showed no sign of emotion. Even
the entry of Aida de Revers, who dashed in
from the back, failed to excite him. Their
subsequent swan-song was a poor thing,
and they died immediately afterwards, to the
relief of many undistinguished persons pre-
sent.
* * * *
Soprano de Revers, though more restrained
in the output of her sharp upper register,
was unfortunately dressed for the great occa-
sion in “Aida.” There was nothing to
choose between her appearance in green
sequins and shaded red boots and her end in
pink spangles and ditto boots. Basso Cacialli,
vocally one of the co.’s big assets, is a good
all-round actor-man, too. Hence his success
with a eoatee beard and an important billet
in the Temple. He led the Solemn Men on
and off the stage, singing not too badly as
they went. Tenor Dagradi was the chief
walking-stick of the performance. Miss
Russ, the haughty minx Amneres, daughter
of the King of Egypt, was the surprise of
the opera. For once her tremolo was almost
forgotten, and she sang the beautiful music
in full, rich notes. Impassioned in her act-
ing, she made her big hit in a. dragging-
scene towards the close of Radames’s coffee-
colored career. Filippini, King of Ethiopia,
in mission drapery, retained the short brass
horns of his primitive splendor, and gave an
admirable vocal account of a small part.
The oi-chestra was fairly good.
“Rigoletto,” heard last week, ranks with
“Pagliaeci” among the Gonsalez Co.’s' per-
formances in Sydney. They stand above all
the others. This was in large part due, so
far as “Rigoletto” is concerned, to the
charm of Miss Gonsalez as Gilda. Her
sweet, pure notes and consistent efforts to be
in the picture brought the thrush-like singer
a storm of applause. When she had finished
“Caro Nome” the; house sent her its love
across the footlights. Rigoletto (Filippini)
“ROMANCE,” AT SYDNEY CRITERION.

Livingstone.
Arthur Greenaway as Harry
Putman.
gave an even and interesting performance.
It was inevitable that tenor Lois —a hand-
some young rascal as the bad Duke —would
be compared with John McCormack in the
famous “La donna e mobile.” But John
doesn’t sing in opera at the price—so there
you are! Both orchestra and chorus were
above their ordinary uncertain level.
“To-night's the Night” started work at
Her Majesty’s, Sydney, last week-end and
proved to be the same old gorgeous English
joke about tlfe well-dressed people who are
living on the bevelled edge of divorce, and
arrange to sneak out to a second-act
cafe where there is a comic head-waiter and
all food-traffic obligingly stops whenever one
of the young women feels like singing a song
full of tender U.S. sentiment in the vesti-
bule, with a humming chorus of sympa-
thetic but unintroduced male customers who
sneak in and hover beyond the limelight
beam like watchful undertakers. There is
also the mildly-wild youth with no money
who takes a racketty lady out to supper,
and the furious elderly lady of whom every-
body is afraid and who has to have the con-
volutions of the plot explained to her at
great length while the orchestra lays down
its instruments and steals away for a smoke
and a yarn. It always happens in the sec-
ond act of these shows, and the writer feels
“FAUST,” AT SYDNEY OPERA HOUSE.
Faust ( Cappelli ), Mefistofie ( Cacialli).
like bribing the musicians heavily to slide
back into their chairs and drown the
unnecessary explanations in loud, cheerful
melody. In London, where these things are
assembled from other successes of the past,
the ineffectual author seems to be allowed
the first part of the second act in which to
work out his awfully funny complications
and show Aunt Julia how it was her college
nephew came to take the maid out to sup-
per with the money he got by pawning her
bracelet. Work and shout as they will, the
musical comedians can’t make it sound as
interesting as an argument in the Equity
Court over a misplaced boundary fence. But
it justifies someone having his name on the
bill as “librettist.” Other indispensable
characters are the broken-English specialist
(man or woman), the comic second waiter,
the Gay Old Dog (Old Cat’s Husband) and
the well-dressed guests (all more or . less
titled, like the people the English illus-
trated weeklies run after) who are always
waiting around the corner ready to sing,
dance or march and use their hands like
puppies’ paws. London has got it all stan-
dardised now, and “To-night’s the Night,”
finely dressed and mounted, is the stuff that
all these screams are made of.
* * * *
Dainty Dorothy Brunton leads the
featherweight show with assured dash; but
on the opening night a drab little girl-super
with a penny flag, marched on with
the others to make a dab of pathos, trans-
formed a merciless patriotic ballad of the
star’s into a roaring comic song by turning
hrerself into a clockwork Bernhardt behind
the singer. Some of the stage girls who do
the fashion-book attitudes tried mistakenly
to straighten up the tiny comedienne; but
the house had spotted the performance and
Dorothy had to pick up the poor kid and
share the applause with her. Maud Fane,
who is in the cast as one of the young wives,
hasn’t many opportunities. _ Connie Ediss,
as the English-French maid who. falls on
and off the sofa, stays out all night, and
comes back looking very weatherbeaten to
give notice, lets slip the few words that
show the ancient French farce-origin of. the
show. Querulous Connie is at her best in a
“So Long, Letty” wrapper or early-morning
disarray. Little Rita Nugent and Jack
Hooker supply an eccentric step dance. It
is one of the best things in a show that is
so light that it has to be held down with a
sofa. Field Fisher, Workman, Maguire,
Plunkett, Marie Eaton, Cecil Bradley and
others are all at their accustomed posts
doing their little bits. There is plenty of
song-and-dance talent available, and some
more of it could be used if a few tons of the
wearisome plot-explanations were jettisoned.

“While the Billy Boils” la composite
stage-version of Henry Lawson’s various
story-books, arranged by Beaumont Smith)
opens at Sydney Royal on Saturday under
the direction of E. J. Carroll and the
adapter. It is a dramatic experiment which
has the good wishes of reading Australia
and all who know Lawson. The names of
players lan Maclaren, Arthur Styan, G.
Kay Souper, Tom Cannam and Esther
.Mitchell guarantee a sound performance.
“Romance,” which is Madge Fabian,
seems in for a long run at Sydney Criterion;
and a play that can survive its silly and
unconvincing prologue, which is chiefly
gramophone selections and a gaiterless
bishop, and its epilogue, which is merely
bathos and more bathrobed bishop, deserves
its success.

Till the Follies come back on October 9,
Sydney Tivoli is keeping the wolf from the
door and dragging in the paying lamb with
turns that have been mentioned before.
Cardo and Noll, the operatic specialists, and
singer Louis London are followed by Stanley
and Burns, Kelly and Pern (of the happy,
twangy “Bungalow”), O’Donnell and the
nursegirl who thinks she is Melba, the Artois
Brothers and the horizontal bars they whirl
on, the two Carlyons and Le Roy, and one
or two others.
At Sydney picture-shows this week
Hoyt’s : “The Snow Bird,” an entertaining story
playing in and out among the beauties of Hudson
Bay; “A Woman’s Past,” which won’t stand too
close investigation'; “Monster British Guns” ; and a
couple of comedy touches.
Lyceum : “Hell’s Hinges,” in which a Rev. Yar-
rington cowboy blows up the underworld with his
six-shooter; “The Little Schoolma’am,” a remarkable
child-actor film; and Charlie Chaplin among the
lobsters and fantods of “One A.M.”
A revival of “The Geisha” at Melbourne
Majesty’s throws no new light on the
mystery of its enormous success 20 years
. ago. When first produced at Daly’s Theatre,
London, this weak-minded combination of
comic opera and musical comedy ran for
26 months without a break. Even Aus-
tralia, which wanted a lot of pleasing at
that time, enthused over the new thing on
account of its simple flowing melodies and
its audacious plagiarisms from “Mikado,”
and because George Lauri was fitted with a
big fat part in it. Nowadays the plot seems
ridiculous, the dialogue mostly unworthy of
Owen Hall, who afterwards wrote “Floro-
dora,” and the jingling lyrics painfully
puerile. They are feeble narrative ditties
as a rule, the unsentimental ones, written
for the frisky comedy lady, demanding from
her certain tricks of mimicry. Minnie Love,
in the part of Molly Seamore, is an extra-
large size frisky tomboy, and her warble is
no great shakes ; but she has a happy turn
for mimicry, and her genial vivacity is a
notable feature of the show. Minnie is
always something more than likeable and
less than adorable. Perhaps she would be
quite adorable if she subjected her round
shoulders to a course of Sandow exercise.
Phil Smith is satisfactory as a worried
Chow who carelessly lapses into cockney-
isms. Ethel Cadman, whilst singing the
name-part artistically, acts it in a tame,
perfunctory way. Hudson —a less affected
Derek than of yore, but not in such good
voice on Saturday—has more to sing and
say than Holland and Ralston, who are less
convincing sons of the sea than Nellie
Payne. This little midshipman is a streak
of sunshine. Bantoek is a dull sort of Jap
potentate; Millie Engler, Alice Bennetts and
Charles Albert fill small parts; likewise
Reg Roberts, who does his little bit very
carefully. Chorus and orchestra will be
kept busy by “The Geisha” for another
week. Then “The Cinema Star.”
** * *
C. J. Dennis has found in Lawrence
Campbell an almost ideal interpreter of his
Sentimental Bloke. The elocutionist, who
arrived at Melbourne Athenaeum on Satur-
day, tells Bill’s story with a spirit and
humor that implies complete understanding
of the points of the character. A quiet,
unaffected delivery and a gift for facial ex-
pression are the entertainer’s chief assets,
but he also possesses an artistic restraint
that prevents him depicting comedy with
circus effects, or drowning a pathetic scene
in counterfeit tears. He gets the last ounce
of allowable humor out of such items as
“Hitched,” for example, and feelingly sug-
gests in “Beef Tea” the sentimental hiatus
between Bill and Doreen, when a little extra
emphasis either way would result in mushy
anti-climax ruining the pictures. Lawrence
Campbell relates the history of Bill’s emer-
gence from the Bloke state and his rise to
the dignity of a family man in 13 racy
chapters, and will continue to do it until
further notice.
“It Pays to Advertise” is exalting the
virtues of No. 13 soap for six nights and
one matinge at Melbourne Royal. The duty
of booming the amazing merchandise again
falls on Hale Hamilton, who revels in the
job with a tongue refreshed by its recent
comparative inaction in “Too Many Cooks.”
Hale is vociferous in his most frenzied
moments, but he has some moderately quiet
periods when he can be just heard in
Bourke-street. Donald Bowles figures
afresh as Rodney Martin, whom Mary
Grayson adopts for life. Donald disguises
a character full of pugnacity and Amurkan
push with an exquisite bleat, and by some
effort of acting or natural charm makes the
impersonation attractive enough. The
dollar-hunger of Mary aforesaid occasionally
brings a cash-register look on the face of
Myrtle Tannehill, but generally she
doesn’t look as though she should be
accused of the heroine’s striking business
qualities. Nancye Stewart, promoted to the
part of the Comtesse de Beaurien, is confi-
dent and untroubled in all her phases as the
bogus aristocrat and spieleress. Edwin
Lester, who formerly appeared as William
Smith (now a forte baritone role by Arthur
Cornell), has less capacity for exasperation
than the previous exponent, Tom MeLarnie.
The only other change of note is the minor
eruption of Mattee Brown in the part of
the French maid. Next Saturday the long-
promised comedy, “A Full House,” will be
road a first time.
A week’s revival of “Quinneys” at the
Melbourne Playhouse is mainly interesting
for the first appearance of Gregan McMahon
in the role of Joe Quinney, formerly made
massive and bucolic by Arthur Styan.
Joseph is less physically dominating in the
hands of McMahon; but Mac’s imperso-
nation is equally forceful and dogmatic and
has a stronger artistic flavoring. Styan,
with all his technical excellence, never sug-
gested an art dealer born to the business.
He presented the improbable picture of a
connoisseur of turnips and mangel-wurzels
afflicted with the collecting mania late in
life. Eily Malyon resumes her convincing
grief as Susan Quinney, and Joy Gwyn
returns to the drudgery of making Posy
Quinney as alluring as possible. W. H. L.
Cooper again refuses to flatter the author to
the extent of looking agreeably employed in
the character of Miggott. Cooper’s furni-

The reception given to Dad and the rest
of the Rudd family at Melbourne King’s
last Saturday suggests that “On Our Selec-
tion” has become a habit with a lot of
people, like meals. They just eat it every
time the Bert Bailey Co. comes along, and
have always got an edge on their appetite.
A new Billy Bearup, with the regulation
falsetto, is the only change in the cast since
the last time Sarah turned her thoughts to
love. The wrath of Dad is still associated
with Bailey s whisker-patch, and Dave’s nose
turns up in the same old place, and the voice
of Mum continues to sound like a noise
borrowed from a sawmill.
For the information of those who have
not yet heard Paul Dufault sing the Mar-
seillaise it may be mentioned that his week
at A|elbourne Auditorium will not be up
until Saturday.
The Tivoli Follies will be more or less
entertaining for one more Melbourne week,
and meanwhile Daisy and Sydney Yates
have joined the Mclntosh forces. They
dance as they lately danced for Williamson,
or even more so, and the plaudits of the
congregation are loud and long sustained.
A picture of Verdun and its surroundings
lends variety to the programme at Hoyt’s
theatres, Melbourne, this week. It is only
a long series of tableaux depicting soldiers
and guns, battered buildings and silent
misery, but it gives the spectator some idea
of the punishment France has received and
delivered in the locality. Of the fiction the
most remarkable samples are two breathless
stories called “Blazing Love” and “The
Quitter.” The first, featuring the beauteous
Virginia Pearson, is not so torrid as its title
suggests. The other illustrates a protracted
combat over the usual sweet girl, with
plenty of artillery practice among the con-
tending parties.
At an art show: —
Irene Rentoul-Outhwaite has a roomful of
brownies and fairies on view at the Melbourne
Centreway. The G.-G. did the official opening, and
afterwards bestowed connoisseur glances on the won-
derful menagerie that the artist had collected.
Rackham’s eerie color schemes and much of his
technical dexterity are disclosed by this clever lady
in several fancies that arrest the grown-up eye and
give small children delicious shudders. The tinted
work is the best, and there is good excuse for linger-
ing over such things as “The Spirits of the
Bush Fire,” “The Moon Boat,” and “The Witch.”
In black and white the artist is graceful and imagi-
native, but her meticulously careful pen-line some-
times so hardens the effect that the sense of fantasy
is lost, and myth looks as everyday as Smith. Her
fairies, however, are charming, and her drawing of
trees is really fine.
❖* * *
Adelaide Repertory Theatre has been giv-
ing Synge’s “Tinker’s Wedding”—from the
Church point of view, a shocking little
piece: the parish priest drinks with the
lowest tipplers, chaffers over marriage fees,
and finally, showing a nasty spirit over the
theft of a tin can which formed part of the
fee, is tied up by the tinkers with his head
in a bag. Poor stuff. Ray Walsh, sound
as ever, almost succeeded in making the
farcical figure seem a priest of reality, but
chief honors go to Mrs. Bastard as a tip-
pling, talkative gypsy woman. “Miss
Civilisation,” a one-acter, by the late Hard-
ing Davis, ought to be already popular in
the “regular” theatre. Blacked-out stage,
with files heard at work, and a girl tele-
phoning very quietly for help; then three
burglars, and the girl entertaining them at
supper, with chaff, argument and defiance,
till the help comes. It is a modern parallel
to “Comedy or Tragedy,” which Gilbert
wrote for Mary Anderson. The leading part
would be hard to go wrong in, and Kitty
Sullivan did it full justice, except that she
made no attempt at the American accent.
Young, handsome, graceful and already
skilled, Miss Sullivan is a valuable addition
to Adelaide’s long list of amateurs.
Adelaide Tivoli has a local monopoly of
the spoken drama just now, the dashing old
“Tommy Atkins” being Anderson’s attrac-
tion for the week. The Fuller extravaganza
crowd has wisely moved from the King’s to
the larger stage of the Majestic (to which
house Dan Thomas, Mankin the frog, and
other clever people should have been credited
last week), and Waller’s' Strollers at the
Queen’s once again prove themselves strong-
est on the musical side. West’s has a
delightful picture in which a timid would-be
suicide hires an assassin to put him out of
liis misery, and then, changing his mind,
has to dodge the executioner with great
agility. Wondergraph films the old Brough
success, “The Masqueraders.”
AT POVERTY POINT.
Elocutionist Lawrence Campbell, from
Sydney, is now at the Athenaeum elocuting
“The Sentimental Bloke,” whose creator
was C. J. Dennis. Campbell is a rawboned
Scot with a full acre of gleaming forehead
and fierce, over-hanging eyebrows which
have weathered the storms of 10 million
recitations. His dress suit is cut in the
style of 1820 —but that makes no difference.
W hat did make a difference on Saturday
was the streaming weather. Good for the
country perhaps, but bad for the showman.
(( Playhouse had a poor audience for
Quinneys” on Saturday. The rain had
descended for two days and two nights with-
out ceasing, and though the theatre itself is
cosy and warm, it sits by the river and takes
getting to. Probably it was the wetness
outside that led to an extra turn. A huge
rat leaped from the heavy folds of the cur-
tain as it was dropped and made across the
stage, to the horror of the stalled Women
in front. People in the audience who come
from Yorkshire commended the brand of
accent used by Gregan McMahon and Eily
Malyon. But they had no praise for the
Repertory Rat.
Melba gave an exhibition of singing tui-
tion in the presence of the G.-G. and Lady
Helen last week. Every one of her students
came up to scratch, and faced the music
like Anzacs. When the last scale was fin-
ished his Ex. confided to the assembly that
he knew more about bagpipes than grand
opera. And then he praised. Melba —from a
bagpiper’s point of view, no doubt—and
made himself pleasant generally.
“Pen”: The actor Theodore Hamilton,
who has just died in America, was the first
man to wear white spats on an Australian
stage. _ As Archibald Carlyle in “East
Lynne” he showed Melbourne the feet-
wraps in the old Princess’s Theatre in ’7B,
where, by the way, he played with Mrs.
Mary Gladstone, a brilliant actress. I sub-
sequently discovered this lady, poor and
broken-through no faults of her own —in
New York. She was then performing in a
dime museum.
The divorce suit of divinely tall Daisy
Yates v. exceptionally diminutive Dan
Thomas, heard in Melbourne last week,
seems to have been decided without dragging
in the testimony of a local woman journalist
who interviewed the little pantomime
“dame” when he first came to Melbourne
with Marlow’s co. At that time Dan’s
lawful Daisy was looking for him in
S’Africa, but another charmer was intro-
duced to the interviewer as “Mrs. Thomas,”
and was written of accordingly. Seeing
that the spurious Mrs. T. had loomed so
large in her matrimonial capacity, it was
magnanimous of Daisy Yates to give her
errant hubby a chance to “make it up for
the sake of the child.” His stage name, by
the way, is constructed on much the same
lines as that of the late Lewis Waller—a
perversion of W alter Lewis. His wife' got
her decree nisi against Thomas Henry
Daniels.
Freda Bennett, youngest daughter of Syd-
ney News Alfred Bennetts, who owned the
Dailey-built mansion of Clairvaux at Rose
Bay for nearly 80 years, made her profes-
sional bow to the public last week in the
Criterion production of “Romance.” Good
looks, good hair and some solid amateur
experience are among her assets, and should
be a help along the pathway to a star.
The old Norfolk Arms, in Rundle-street.
Adelaide, now balances on the brink of a
hole which is going to be a new picture
theatre. The old Clarence in King William-
street, which did the same for a time, while
the Majestic Theatre was being erected on
the site of the original Tivoli (or Bijou),
is now just being finished as a four-storey
Majestic Hotel —a fair match for the
Exchange, alongside of which it stands.
They want a lot of good-bye, these
show people. The latest to return for another
helping is Maskelyne, the conjurer, showing,
when the last mail left, at St. George’s,
London. The ancient cabinet and box trick
was again his star line. Fifty-two years
ago he claims to have invented it. and since
then he has performed it 11,000 times.
Yet he repenteth not, although he is 77 and
cannot have much longer to live.
Tom Shelford and Harry J. Ford, the
thin and plump comedians of “The Glad
Eye” Comedy Co., have joined the artillery
in England, and Laurence Hardinge, brother
of the rotund Bob Greig, is in camp with
the Flying Corps.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
The bitter cry of an Australian officer
after the Pozieres push :
For Heaven’s sake, teach Billjim Avhen to stop!
Hundreds of the finest, bravest felloAvs in the world
are lying out there simply because they reckoned
they could do more than they Avere asked to do and
tried to do it. The plan Avas to take two trenches
and a communication trench, and hold them. The
staff had worked out everything to fit in with that.
Our fellows took the two trenches at a gallop. “Oh,
this is too easy!” they said. “AVe can do another.”
They rushed the third and occupied it. Then two
things happened. The Germans flooded the trench,
and our fellows found they were without
supports and must get back. But the Ger-
mans had come in behind them. Machine guns
and artillery could not help them: they could not
do damage to the enemy without killing their own
men who Avere iioav fighting their Avay home. The
slaughter Avas horrible, and Avhen it Avas over they
Avere too weak and exhausted to hold even the second
line, Avhich, before, they could have held easily.
This eagerness is magnificent. In any other sort of
Avarfare it might be invincible. But in this siege
Avar, this conflict of great guns, there must be the
most perfect collaboration. It is machine Avarfare.
Get them drilled into this. They may be able to do
more than they are told. But to do just Avhat they
have been told to do is the best they can do. In
this business to do more is not to do better. Say
it again and again, and Avrite it up in letters a foot
long.

“Bobstay”: By a coincidence the same
week which saw the wreck of the oldest
sea-going steamer on the Australasian
Register (the Storm bird) Avitnessed the
decease of the most ancient Avindjammer—
the iron barque Antiope. This old packet,
built in ’66, Avent ashore at the Bluff
(M.L.) and became a total wreck. Con-
demned last year to be a hulk, the Antiope
Avas again commissioned when scarcity of
tonnage began to be keenly felt. The old
girl was Avell known on both sides of the
Pacific, as she ran in the lumber-one-way
and coal the-other trade for nearly half a
century. Her last A r oyage was always being
predicted, so the pessimists scored when
she was safely wrecked.

“Court Reporter”: It happened recently
at the Coroner’s Court, Parramatta
(N.S.W.), and lam prouder than ever of
the Australian spirit. The case was one in
which a returned wounded soldier was con-
cerned with the death of a boy 10 years old.
The soldier was shooting with a pea-rifle in
the bush on the outskirts of Lidcombe, Avhen
five boys and a dog joined him. The dog
jumped on the trigger of the rifle, exploding
the charge and fatally wounding one of the
boys. The point that was not quite clear
was whether the rifle, at the time, was being
held by the soldier or was lying on the
ground. The boy witness who delighted me
but horrified the coroner and the sergeant of
police had seen the importance of that point,
too, and it transpired that he took immediate
precautions to defend the soldier, by telling
the other boys to say that Bill Anzac was
not carrying the rifle when the dog jumped on
the trigger, but that it was lying on the
ground. At the court he admitted that it
was a lie. but he wasn’t going to let a
wounded Billjim get into trouble if a lie
would save him. And though all parties
combined to assure him that he was the
wickedest boy in the County of Cumberland
he looked as though he’d chance it, if neces-
sary, another time.
Justice Powers, of the High Court, has
made another amazing discovery—that
young women should not be paid the same
amount of money for doing the same
amount of work as young men, because
young women are not supposed to be saving
up to buy furniture, etc. On the same
principle, if Justice Powers has one child
and Justice Smith has nine, it plainly
shouldn’t matter whether Justice Powers
and Justice Smith do the same sort of
work and the same amount of it —Justice
Smith should get about three times as much
as Powers. The Bulletin hopes to see
Powers either recommend this to Parlia-
ment or throw his rotten theory overboard.
No reprieve came for Antonio Pic-one, the
Italian fruiterer who on July 25 fired three
shots from an automatic at his enemy,
Giuseppe Lauricella, a compatriot in the
same line of business, and laid him dead on
the pavement of the Victoria Market amid
the startled bargainers. Picone, having no
influential friends, was hanged in Mel-
bourne Gaol. His sole request oefore he
went on to the drop was that a photograph
and a lock of hair that he treasured should
be buried with him. The hangman, a free
man, had insured his anonymity by smudg-
ing his face with black and fringing it Avith
Avhiskers that put those of the Auld Licht
elders in an impenetrable shade. The
hangman’s chief of staff, who, though
undergoing a sentence, did flashy justice to
the permission accorded him to wear civilian
clothes, resembled the hero of a movie
drama. Picone, whose sanity seems to have
been doubtful, might at least, if not granted
a reprieve, have been spared this vaudeville
turn AA’ith a fake nigger in it.

Miss Golding, of the Women’s Progressive
Association, giving evidence before the
N.SAV. Parliamentary Committee on Ven-
ereal Diseases, said that, “while she would
not favor medical examination of both
parties before marriage, she thought a father
Avould be justified in asking his future son-in-
law for a certificate of health.” Possibly Miss
Golding may believe that there is no need to
worry about the goose if she makes sauce for
the gander, but some recent evidence given
to the committee by Dr. Maitland —who is a
colonel-surgeon in the Commonwealth De-
fence Forces —suggests a doubt: —
The disease that is being spread abroad is com-
ing: from the clandestine immoral girl, who is out-
wardly respectable. ... Down at Milson’s Island
(Sydney) I have had, in seven months, 2000 eases
of men suffering from venereal diseases, and I find that
the greatest number of these patients blame the Cen-
tral Railway Station. They confess that there has been
no talk of payment, and I know that these girls
keep away from the medical men and are not
known to the police.
Most of those “outwardly respectable” girls
will marry, and ev T en Miss Golding may he
inclined to admit that there are vast possi-
bilities in that fact.

Some years ago the Victorian Parliament
voted an annual £lOOO to the Melbourne
University for research scholarships, the
idea being to set the brainy Australian
student hunting for some nevv thing that
might be useful to his country. A good
start was made by spending the cash solely
on science research, but in 1911, acting on
the brilliant suggestion of the Education
Department, the University authorities
began to hand out awards for other sub-
jects. Lately the Assembly’s Public
Accounts Committee, perhaps suspecting
that the “research” students might have dug
up something that would be useful in these
stressful war times, went along to inquire,
and in its report mentioned some of the
nationally-valuable subjects on which the
money had been spent:—
A Statistical Inquiry into the Health of Austra-
lian Woman Graduates, £5O; the Scottish Political
Exiles to Australia, 1793-4, £100; Holinshed’s
Chronicles, £75; The Median Sagittal Tracing of
the Crania of Prehistoric Man, £5O; an Inquiry
into the Sources of some of Shakspeare’s Plays and
the Relations between Quartos and Folios, £5O;
The Exercise of the Prerogative of Dissolution in
Australasia, £100; Kant's Critique of Practical
Reason, £75; The Norma Facialis of the Aus-
tralian and Tasmanian Aboriginal Crania, £125.
The Committee reckoned that these things
were very well in their way, but as they
didn’t produce two cartridges where there
was only one before, the Assembly might
mention the need for cartridges when Act-
ing the next £lOOO, or words to that effect.

PROOF OF SOBRIETY.
WIFEY: “You came home very late last night."
HUBBY : “I looked at the clock when I got in and it teas only one."
WIFEY : “I didn’t say you were intoxicated. I said you were late."
turf. An Act just passed through the S.A.
Parliament will prevent such capers in
future. Part of the scheme was to remove
the old chimney on the hillside, but the co.
promised to let it stand on representation
being made that it has historic interest. It
was part of the plant of a silver-lead mine
which tried its luck there 70 years ago.
After being abandoned it was found to give,
when taken in line with the G.P.O. tower,
a useful bearing to ships away in the Gulf;
so it has been whitewashed periodically
ever since, and kept standing for that pur-
pose. The mixture of sentiment and utility
will probably be enough to preserve it
indefinitely, especially as the hill-slope is
too steep for use.

THE CAT: “But what about Herbert, dear?"
“Mary McCommonwealth”: A London
writer pleads for the standardisation of
woman’s dress. As a war economy it is the
best proposal yet put forward. Nobody has
worked out the difference between the cost of
a regulation female uniform and the amount
at present expended, but it can be safely
said that it would be enough to pay the
interest on the war loans. N. S. Wales has
a Bill before Parliament permitting women
to wear trousers if they want to; but,
trousers or skirts, the great economy w’ould
lie in clothing all women in the same stuff,
made on the same pattern and produced
cheaply in Australian workshops from
material made and grown in Australia.
Besides urging men to recruit and shed their
blood for their protection, what are women
doing to help on the war, anyhow? A lot
of them have given their sons and husbands,
and those may be tremendously big items ; but
many more have only given knitted socks
and a few entertainments, which amount to
nothing compared with the sacrifice of wear-
ing a national uniform. The change, besides
saving millions, would really promote happi-
ness, because the average man would be
able to arrive at a much juster estimate of
the average girl. At present women are
largely what their clothes make them, but
dressed in a national uniform they would
be largely what they are.

“Plumber”: I met little Billy Anzac
yesterday. He is just five, ginger and
freckled, and his father runs a dairy farm.
I had called in to see if there Avas any work
waiting for me. The missus said _ she
thought not, so I left. I had about finished
a small job at the house half a mile further
on, when I heard voices at the back door.
“ ’Day, Mrs. Greenfield; is the plumber
here?” “Yes, Billy.” “Hullo plumber!
Dad says please come back. He wants the
cooler pump fixed.” “All right, Billy; I’ll
be done here in two minutes. You wait out
by the cart.” Ten minutes later, as I slung
the tools in and lifted William up, I noticed
his wrist \\-as cut rather badly and didn’t
look as if it was going to stop bleeding. As
I bound my handkerchief round it I asked
him how he did it. “On a bit of glass in the
dairy window.” “But why didn’t you get your
mother to tie it up?” “I was going to, but
Dad said if I didn’t catch you here you’d
get away, and you wouldn’t be round again
for a month ; so I didn’t let on.”

“Silas Snell”, tells a true tale:—
There had been several burglaries in the suburbs
and Constable Clamm was very alert. Tucked in the
shadow of a pepper tree opposite be had seen the man
enter the garden at No. 9. He had watched, and the
door at No. 9 had not opened. Observing due pre-
cautions, Constable Clamm also stole into the garden
at No. 9. Creeping from bush to bush, he located
his quarry at work on a side window. Having shot
the window latch with his knife, the man opened the
window; then he sat on the sill and removed his
boots. It was while he was struggling with the
second boot that Constable Clamm did his dash. The
capture was easy. The other fellow offered no resist-
ance ; he merely looked Constable Clamm in the
face and grinned a bland, fatuous grin. “Fact ish
I’m married man,” he said. “If my wife knew I
was cornin’ ’ome at thish hour of the mornin’ she’d
lift the roof. Why should she know ? I put it to
you, offisher, as man to man, why Should a mansh
wife know when a man conies home?” Constable
Clamm was disconcerted. “ ’Tis no way to enter
your house afther midnight,” he said. “All
dependsh,” said the man in the window. “If you’re
single, you pleasli yourself; if you’re married, you
don’t displeash your wife. ’S tha’ true? Now we’re
friendsh, have drink —haveadrink wish me.” Con-
stable Clamm didn't mind if he did. The man stole
into the dark interior. He presently stole back with
two generous helpings of whisky. Constable Clamm
took one, and drank it with liberal good wishes.
Then the policeman withdrew from the case. He was
still half-dead with sleep when he reported. He
understood why when he knew that No. 9 had been
burgled that morning in the early hours and £212
worth of booty removed.

Will Lawson’s few words over the bones:
When the Stormbird, flying light, was lushed at
the W anganui bar and, finding the task impossible,
climbed over the western mole, she broke in halves.
But that is not the first time she has been in two
pieces. Built on the Clyde in 1854, the little vessel
was hauled up on the beach at Wellington 33 years
ago and a gang of ironworkers headed by one David
Robertson cut through her waist with cold chisels.
Eighteen feet of new framing and plating was intro-
duced between the original halves, and her
clipper bow was also straightened. It is proposed
now to erect a sort of memorial to her on the
wharf at Wellington. The most interesting part of
the old smoker was the deck-bell. For years it
hung behind the steersman on the bridge with'
“Stormbird 1854,” graven on it. When the dispute
began as to whether she or the Edina or a half-dozen
other vessels were the oldest steamer afloat, the
bell was removed to a more secluded place. Perhaps
it has been lost utterly in the wreck; survivors tell
that they heard it toll as the old identity jumped
the mole.

“P.X.”: When young Hotsox got the
military census card last year asking him
when he would be willing to go up for train-
ing he was one of the hundreds who tried
to cover their shame with flippancy. “When
the roses bloom again,” young Hotsox said.
The other day a very limp Hotsox junior
collapsed in a chair as he opened his mail.
“The roses are blooming again,” it said.
“Report to your military area headquar-
ters.”

“Quill” : Tom Rolin, K.C., struggles with
an avalanche of figures to prove that “under
350 people in Australia have incomes
of over £5OOO, under 7200 people have in-
comes of more than £lOOO. and only about
25,000 people have incomes of more than
£5OO a year”:—
The “wealthy” people drawing over £5OOO a
year take 1J per cent, of the total income....
People drawing £5OO a year and upwards draw
£24,298,681 out of the total £183,374,102; that
is to say, just over 13 per cent, of the total.
The answer is that the war-census cards, on
which the cheerful Iv.C. proceeds, are open
to a lot of suspicion. Take, for instance, the
allegation that under 7200 people have
incomes of more than £lOOO a year. In
Victoria alone there are 11,230 persons with
taxable incomes of from £5Ol upwards. If
allowance be made for the exemptions con-
ceded by law, Vic. alone can have nothing less
than 10,000 inhabitants with over £lOOO per
annum. That is, 2800 more than Mr. Rolin
allows for all Australia. He tells us that
people drawing £5OO a year and more get
only 13 per cent, of the aggregate Australian
income. I am entitled to say that my
friend’s evidence is no good even if I can't
produce any good evidence myself. I say
there is no conclusive evidence on the sub-
ject. But there is this evidence. Of the
N S. Wales income-tax payers 3 per cent,
are in receipt of 34 per cent, of the total
income on which taxation is levied. Tom
Rolin’s idea is that the inequality of wealth
is of not much account, after all. Perhaps,
in spite of the fact just cited, he is right.
But if he is it is quite an accident, because
he has used ridiculously misleading figures
to help mm to his conclusion.
“Splodger” : On the cheery subject of the
hangman’s fee. Years ago in the Kimberley
(W.A.) district a white man was murdered
by two blacks, who were later caught and con-
demned to be hanged at the spot the local
reporter would have marked with a cross if
there had been any local reporter. The hour
fixed for the function approached, but no
hangman was forthcoming. Tenders were
then hastily called, and £6O per head was the
lowest. I believe the hard-case who got the
job gave good value for the money. At
any rate, neither of the customers com-
plained.
Melbourne was recently asked by the
State War Council to do something special
in the way of raising cash for its repatria-
tion fund. A committee of citizens was
formed, discussed the matter at length, and
finally decided in the most brilliant manner
that its effort should take the form of yet
another “queen” com-
petition. The War
Council frowned on
the proposal, though
the idea promised a
large sum, since the
whole State was to be
the field of “queen”
selection ; but the com-
mittee is going on
with its plan, all the
same. The money
won’t go to the sol-
diers, however, but to
various charities. By-
and-bye, of course, a
yet larger effort will
be started by some
inspired idiot to dis-
cover the boss “queen”
of all Australia; and
in that day. if it is
still sane, this country
will put its foot down
and decide to ■ sustain
its “charities” by taxation, and so avoid
much frill and waste.
The confessions of “0.K.” :
I hate the wowser for his hypocritical self-righteous-
ness and intolerance. Yet in mine old age I find
myself drifting gently towards wowserism as regards
drinking, gambling and even smoking.
I would do more than merely close the pubs at 6
o’clock if I were king. No man should profit by
the production of liquor or be financially interested
iu inducing his fellow citizens to fill ’em up again.
If pubs were State enterprises, carried on under a
system of Government inspectorship, every manager
would be a vigilant preserver of sobriety on the
premises, under penalty of dismissal if he wasn’t.
As regards the gambling evil, which cannot be
eliminated from commercial business, I would con-
fine betting on horse-races to the racecourse by means
of the tote. In this, I know, I am dead opposed to
the wowser, but that is only because wowserism
still remains ill-instructed. My dismal bloodless
brother believes that the tote would provide further
facilities for the temptation, ruination and damna-
tion of sinners who refuse to be miserable. He
knoweth not the game. It abolishes the temptation
ot the betting shops in town, and reduces the losses
or punters on the course. The legalised tote, like
the quality of mercy, is twice blessed. Indeed it
is thrice blessed, for it aids the hospitals in a
socialistic way, and must eventually get the sorrow-
tin sanction of the snufflebusters. As a strictly worldly
wowser I pray for the coming of the tote to Vic-
toria. I would fain be delivered by the Law from
some temptation, and be benefited by the decent
dumb machine which pays a dividend on the second
gee-gee in a race.
And as regards smoking, which is hateful to the
average wowser and injurious to anybody when
practised to excess, why, in the name of common
sense, is the vendor of tobacco privileged to trade
after 6 o’clock? llow comes it that the suffragettes
have overlooked this glaring example of man-made
legislation, and that my dismal brother has averted
his fishy gaze from the sinful anomaly? Oft in the
noisy night, after smoking more than is good for
me and being left without a cigarette, I crave for
further self-indulgence. The pubs are closed, but
the open tobacco shop lures me to its counter. The
absurdity of it! Therefore I rise to move that the
tobacco trade be brought under the same law as the
drapery and ironmongery trades.
And oh, my friends, let us jump with all our feet
—our large flat feet—upon the fallacy that men
cannot be made sober or moral by Act of Parlia-
ment. In face of the fact that Parliament does
protect us against a lot of temptation and could
save us from a good deal more, the old pernicious
maxim is still dinned into our ears. It is the creed
of the brewer, the Tory newspaper and all such
Anti-Socialists. It is the Dogma of the Devil.
“W.L.”: Five men hailing from Sydney
and Newcastle intend to navigate the paddle-
tug Ivoputai from Port Chalmers (M.L.) to
Sydney. The vessel, which was built in
Scotland in 1870 and has been churning up
sand in shallow Otago waters since early
youth, carried a carronade in her bows dur-
ing those eventful years. But Captains
Dunne and Bailey and the other three
mariners want every inch of space and every
ounce of buoyancy, so the old gun is to be
dismounted and raffled in aid of the Maori-
land patriotic funds. The Koputai’s register is
158 tons, and she couldn’t carry enough coal
to push herself right across the Tasman. At
first it was thought that a big collier would
pick her up in Cook Strait and rattle her
across the seas at the end of a towline till
Sydney Heads loomed up. But the five Aus-
tralians who have bought her and are going
to use her as a tug in Port Jackson scoffed
at the idea .of being wet-nursed. They will
fill her with coal and bend sails on the old
spars and do the trip on a mixed diet of wind
and steam. When the rush set in to Hokitika
scores of boats no bigger than the Koputai
thus barged their way across the pond.
One, the Settler, thought she was heading
for Maoriland, and was surprised to find her
nose poking into Moreton Bay. The compass
had gone overboard early in the proceedings,
The Settler never tried to go home. She
settled at Brisbane and became one of the
town’s respected residents.

THE WILLIETANK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am the boojum, I’m the tank,
The latest thing in British swank,
The newest taxi on the rank,
The willy-nilly Willie!
A weird, terrestrial submarine,
A monster of the pleistocene,
The grimmest joke that’s ever been —
My humor knocks ’em silly !
I am the hedgehog and the slug,
I’m creme de menthe in a big jug;
The strongest dugout ever dug
Is squashed where’er I revel.
And when, contemplative, serene,
Upon a wall I lightly lean,
There’s nothing where that wall had been:
I’ve smoothed my pathway level!
I am a firing-trench on wheels,
A dreadnought on two crazy keels,
That over craters crawls and reels,
With sudden death within it.
The dizzy speed that I can make
Some beating certainly will take,
World-records I am sure to break—
I go a yard a minute!
I am the taxi that will ply
From France to Berlin by-and-bye,
Although I don’t exactly fly
I’ll get here, willy-nilly.
For I’ve arranged to pick up there
A most important passenger,
A highly-profitable fare,
Whose name is also Willie!
N.S.W.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Sj’dney IJni. has produced an LL.D. after
a spell of 13 years; and the man who took
the degree then, Dr. Waddell, was the first
to do it after the Law School had been estab-
lished 12 years previously. The new doctor
doesn’t practise law in a regular way; he is
Frank Gellatly, who makes the S. M.
Herald’s trade and finance columns so well
worth reading. He succeeded the late Peter
Proctor as Financial Editor of the Herald
11 years ago, and has now and then put so
much ginger into his page that the old lady
has got very red in the face.
The late “Barney” Wise was very keen
on winning when once he got really inter-
ested in a case. Jack Want once told
another “silk” that he didn’t think he would
appear with Wise again—“ Barney” was
“a bit over the odds.” “You know,” he
said, “he misquoted a letter in that case
to-day.” “Yes,” said the other, “that’s
pretty bad.” “Oh, I didn’t mind him mis-
quoting,” said Want. “But he doesn’t do
it with discretion. I was on the other side,
and he ought to have made sure first that
I didn’t have a copy.”
W. G. Spence, the finest old figure in the
Labor movement in Australia, has been
“suspended” by the A.W.U. Forty years of
his life Spence has been working for the
workers. In the late ’7o’s he saw that
Chinese labor threatened the miners of Vic-
toria, and he fathered the A.M.A. When
he had seen the miners organised and
capable of fighting their own battles he took
in hand the bush workers —primarily the
shearers—till then the worst-treated body of
laborers in Australia. Nobody who doesn’t
know the violence of the pastoralists’ oppo-
sition at that time and the apparent hope-
lessness of organising nomadic labor can
realise the size of the job that this great-
hearted man faced. But he was as indomit-
able as he was gentle, as valiant as he was
unselfish and incorruptible. Ever since then
he has been the guide and philosopher of
the big bush union, the long-headed old man
who sought to benefit everybody but himself.
And now, because he can see no way out
but conscription, and because he must tell
the truth as he sees it, he is “suspended.”
He isn’t good enough to belong to the unions
which he fathered, which he fought for when
there weren’t a dozen others to do it. He
must be a sad old man—-not for himself, but
remembering that those whom the gods
destroy they first make mad.
Holman in his fine Assembly speech on
the death of B. R. Wise hinted dimly at a
good turn once done him by the deceased.
This was the good turn. Early in 1903
Holman had passed his final . exam, for
entrance to the Bar. The profession, recall-
ing the Socialist’s connection with the
Daily Post failure and his subsequent
trial, thought they saw a reason for oppos-
ing his admission, and accordingly invoked
the Bar Council. B. R. Wise was Attorney-
General at the time andj as such, head of the
Council. After hearing argument he ad-
journed the meeting until the following day.
Meantime Holman was invited to gather up
his robe and hasten to the court, where
early the following morning Wise himself
moved for his admission. Later the same
day a message came reminding the Attorney-
General of the adjourned meeting; but the
man with a heart sent back word that it
was then too late to deal with the case, as
the awful fellow had been admitted that
morning.
The Anglican gaiters at Armidale
(N.S.W.), vacant since Bishop Cooper’s
death a couple of months ago, are to be
filled by Wentworth-Sheilds, who superin-
tends Sydney’s most fashionable collection-
plate at St. James’s. His father was one
of the Ma State’s earliest railway engineers,
who worked on the plans of the first line
built from Sydney. Sheilds (one of whose
minor worries is that comps, will persist in
printing him as “Shields”) was boi'n in the
Cold Country a shade over 50 years ago.
After getting his M.A. at London he was a
curate in that city till his cousin, Dr. Bar-
low, who then bished at Goulburn (N.S.W.),
imported him in 1902 as precentor. He
afterwards shepherded at Cooma and Wagga,
in Barlow’s diocese, wearing an archdeacon’s
A Phil May drawing of fust-dead B. R.
WISE, Agent-General of N.S. Wales.
spats for a while in the latter town. In
1910. when the vestments riot broke out on
parson Carr Smith’s departure from St.
James’s, Sheilds was in a temporary job at
St. Ivilda (Vic.), and his invitation thence
to Sydney by Archgaiters Wright was the
final move in the shindy. As he is tactful
and broad-minded, he managed to keep the
congregation together and smooth its ruffled
feathers.
The Edina, which is the oldest steamer
afloat in southern waters now that the
Stormbird has gone, has probably the
skipper with the longest service on one
bridge. For 36 years Captain Forbes has
run her up and down between Melbourne
and Geelong, and her chief engineer, Nolan,
has been 33 years on his job.
The young dominie has done wonderfully
well for Australia in this war. Carl
Ivaeppel, one of the latest batch of Aus-
tralians to get the Military Cross, is another
of them. Born at Mittagong (N.S.W.y 29
years ago, of Dutch parentage, he gained
his B.A. at Sydney University with the
highest honors obtainable in classics. Since
then he has been teaching, first at the
Anglican Grammar School at North Syd-
ney, and latterly at Armidale.
HE HOPED THEY HADN’T ROBBED
HIM.
WIFE (with grieved accent ): “John, you’re
drunk!”
JOHN : “Well! I’ve toasted four-an’-shim if
I’m not.”
Politicians who represented the Northern
Territory in the S.A. Parliament have
found the experience a good stepping-stone.
Vabe Solomon (since dead) was for just one
giddy week Premier of the State; Charles
Herbert became Administrator of the Terri-
tory and is a Judge in Papua; now the
lightweight S. J. Mitchell has succeeded to
the police-magistracy in Adelaide left vacant
by the untimely death of Tom Gepp.
Theodore Flatau, killed in France at 26
while captain in the East Surrey regiment,
was boi'n in N. S. Wales and schooled at
old Sydney Grammar. After going through
Oxford, he tried the inky way and edited a
Cairo paper. Latterly he had been a Lon-
don journalist. Flatau was the author of
three novels, of which “The Soul of a
Dancer,” published while he was in Egypt,
is best known. His father is a doctor in
North Sydney, and a brother is on the staff
of London Times.
In a recent Bulletin appeared, at the
end of a fine war-story, a name which is
now on the roll of honor of the noblest
Republic. The news has just come through
of the death of Lieut. Rene Tournouer, of
the Second Zouaves, and formerly of Mel-
bourne and Ballarat. He fell when his
regiment counter-attacked at Fleury, on the
Verdun front, on July 20, after having
passed scathless through the Champagne
offensive of last September, when he won
the Croix de Guerre. Early in 1915 his
eldest brother Jacques was killed, also in
the 2nd Zouaves. It was his place that
Rene took. He will be succeeded in turn
by a third brother, Roger, formerly well
known in Maryborough (Q.). A fourth
brother, Gontran, whose Christian name,
appropriately, is the same as that of Du
Maurier’s “Zouzou,” left Brisbane in Octo-
ber, 1914, with the First Light Horse Regi-
ment. The father of these gallant lads is
a major on the staff of General Petain, the
man who saved Verdun.
W. J. Back, dead at 77. was the oldest
member of the Adelaide Stock Exchange.
He was chairman of the old “South Aus-
tralian” Exchange, and one of the 50
original members who in 1887 founded the
present body, which superseded it. The
only other survivors of the 50 are Gavin
Gardner, David Fotheringham, A. F.
Weaver and Sydney Beach.
Chaplain-Captain Mullineux, the wonder-
ful little English footballer of old, is in camp
in the Wairarapa (M.L.). Six months ago
he was running a seamen’s mission in ’Frisco.
But life in a neutral country was more than
he could put up with. His job now is to
attend to the spiritual needs of the soldiers
in the camp, and he has begun by asking for
a marquee, a piano, chairs, tables, books and
other trifles. Bibles and talks about the 12
Apostles can come later.
The death of the R.G.
Bishop of Bendigo is
announced; but, as a
fact, there has never
been any such person.
In 1875 Dr. Martin
Crane, of the order of
St. Augustine (the older
that Martin Luther
belonged to), was ap-
pointed by the Pope first
Bishop of Sandhfirst;
and. presently Dr.
Reville became his coad-
jutor. Dr. Cralie hffd
been head of an Augus-
tinian hofise of his
order in John-street,
Dublin. The church in
John-street had also a
high school for boys on
one of the quays of the
sweet-smelling Liffey,
and it was there Dr.
Reville worked before
he was sent out to Sand-
hurst, Bishop Crane hav-
ing fallen blind. That was
in 1885. He was made
nominal Bishop of Cera-
mis, and when Dr. Crane
died Reville succeeded
as Bishop of Sandhurst.
He was educated and
ordained at Ghent, in
Belgium, and he took an
enormous interest in the
War now going on. The
other day he, in his
tUrtt, applied for a dOad-
jhtor, and the clergy
sent three names to
Rome. It is very likely
one of the three —most
likely the “dignissimus”
—will now be made Bishop of Sandhurst.
Or Rome may now take the opportunity to
call the place “Bendigo.”
James Thomson, who has been a pro-
minent Laborite in Sydney ever since Dub-
lin boycotted him for participating in the
movement which Jim Larkin led, is one of
those who have blazed into wrath over the
P.L.L. attitude towards conscription.
Thomson has always been a fighter for
unionism and solidarity; but he was also
prepared to fight for the liberty that makes
both possible. He got into khaki early in
the war, has been away doing his bit, and
knows the Hun the other talkers have only
read about.
ARTHUR MYERS, Minister of Munitions,
Maoriland.
The Hun finished another son of the late
Dick O’Connor, of the High Cpurt, when
Lieut. Roderick Alan O’Connor went out
in France lately. He had a commission in
the Royal Leinsters, and only got to the
Front in May.
The Speck has got its first woman coun,-
cil-clerk: Grace Roberts is to help keep the
Huon municipality’s accounts in order.
BOOTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sit here humped upon my seat,
The lap iron on my knee,
And liltin'ly the leather beat.
To sew my welts and see
The little pancake world go round
Is life enough for me.
I have no dimpled babe to grab
My long, lean neb and crow ;
I live here like a hermit crab,
Not ever lonely, though,
While there’s a pile of droll old boots
To tell me all they know.
No other heeds ’em, I suppose,
Old boots so dull and dree;
They’d give most men the ’opeless joes,
But some few may agree
How very whimsical at times
A droll old boot can be.
Of well-worn brogues of black and tan
Parade a motley score,
And I'll set out what kind of man
Each moulded blucher bore;
For nought’s so like a fat man as
The boot the fat man wore.
The bright, the dull, the short, the thin,
Each his peculiar shoe,
With wrinkles twisted round a grin,
Or deep-cut lines of rue,
Or prim with straight-laced front to show
How prim his life is, too.
That’s Mossy Blobaum, fat and flat; ;
There gigglin’ Annie goes ;
What man could wear a shoe like that
But preachin’ Brother Roe?
And snub-nose Brown hops after, with
A comic turned-up toe.
But here’s the fairy twain, my son,
Cind’rella couldn’t beat,
a » vuueh each little one
Where joy and beauty meet;
A cheap pair, too, made dearest by
A pair of fair young feet.
Again she’ll dance in by-and-bye,
Brown eyes as brisk as bees.
“Not ready yet, my bird,” says I.
“The fact is, if you please,
It breaks my heart to part with such
Dear, witchin’ shoes as these!”
The width whereon my window looks
My whole wide world computes ;
I’ve only twenty tattered hooks,
But scores of substitoots;
,1 read each day my circulat-
in’ library of boots!
VICTORIA.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
State Accident Insurance Office of
Victoria :
June, Revenue. Outgoings. To Reserves. Reserves.
,1915.. £27,546 £7,837 £19,709 £19,709
1916.. 25,951 17,791 8,160 27,869
But the reserves iu a case of this kind are
not accumulated profits. In the first year,
for instance, as business was only actually
written for seven months, the policies paid
for 12 months had in every case at least
five months to run, and most of them had
more. Put it another way. Suppose the
whole of the policies came into the office on
the day the office was opened, then the
revenue shown above was for 24 months and
the losses were for only 19 months. Accord-
ing to the office reckoning it requires £12,660
of the total of £27,869 to re-insure the unex-
pired risks; and that leaves £15,209 of more
or less genuine reserves, wdiich have been
divided up as follows: Bonus reserve, £5459;
general fund, £9750. That means a profit
of £15,209 on less than two years’ opera-
tions. The office started off with the back-
ing of the State, but no capital. The
Workers’ Compensation Act, 1914, made
industrial insurance obligatory ; and for the
purposes of this law industrial accidents
include industrial diseases. The employer
carries the liability to insure his workpeople.
In case of incapacity the servant is entitled
to half-wages up to 30s. per week, with a
maximum of £5OO. The only people who
are exempt are non-manual workers receiv-
ing more than £250 a year.
Insurance may be effected with approved
private offices; but, insurance being com-
pulsory, the State did the right thing and
provided a means by which employers might
get insurance at cost price. This office is
the result. Gn the experience already
gained, the rates for no less than 134
classes of risk have been reduced; and
the office declares: “The rates now charged
are less than in any other State of the
Commonwealth, except perhaps Queensland.”
In addition, a bonus reserve is being built
up. This, the Insurance Commissioner
hopes, will be distributed to policy-holders
at an early date. The office might find it
profitable to make a feature of these bonuses,
rather than give the policy-holders the
advantage of lower premiums. In life
insurance, at any rate, not one policy-holder
in a dozen troubles about anything but the
size of the bonuses; and not one canvasser
in six talks about the net-cost of insurance,
though, after all, that is what really matters.
Of the outgoings of £17,791 last year
claims ran off with £12,369 and exes, with
£5422. Of the premium income 48.3 p.c.
went to meet claims and 21.1 p.c. to meet
exes.; so. all going well, there is 30 p.c. of
the premiums to cut up between the policy-
holders and the reserve fund. But the
concessions mustn’t be rushed up. As this
paper pointed out a year ago the claims
upon a workers’ insurance fund have a habit
of growing not so much because of malinger-
ing as because when employers have to pay
for insurance they will lay men off for in-
juries which, though small, are nevertheless
some sort of an impediment; and other men
will lay off on their own account.

Voted to Provident fund, £50,000 )
Fortune has favored the—well, call it brave
if you like. In 1913 things looked very
different. The co., with a comparatively
small capital, had plunged into big ships for
the inter-State trade, and as at June 30 of
tiiat year owed no less than £524,679, plus
a small amount on account of unused
tickets. Most of this was pretty dear
money; and, the ships being built ahead of
requirements, it was only under good condi-
tions that they could pay this interest and
'earn something substantial for the co. in
addition. When on top of rather poor trade
conditions in 1913-14 there were cumulative
rises in wages, etc., it looked as though the
Adelaide S.S. Co. had made a mistake. Then
came the war. As soon as that catastrophe
happened the Grantala was requisitioned for
service, the Wandilla a month or two later,
and subsequently the Warilda; and the
Yankalilla is under charter to the Common-
wealth Government for wheat carrying.
During the past year the Grantala, having
been released by the Naval Department, was
sold, along with the Wollowra, Innamincka
and a lighter.
The Innamincka was one of the older
vessels of the fleet, and had been probably
written down to very little ; the others were
comparatively new, yet, of course, bought
before the war. What profit was made on
these sales is not disclosed —probably well
over £lOO,OOO. Anyhow, look at this: —
1910. 1915. 1916.
Liabilities (unused tickets
and sundry creditors) . . £79,514 £362,695 £204,355
Assets (other than steam-
ers, freeholds, etc.) . . 118,503 153,341 262,384
Surplus assets . . £38,989 . £58,029
Excess liabilities. . £209,354 —-
That is to say the co. has improved its
position, from this point of view, by £267,383
during the year, notwithstanding that the
book value of steamers, freeholds, etc., has
been reduced by only £94,780. Or put it
this way : The conversion of assets standing
in the books at £94,780 plus the year’s profit
has added £267,383 to the liquid assets,
after paying dividends during the year. The
accounts put it that the net profit for the
year was £71,531. The total shown in the
table is made up this way
Added to insurance account .. .. £50,000
Added to renewals and replacement fund 55,000
Vote to provident fund . . .. . . 50,000
Profit as per profit and loss account .. 71,531
Total . . .. £226,531
No doubt the bulk of the first three items is
war profit; and whether any provision has
been made for war profit tax isn’t clear. It
may be hidden somewhere. If not, it is
quite possible that out of next year’s profit
a large bite will have to be taken to satisfy
the tax in respect of the past year’s abnor-
mal earnings. This matter is so important
that some reference ought to have been made
to it in the report or accounts.

This paper doesn’t know the nature of that
machinery and plant fund. It may be a
set-off against actual wear and tear, in which
case it isn’t a real reserve and the profits
of which it is composed are not real profits.
Or it may be something extra which a very
prudent management has put by to cover
contingencies, and iu that case it may be
regarded as a fairly genuine reserve. As
the fund is built up by appropriations out
of what are declared to be net profits, it
looks like a reserve to cover unforeseen con-
tingencies, all others being provided for
otherwise.
The co. took over the business very oppor-
tunely—for shareholders, at any rate.
Rubber soon afterwards started to boom as
it had never boomed before; and though,
incidentally, a few people were stung when
the market collapsed, there was no shrink-
age in the use of the goods. On the con-
trary, every month the world seems to find
a new use for rubber; and mostly, though
it proceeds at once to look for a less expen-
sive substitute, it wonders how it could ever
have used anything else. Added to that there
has been a growing inclination to believe
that, after all, rubber goods may not be
really improved by being carted 12,000
miles and baked en route on the Equator.
So, between one thing and another, there
has been an extraordinary growth in this
eo.’s trade. Here are seven years’ figures.
1909. 1914. 1915. iMG.
Capital. . . . £60,000 £l3o,o'O'O £130,000 £140,000
Liabilities .. 15,849 36,644 42,71-
Stock .. .. 35,838 83,960 81,016 111,i64
Freehold, plant, „
etc. .. 24,476 76,599 82,757 89,470
Book debts .. 9,625 28,118 48,940
Other assets . . 839 792 879. J6O
Capital has been increased to times its
old size, and there is another issue now in
progress-—at par, too; and that is a nice
little bonus, seeing that the shares carry an
easy 12 § p.c. and are worth at least 30s. in
the market. It is unlikely in the extreme
that the standard of profits reached during
the past couple of years will be maintained
after the war, when Germany and Austria
will be trading again and British, French
and Russian manufacturers will be looking
for foreign markets once more. But
Perdriaus are on a good commercial line;
they handle articles in which there is a big
repair trade also; they have put themselves
on a sopnd financial basis; they have estab-
lished a valuable goodwill; and their profits
during the past prosperous years have much
exceeded the requirements _ of the 124 p.c.
dividend. In all these circumstances the
shareholders seem to be- justified in looking
for at least a good return even in pretty bad
times.

The advances that have been got in have
thus been kept liquid, which really means
that they have been put into British and
Australian war loans. Though this may
not be much good from a profit-earning
point of view, it makes the position sounder.
Liquid assets now represent very nearly
half the deposits; and in that and other
respects the co. has a reserve of strength
beyond what is generally regarded as a fair
thing. As for the future —who can tell?
But, whatever happens, Tasmania promises
to be an immediate gainer by the determina-
tion to cut Germany out of the metal-smelt-
ing business; and there is a reasonable pro-
bability that one industry will lead on to
another, until old Hobart will hardly know
itself.

The gross earnings have moved up steadily
in spite of the mad Kaiser —from £258.024
in the year preceding the war to £290,555.
But. chiefly through increased taxation,
exes, have rushed ahead too. This co. is
being very unfairly hit by the double income
tax—3s. Sd. in the £ in Maoriland and ss.
in the £ in London : together Bs. Bd. in the
£ of the income, which ought to be nearly
enough to satisfy even those who are yelling
for conscription of wealth.
The rather mixed figures which follow
the first table show that £25,000 was written
off last year’s profits to provide for depre-
ciation of investments. The bank has
£481,575 in “Home, Colonial and Foreign
securities” and £079,609 in British Govern-
ment War Loans and Treasury bills. These
have been written down ; and in most cases
it seems to be simply another method by
which the management may stuff a bit up
its sleeve. It certainly has done a lot in
that way hitherto. Premises, for instance,
had been written down to next to nothing
until some recent purchases were made.
This asset must be worth a couple of hun-
dred thousand more than it stands at in the
books. But even without these internal
reserves the position looks wonderfully
sound. Take these figures first: —
Liabilities.
1906. 1915. 1916.
Notes .. .. £268,872 £465,515 £535,665
Bills, etc. . . 489,908 699,093 841,555
Deposits . . 3,440,456 4,740,397 5,298,296
Assets.
Liquid assets.. 2,171,985 3,262,497 3,553,791
Advances .. 2,509,612 4,021,051 4,499,662
Premises . . 72,099 161,037 170,672
So the bank owes £6,675,516, and its liquid
assets (including public securities) total
£3,553,791. There is thus £3,121,725 of
debt not represented by liquid assets, and
to meet it there is £4,499,662 of securities
and premises, property, etc., written down
to £170,672. Depositors in a bank or any
other concern which lends on the security of
Maoriland land values ought to require a
wide margin, because those land values
have been most violently inflated. They
have here all the margin that can reason-
ably be asked for.
As for the shareholders, this paper isn’t
xo confident about their future. Maoriland
is booming on war profits. Looking over
the Dominion’s trade figures the chairman
of this bank told shareholders the other day
that “the export of wool shows a consider-
able decrease in weight—nearly 25 p.c.—
but there is an increase of 12 p.c. in value.”
The total trade has run up to £49 per head
of the population per annum—larger figures
than perhaps any other country in the world
ever reached. Of course the extent of the
trade is partly due to the awful tendency
of the people to let somebody else make its
hats and its pipes and its beds. But it is
largely due to the abnormal prices of wool,
meat and dairy produce; and when they
crash, as crash they will after the war,
Maoriland’s prosperity is going to slump.
And that is when the war bill will have to
be paid. “It is evident,” the chairman told
his shareholders, “that you must in the near
future contemplate either the payment of
your dividends subject to income tax” —
which means a difference of 2 or 3 p.c.—
“or you must accept a lower dividend, or
possibly both.”
