Or, if you like, you can make the profit for
the previous year £28.585 and for the past
year £30,469 —it depends upon the year in
which you charge a bonus paid to employees.
This bonus was declared payable out of
the 1915 profits, but it was not actually
paid until the year just past. There was
no similar bonus for this last year. Pro-
bably the directors weren’t feeling quite so
affable, on account of a strike at the mill,
which hung operations up for a month.
Of course it's the war that has been
mainly responsible for the extraordinary
vigor of the profits:—
Orders for military uses have engaged the co.’s
mills and clothing factories to a larger extent than
in the preceding year; so much so, in fact, as prac-
tically to prevent any manufacture for the domestic
trade. In consequence the directors had to meet
demands, where possible, by increased importation—
a branch of the business showing much development
during the 12 months, and now forming an important
department of the co.’s trade.
The co. has thus benefited both ways; it has
had its mills working full speed (except
for the strike) for new customers, and it
has more or less held the old customers by
going temporarily into the import trade.

* * * *
According to the British Australasian,
Yorkshire mills have taken up the marine
fibre of which South Aus. has an un-
limited supply in Spencer’s Gulf. The Hun
used to be the only person who knew how
to mix it with wool, but now Bradford can
make clothes out of a half-and-half blend.
A co. was formed in Sydney and one or two
elsewhere to dredge for the stuff; but up to
now they have all had a shocking time.
One direct result of the recent ill-timed
miners’ strike at Kalgoorlie was the almost
total shut-down of Oroya Links. Previous
to the war the property was worked at a
profit, but since costs ran up, owing to the
war, operations have been carried on at a
loss. When the strike caused a stoppage,
directors determined not to restart, for the
time being, at least. There are others in
much the same position; and their recent
history ought to interest Treasurer Higgs
when he is considering his War-time Profits
measure and its relation to gold-mines.
* * * *
__ Most of the August returns from the
Kalgoorlie mines show a falling off as a
result of the alien labor dispute. South
Ivalgurli was probably affected most by the
stoppage, and though costs were kept down
expenses exceeded revenue by nearly £lOOO.
Ivalgurli, which was about the only big mine
undisturbed, earned its usual profit. A
report just sent to London by the general
manager extends the life of the mine another
year or so.
* * * *
Associated Gold Mines (W.A.) share-
holders met in London last month and were
given a little more information about the
co.’s ventures. At Kalgoorlie the ore treated
last year was 2s. per ton better than for the
previous 12 months, but dear supplies, etc.,
increased costs by Is. The ore now being
treated is of still better grade; though the
mine’s resources may give out at any time.
The chairman stated that the option on the
now idle Iveeley mine, in Canada, had been
extended un*il six months after the war,
and as silver was then expected to rise the
Board reckoned it had made a good deal.
Ore containing 300,0000 z. of metal was
declared to be in sight, and about one-third
o!: this quantity was said to be contained
in bagged stuff worth IOOOoz. per ton.
Developments in the North Thompson gold
mine at Porcupine (Canada) being still
good, a co. to take over the show was regis-
tered in Ontario, and production is expected
to start in 1917. Outlay on this venture
totals £41,500, of which £25,000 is to be
recouped by a further allotment of shares.
On March 31 Associated’s net cash assets
stood at £32,000, and on paper the co.’s
specs look as if they will require a lot of
financing.
Linden, on the East Mt. Margaret field
(W.A.), now has four Melbourne syndicates
sampling its wares. One is on the Devon,
where a level is being driven at 100 ft.;
another has some oO tons of “good grade”
ore dumped on the surface, and has until
November to decide whether it will complete
the deal. Options are also held on Lowe’s
claim and the Grant Junction.
The miracle of raising the dead is about
to be attempted by new Westralian Minister
for Mines Robinson. The achievements of
old-time gold producers now idle are to be
published in the local newspapers and circu-
larised in the Eastern States. By this
puffing he expects new capital to rush to
the assistance of the defunct. But what is
needed most is new mines, and those on the
look out for them are meeting with very
little success.
Southern Cross (W.A.) is again stagnat-
ing. Fraser’s Group is waterlogged, and
most of the surrounding leases are having
a dismal time. Bullant Mclntyre and others
who can’t get out still cling to their belief
that enormous wealth lies underfoot, and
contend that if capital could be induced to
forget past failures, Southern Cross could
again be made to boom.
A little gold is being got at Westralia’s
latest find near Mt. Monger. Most of the
returns have so far come from small leaders,
but big lode formations are now being tried,
and one or two are said to give fair pro-
spects.
* * * *
For the first time since he took up the
Mararoa, Norseman (W.A.), Bill Hender-
son declines to give an estimate of ore
reserves. Six months ago the total was
10,000 tons; yet he has since scraped out
16,000 tons and earned a working profit of
nearly £BOOO. The additional 6000 tons
must have been odds and ends from the
Hardy Norseman lease, together with the
rock broken out of the Nos. 5 and 6 levels
north and the No. 8 level south in Mararoa
itself. The co. had on June 30 over £lO,OOO
in hard cash, after providing for all lia-
bilities. The profits from subsequent crush-
ings have more than paid the 6d. div. in
August, and there must be now about
£12,000 in the till. This would give 2s. 6d.
cash, while another shilling is available
from the ore unstoped. A big reef shows
at No. 8 south, and any change there would
set the market agog. But Henderson can’t
build on this chance, for all Norseman mines
are poor at 1000 ft.
* * * *
From Cloncurry:—
Though not much has appeared in the press about
it for the last two years or so, the Mount Elliott
people have been steadily preparing’ for a big for-
ward movement by purchase of controlling interests in
other groups of mines and spending large sums of
money in development work; and the concern gives
every promise of soon standing once more in the
front rank of copper producers in Australia. The
Elliott group now embraces the co.’s own mines,
the Queensland Exploration mines, the Mount Oxide
mines, and the Queensland Copper Freeholds mines.
When the British Treasury regulations permit, the
approximate position will be a co. of 370,000 shares
of £5 each, of which 90,000 will be held in
reserve. Meantime the shares in the component
groups as represented by their equivalents in Mount
Elliott, Ltd., rank alike for dividends.
The original Mount Elliott is now bare of high-
grade ore in the upper levels; but below the 500 ft.
level a winze showed patches of high-grade ore and
above this level there is over 1,000,000 tons of 3 p.c.
ore. The Consols mine (at Hampden) at the
480 ft. level shows a body about 18ft. wide with a
value of 5 to 10 p.c. copper and some hundreds of
thousands of.tons of low-grade ore above this level.
At the Australia mine (Cloncurry) the 300 ft. level
shows a width of from 20ft. to 90ft., which will
average about 3 p.c. copper. This ore is high in
lime and magnesia, and, though it would be of no
value by itself, still in conjunction with ores from
the other mines it will pay to work even under
present conditions.
At the Dobbin the lode in the 200 ft. level is
about 40ft. wide, of which 10ft. goes from 8 to 12
p.c. copper, and the crosscut at the 350 ft. level will
cut the lode within the next 20ft. The Lady mines
(Ballara) show a long’ line of lode averaging from 5
to 8 p.c. of copper over a good width. Block 14 (Clon-
curry) has a very wide lode formation, and the cross-
cut at the 200 ft. level will intersect the lode in
the course of a few weeks. The Argylla shows a
lode channel over 100 ft. wide, carrying very good
ore. The Solomon is a larg’e body of lime averaging
4 p.c. copper. The Mount Oxide shows a fair ton-
nage averaging 20 p.c. copper and a great tonnage
of low-grade ore. The Chinaman mine has a wide
body of quartz low in copper but with patches of
high-grade ore. The quartz will be utilised in the
converting of the matte to blister, and this makes
it of great value. About a dozen other mines belong-
ing to the different groups are in course of develop-
ment.
llie co. realises that it must be in a position to
obtain a tonnage of ore and be able to draw from
various sources to blend for smelting. Also, in
order to warrant the erection later on of an up-to-
date plant to handle the low-grade ores, a vast ton-
nage must be assured. Works are being constructed
at Bowen for the refining of the blister, and the
coalfields there are going to make it possible to
profitably treat the huge masses of low-grade copper
ore in Cloncurry, thus providing employment for a
large population. The management of the co.’s
affairs, it is generally admitted, is in very com-
petent hands, and with the new smelters going in
January, shareholders should once more g-et a fair
return for their money, with the prospect of a steady
increase as the development of the different proper-
ties progresses and it is successfully demonstrated
that the low-grade ores' can be profitably treated. In
its determination to show that this can be done, the
co. deserves well of Australia.
* *
Brilliant Block, Charters Towers, is
attempting reconstruction to provide fresh
capital. It is proposed to have a nominal
capital of £70,000 in shares of 10s. each;
40,000 paid shares to be issued in payment
for the property, and 100,000 contribs. to
carry a. liability of 2s. 6d. per share. There
is a mill and cyanide plant, but not much
payable ore available, and it is doubtful
whether £12,500 will go far enough.
Fred H. Bathurst writes: —
Not to me, but to dead geologist Stirling, solicitor
Croker, the Baillieus, Stamp, Geo. Nicholson, and
last but not least G. D. Meudell, give credit for
forcing forward the possibilities of our Victorian
brown coal. The big step was taken when the
Baillieus got the best European talent to advise as
to the extent of the deposits and the scientific way
to handle them. So long as the State retained the
right to purchase, it was a blunder not to have encour-
aged the firm to work the measures. All I have
sought to do on the New Industries Committee is to
direct attention to the dormant resources of the
formations, and to put before the members the in-
formation that the north-west coast of Canada is
setting out to create wealth from vastly inferior
deposits of brown coal.
Cassilis, Gippsland (Vic.), has to bow to
the inevitable after struggling along without
a div. since 1907. The lack of cash to pro-
vide cheap power first put the brake hard on.
And when, the plant was secured a record
drought kept the mountain streams dry, and
by the time the trickles had started again
the fountain of calls was parched. Cas-
silis ore was tough stuff to treat, so though
110,000 tons were crushed for 93,0000 z. gold,
the co. paid only £16,000 in divs. The machin-
ery man will have a picnic over the sale
unless someone has the grit to take the show
over on liquidation terms and give it a run
on bedrock lines. There are reefs parallel
to the Cassilis, and the rich little Ceresa
vein should certainly be tested before the
final whistle blows.
Ajax North, Daylesford (Vic.), closed its
books in September with no liabilities and
assets worth £18,746, including uncalled capi-
tal, £15,750, and plant, £2OOO. Uncalled
capital in a no-liability co. is a very doubtful
prop on which to rest, and plant is worth
only just what the machinery man will bid.
More tangible are ore reserves, but neither
George Buchanan nor L. Hansen, who runs
the mine, go into that subject. Nothing
worth much has shown up above 500 ft.. and
of late Hansen has been hustling out quartz
mostly from stopes in the No. 8 level work-
ings. His big effort has been to drop his
shaft from 800 to 1000 feet, and now he is
there he will start out to test the different
reefs west. Besides, there is a good chance
of scoring in the upper levels in the western
country. In No. 5 level to the north-west
he has a promising formation on the McMil-
lan line. The co. is spending at the rate of
7s. 6d. per ton on development, against other
costs of 14s. per ton, which shows that Han-
sen is being stinted on progressive work.
The co. so far has paid 9s. per share in
divs. on a trifling capital, and still has a big
claim to develop.
According to Edna May Central half-yearly
report just out N. E. Giblin regards “gen-
eral prospects as favorable,” and also
“further possibilities of the mine (on Sept.
11) impress him considerably.” Giblin is
check-sampling everything anil expects to be
able to carry out vigorous development whilst
maintaining output and values;- To June 30
48,327 tons have given £53,421. Costs, in-
cluding depreciation, were 20s. 2d. last half,
compared with 19s. 7d. the previous term.
It is clear that if Giblin can’t lift the values
well above the mine average since crushing
was started, Edna Central won’t travel far
on the road to tall profit-making. The need
for richer stone comes with Giblin’s adop-
tion of the Board’s policy of shaft sinking
and big crosscutting, which rapidly absorbs
funds. Everything considered, it would
have been better to have passed the last
div. till piblin’s estimate of the worth of the
ore in sight and its tonnage had been ascer-
tained. The New reef bumped up the average
during the six months from 20s. to 30s. on
a yield of 17,115 tons, compared with 13,392
tons for the previous half-year. But the
nigger on the fence is the drop in the grade
of the last three crushings from this
reef. Ex-manager Dick Williams has his
final say in the report, and to his credit he
is as cheery about the Central becoming one
of the biggest mines at Westonia as though
lie had not been unpleasantly jolted out of
his job.

Three zinc processes are hatching in Tas-
mania. The Amalgamated Zinc (De
Bavay’s) expects to use the Anaconda pro-
cess at works which will shortly be estab-
lished near Hobart; J. 11. Gillies will use
his own process at North-West Bay; and a
local syndicate has just completed an experi-
mental plant in Hobart. The two former
are electrolytic, and as Gillies has made all
his experiments on local ores he should have
a pull oyer the Anaconda process. The
third, which has been kept quiet, is said to
be a chemical process.
* * » »
The Tasmania,n Smelting Co.’s works at
Zeehan (Tas.) whU probably be sold shortly
at the request of the Government, and Mt.
Lyell is likely to purchase. In 1912 the
Government lent the co. £20,000 on condi-
tion that the latter raised £22,000. The
Deutsche Bank, London branch, advanced
this sum. Debentures, ranking equally, were
issued to both parties and constituted a first
mortgage over the assets of the co. As
assets are not clearly defined, it is doubtful
whether the slag dump is included. These
contain from 15 p.c. to 20 p.c. of zinc, and
will be valuable when a zinc process is estab-
lished. As the Hun will need the metal
after the war, the German Bank will pro-
bably put up a hard fight to get them.

“Paddymelon” : Tllere is talk in Brisbane of
a line from the Clermont coalfield to Charters
Towers to provide cheap fuel for that very
sick goldfield. Over 20 years ago I suggested
to Bob Pliilp that the Government should
generate electric power on the Bowen coal-
field and transmit current to liavenswood, the
Towers, Bowen, Townsville and several
other fields thereabouts. Of course, Bobby
was agin it; it smelt too Socialistic. What
about the present Government tackling the
scheme? Cheap power with one up-to-date
mill would give the Towers a new lease of
life.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
GAS IN THE STOMACH IS
DANGEROUS.
PHYSICIANS RECOMMEND THE USE
OF MAGNESIA.
Sufferers from ' indigestion or dyspepsia
should remember that the presence of gas or
wind in the stomach invariably indicates that
the stomach is troubled by excessive acidity.
This acid causes the food to ferment and
the fermenting food in turn gives rise to
noxious gases which distend the stomach,
hamper the normal functions of vital internal
organs, cause acute headaches, inteijfere with
the action of the heart, and charge the blood
stream with deadly poisons, which in time
must ruin the health. Physicians say that
to quickly dispel a dangerous accumulation
of. wind in the stomach and to stop the food
fermentation which creates the gas, the acid
in the stomach must be neutralised, and that
for this purpose there is nothing quite so good
as half a teaspoonful of bisurated magnesia
taken in a little water immediately after
meals. This instantly neutralises the acid,
thus stopping fermentation and the forma-
tion of gas, and enables the inflamed, dis-
tended stomach to proceed with its work
under natural conditions. Bisurated magnesia
is obtainable in powder or tablet form from
any chemist; but as there are many different
forms of magnesia it is important that the
"bisurated which the physicians prescribe
should be distinctly asked for. — Apvt.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 25, 1916.
My Dear Arini. —
Spring fashions are showing themselves,
if somewhat abashed by the tremendous times.
In the greatest crisis of the Big Gun, we
get delicate garlands of flowers on the sum-
mer silks. Hats reply in similar floral strain.
The pancake millinery, with posies stamped
in full bloom across crown and brim, are
supposed to do away with trimming. A
rosette or a twist of silk, perhaps. But
there’s no cheeseparing in the scheme. The
fine tagel straws, which have a bouquet of
garden flowers in their design, are lovely
fancies. Prices correspond.
Little Florence Scapini was too ill to play
at the French League concert, so Cyril
Monk scraped the ice off with a serious com-
position for the fiddle. Pauline Bindley’s
soprano was pleasing in everything but its
incursions into French. Ballads in English
will for some time to come be Pauline’s
best advertising line. Truman added the
organ’s blast to Dufault’s fiery “Marseil-
laise.” But the remote organ pealed coldly
to time, while Paul marched on as the
spirit moved him. “Eet is ’orrible!” said
an adjacent Frenchman, and it was. The
foreign Consuls, led by Cha.vet, were in
prominence, and Premier Holman —president
of the French League—had his feet on the
blue Axminster. French Leaguers, with
their faces puckered up in smiles (think
what the cheque will buy for little orphan
Jeanette!), sat up very straight on front
chairs. Manager Freddie Shipman and his
small commanding-officer joyously led the
applause.
A fete to help St. Martha’s Home at
Leichhardt was opened by Mrs. Holman on
Saturday. Martha is troubled about many
things, but chiefly orphan girls. So the
gentle grey-hooded nuns gather them in and
teach them the way of a broom or the tricks
of a needle. Limerick lace, fine as a mantle
of dew, falls from the older girls’ expert
fingers. But that’s an extra. Stitchery of
a more mundane kind, that helps them later
to good billets, is Martha’s leading line. In
a big marquee tent the wares are still selling
fast, and the cash goes towards the feeding
of the orphan.
St. Martha’s Home, by the way—there
are 78 girls in residence, the baby being
a motherless mite of four —is part of the
early history of Sydney. In the long ago a
good citizen named Norton built a mansion
in the Early Colonial style. That is to
say, he modelled it on those great airy houses
with wide verandahs which English cotton-
planters once built in old Kentucky. You
could drive a four-in-hand into the hall and
leave room for a grocer’s cart to pass. The
rooms upstairs make ideal dormitories. The
long-dead Norton’s estate included an
immense orchard, where now there are rows
of dusty little terraces; and he used to have
fruit and vegetables gathered every day and
placed where his poor neighbors might help
themselves. Through gates that were never
locked visitors could walk into the beauti-
ful grounds. Miles of brick and mortar now
replace the shade trees, and the house has
been for 30 years in the hands of charity
organisations. It’s an object-lesson to the
modern architect who builds a collection of
gables and cells and calls it a home.
Governor Strickland has gone over the
Murray to eat a chop with brother Stanley.
Lady Edeline takes a two-days’ trip to the
South Coast, and the races start on Sep-
tember 30. Some people are buying glad
rags, and others ? Well, read the
casualty-lists.
General Haig has given his first official
praise to Australia’s army, and it is the
Division that gets it—for great gallantry
and other good qualities. The remark-
able thing about the Division is
that it has hardly a single Comforts Fund
or committee to its name. The artillery
brigades, for instance, were formed in
Egypt, of Australians raked out of other
brigades, strengthened by reinforcements that
weren’t wanted by other arms. So the boys
who are the first to catch Haig’s eye, and
also to get cheers from General Blumer, have
no Susies knitting socks and making other
comforts for them in the Australia that
owes them three cheers, too. It only illus-
trates again how badly this battalion and
brigade comforts work wants reorganising.
Brigadier-Gen. Jack Antill, of the Light
Horse, has his heart’s desire. After months
of Gallipoli, followed by more than enough of
Egypt, with the Romani fight to liven things
up, the man from Picton—long, lean and
tough as six feet of iron nails —is sampling
the slop of France.
Dr. Moran, the man with a pre-war repu-
tation in the muddied football field, addressed
the Red Cross executive and sundries the
other afternoon. He was late, so a feminine
orator gave some of her notions while a lady
scout rushed into the highways to find the
lecturer and lead him in, the club rooms
being out of the way and easily overlooked.
She landed her quarry safely; and then the
man from Balmain and Mesopotamia told the
sorry tale of the expedition. He didn’t say
so, but one or two bigwigs have been sent
into obscurity over the hideous bungle—a
merely minor satisfaction. Surgeon Moran
shows the effects of his share of short rations
in fever-haunted swamps. The miracle is
that anyone lives to tell the tale.
To help the Italian Red Cross rally the
Art Society lent its own loan premises the
other night. These are the long galleries
with crushed-mulberry drapery under the
Education Palace’s skylight. So signors
and signoras came along, the latter with a
bit of native style in the form of fancy
scarves over ropes of coal-black hair. Cap-
pelli sang his sweetest to thunders of
“Brava!” Of course his photo, was auc-
tioned. The sport who ran the price up to
£7 said “I’ll make it £lO if the signor will
give us another song!” Maurico immedi-
ately obliged. This done, his face was
handed back to him; whereon he gallantly
presented it to the star amateur of the even-
ing—Miss Goldrick, a youthful pupil of Di
Giorgia, whose voice he admired in Italian
superlatives.
A few years ago a charming ’Murkan
lady, Mrs. John McVey, spent some months
at the Australia and made a host of
friends in Sydney. An only daughter hav-
ing just married, the young-looking mother
(a widow, by the way) set out on a world
tour. In this town Mrs. McVey, formerly
Miss Hopkins, of Ohio, discovered a rela-
tive in “Hop,” who now gets news that
his handsome relative died in Connecticut the
other day, after a short illness.
Do you remember the old Nelson, her
Majesty’s corvette, or something equally
harmless? There’s a newly-decorated
admiral, who used to be a loo’tenant on
that ark. Australian matrons will recall
the dashing young sailor of the early ’9o’s.
Now he is Rear-Admiral Christian and was
lately handed a C.B.
The Cullen lady is presiding genius at a
Battalion Comforts gathering in the Royal
Society’s room on September 28. Leslie Hol-
land and the ever-willing basso Charlie
Smythe head the list of entertainers.
This photo shows
you a strenuous
woman with the
versatile gifts of
the true daughter
of Australia. Mrs.
Kettlewell, known
to South Aus-
tralians as Agnes
L. Storrie, writes
flowing verse, is a
journalist w i t h a
literary style, and
makes little gar-
ments for destitute
Belgian babes as
deftly as she turns
a sonnet or tosses
a pancake. With
the invaluable
assistance of J. H.
Maiden, the director of Sydney Botanic
Gardens, she founded the N. S. Wales
Wattle League in 1909. Twenty years ago
there was a small Adelaide coterie which
tried to push the yellow fluff as Australia’s
national emblem. But it had fainted away
long before the now virile N.S.W. League
was born.
As you pass our new Commonwealth
Bank look up. You will see the Australian
coat of arms, with the wattle in one corner.
This, our first —or almost the first—national
tribute to any native bloom, lifts the golden
tassels to the place occupied in Ireland by
the shamrock. The tribute is entirely due to
the representations made by the N.S.W.
Wattle League to the Commonwealth
authorities.
Speaking in London at the formation of a
Wattle branch there, in 1913, Airs. Kettlewell
made an impassioned appeal to Australians
in England to fan the breath of patriotic
love for their native land by an annual fes-
tival of the gorgeous bloom. With other
enthusiasts she got permission from the
British Admiralty to trim the then baby,
H.M.A.S. Australia, with garlands of it for
the King’s first visit to the ship.
Battalion committee dames are wrinkling
their foreheads for new ideas —not chest-
nuts wrapped in bonbon papers. Here’s
the latest scheme. At the Tom Raines’
garden fete in October a well-known Syd-
ney man has volunteered to do some kite-
flying. No, not Holman. For the present
his name is Mystery. He is experimenting
in some secret fastness, with miles of string,
cardboard and gum. Messages to Mars will
draw the cash.

ago The Bulletin
found out that a
girl with a voice
came from Alice
Springs, in the
heart of Australia •
and it gave her a
hand up the ladder
mostly because it
wanted people to
see that the heart
of Australia wasn't
all dry sand. Lu-
cille Benstead she
was, and after her
Sydney Town Hall
concert she went on
to London. Every
report since she got
there has been en-
couragmg, ana now she sends her love to
Australia and her photo, along with the
cheerful remark that she’s “full of engage-
ments till 1920.”
Aide Boulton, the New Boy, is seeing the
inside of more factories and freezing machines
than are known to most lads of 19. In a
recent sub-regal tour there was one bright
spot. That was the pilgrimage to the pic-
turesque mill where they make paper-board,
etc., embosomed in a leafy arm of Upper Lane
Cove River. No, his Ex. didn’t bring away
a sample of the dividends. They haven’t
made any yet.
A Melbourne girl, Grade Mates, is to be
married to big Bob Maddrell, of the Braid-
wood family, on October 11. It’s to happen
in Sydney.
About a few busy Australians from one
of them: —
I’ve just got back from a fortnight in Cornwall,
which is the nearest thing to Sydney I have found
since I left home. Splendid surf-bathing, white beaches
and rugged rocks, riddled with smugglers’ caves,
running, they say, three or four miles inland. But
I didn’t strike any, though I dug my toe into the
sand as hard as I could.
I was staying with some Sydney people—Violet
Elwell (once ’cellist in the Philharmonic orchestra),
whose husband, A. Elwell Sutton, R.N., has just
written a book on “Humanity v. Unhumanity,” a
philosophical treatise on the German Idea.
We were entertained the other evening at a pri-
vate reading of Miles Franklin’s play. I hope it is
staged over here; the Australian boys would come
battalions strong, and my corps would turn up
cn masse, as there’s a delightful W.V.A. in it.
My corps is commonly known as “Kitchener’s
Lizzies,” as we do all the dirty w r ork at hospitals,
canteens, etc. We entirely staff Peel House, the
accommodation-place for Overseas Forces —400
Eunice Mort (now Graham) makes beds there; also
Alice Treekman, whose brother has just been killed,
first day of the Big Push. Lydia Iloare is in the
corps, also Alice May Cadogan, who shone for a
while in the musical firmament. All Sydney girls
these. And I wish we had a few more of them.
Mrs. McLeod, a daughter of old-time
Brofessor Tate, of Adelaide, is over here on
a visit. She is camping with brother
Ralph, of the Botanic Gardens. Father
was Ralph, too; and though he specialised
in rocks he also knew a tree when he saw
it, and did a lot to make orange-growing
in S. Aus. the important business it has
now become.
The Queen Victoria Club ladies looked as
if someone had slapped their faces the other
afternoon. All that had really happened was
that Mrs. Laura Luffman had read “Dam-
aged Goods” in her best style. One hushed
voice made a final suggestion that it would
be better if the Thing were read to men
only. Heaven knows what kind of audience
will enjoy it as a play !
Still another new soprano who goes to
London. She’s a sweet-voiced young night-
ingale, Rose Alba, who holds one of the chief
scholarships in Sydney Conservatorium. To
help her there’s to be a concert in the Con-
servatorium on October 14. John Brown-
low is slogging at the details.
Hasn’t the war sharpened up feminine
wits? At a recent meeting at Burwood the
local soldiers’ Christmas fund committee
passed a vote of thanks, or something simi-
lar, for a kind donation of shaving paper!
The pretty brown-eyed Ailsa Craig, who
was so helpful as hon. sec. to the A.S.C.
committee, goes to England with her parents
in October. There’s a son of the household
at the Front, and the family hanker to send
him a coo-ee across the Channel.
The “A.8.C.” Bank fed its ever-so-polite
secretary, William Reid, at the Carlton last
week (of course it wasn’t eating all the
week) after 50 years’ service. And it wasn’t
always the Australian Bank of Commerce,
either.' When Mr. Reid first smudged its
ink on his shirt-cuff it was the A.J.S. Bank
and it kept the overdrafts in a house in
George-street wherein big Geoi’ge Dibbs had
just previously hung up his hat and waited
for Miss Robey to say “Yes.” But that
wasn’t its earliest office either. It was born,
the Oldest Inhabitant tells me, where par-
son Richard Johnson had Sydney’s first
orchard. Blooms still grow there; but they
are grog-blooms now. The Empire Hotel
is on part of it.
The Art Society, which puts up its Exhi-
bition shutters on Saturday, records an
advance on last year’s sales. In the black-
and-white section Eirene Mort sold an inter-
esting sketch of old Government House,
Windsor. There is a big tree dying on the
ground in front, and most of the roof is like
a sieve.
Madge Fabian blows like dainty thistle-
down through the Criterion play, “Romance.”
As the lively Italian star Cavallini, her
lovely inky curls, shining like black satin,
are the last word in the wigmakef’s art.
But no tradesman made the exquisite little
willow-wand figure that floats across the
mid-Victorian drawing-room. Madge, though
attached to fluffy white, gets every ounce of
effect out of the old-fashioned black velvet
gown with a tight bodice. But then she
shakes those raven curls over her pearl-
white face and has a way wid her!
Another happy return to life from the
list of the killed. Maoriland airman James
P. Dennistoun, lately reported dead, is now
known to be a prisoner. He was brought
down by a Fokker, had to undergo a very
severe operation, was “treated very kindly
and skilfully” by the Germans, and at latest
was racing along the road to Recovery.
A Melbourne man, Harold Hunt, met
Eileen Collins —lately of the Julius Knight
co. —at St. Mary’s altar the other afternoon,
and took her away to look after his buttons.
Eileen comes from Gisborne, in Maoriland.
The British Red Cross is said to be on
the track of more sturdy Australian
V.A.D.’s; but there are no details as yet.

lass, and adds two years’ training in Prince

Father came down from Bowral to see his
girl run up the gangway.
Lola Playfair, a daughter of the late Tom.
is engaged to Lieut. Joseph Vachon, of the

though it may be too proud to fight. Grand-

Mrs. J. Swan, who died the other day after
about a year’s married life, used to be
Violet Wickham. Grandfather was the naval
Wickham, a one-time Lieut.-Governor of
Queensland, whose portrait hangs in the
Sydney Pioneers’ Club.
Ambrose Gaffney is in Sydney from Mel-
bourne for the joyous purpose of marrying
Miss Winifred Cotter, of Auckland, M.L.
The damsel is staying at the Australia, and
the wedding occurs quietly at once.
Most Sydney folk remember the beautiful
Mrs. Willie Mitchell. Her bellehood raged
for some years up to the end of the nine-
teenth century. (After all, this twentieth
century is only 16 years old.) She, by
widowhoods, acquired three husbands and
lives happily in India. But what I meant
to tell you was of the (1) tragedy and (z)
good fortune of her daughter, Isabel Mitchell,
who went to London some months ago to be
married to a military somebody. Death in
battle anticipated the bride-elect. But the
income from £40,000 had been secured to the
girl by her fiance’s will. All the same, it
is a very broken-hearted maid who takes the
bequest.
Miss Emily Cox has been elected president
of the Women’s Committee of the Prison
Aid Association, vice Mrs. Plomley, who has
a year’s leave of absence to go within visit-
ing distance of her son fighting in muddy
Flanders. Mrs. L. Edgley will pro tem.
take Mrs. Plomley’s place on the National
Council of Women.
The Women’s Horticultural Society has a
new depot in Victoria Arcade. As gifts
roll in fresh eggs, flowers and homemade
jams are sold at bedrock prices for the
Cause. Mrs. Bertram Stevens is hon. shop-
lady till a permanent manageress is secured.
Our Western Suburbs Hospital has just
had a huge egg-fall. Adjacent hamlets,
Croydon, Ashfield, etc., collected the fruit by
the hundred. And great was the joy therein.
The war nips our sick poor of the little
luxuries of peace-time. So the recent raid
on local nests was a godsend. The good-
natured Peters family, of Mount Joy, the
place where the big Belgian carnival raked
in such a heap of money, conspired together
with a team of adjacent mayoresses.
Recent Maoriland arrivals include the
squatting Willie Ritchie pair from Albury,
S. Canterbury. Mrs. Ritchie used to be Miss
Dibbs, of Graythwaite. Her husband’s
brother, popular Jock Ritchie, was lately
killed at the Front, and a younger member
is badly wounded. That one was best man at
brother Jock’s recent wedding in London,
and later on annexed the chief maid thereat.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, September 25, 1916.
My Dear Yaruie, —
Dicky Nevill, writing from Canada to
Melbourne friends, tells them of the accident
which resulted in a broken thigh. He
slipped on the railway station and fell
heavily, but at the time thought he was
only bruised. However, on returning to
Government House, the Duke of Connaught
insisted on the doctor being called and the
damage discovered. Five weeks later the
plaster of Paris was off and the long patient
was on crutches.
Mrs. Percy Russell surrendered her job
as Hawthorn mayoress with a final rattle of
trays. The Red Cross Kitchen in the
Domain was initiated by her, ana last week
she made over the useful going concern to
Lady Helen, Senator Pearce supplying a
speech of acknowledgment. The job of this
central Domain Red Cross Kitchen is sup-
plying luxuries for invalid soldiers in all
the military hospitals. Consignments of
all sorts of perishable delicacies are brought
to the centre several times a day, and some
Sassiety girls call as regularly as the milk-o
with dainties they have cooked an hour or
so before; and then the merely Marys of
the Kitchen lend their motor-cars to distri-
bute the supplies. Plawthorn rightly feels
proud of it all —and of Mrs. Russell.
The latest oversea news of heiress Marie
Hall, of Bendigo, is that she is pining for
her fiance, who is in France, and filling
in her spare time on a Thames houseboat.
The Jim Watsons of Brighton are greatly
distressed by the latest news of son-and-lieir
Eric, who is reported seriously wounded. His
little bride-sister still has her soldier-bride-
groom with her. He was detailed for work
at Duntroon, and that postpones the fare-
wells.
The wife of the President of France,
patroness of the Society for Helping Mothers
and Babes, sends messages of thanks to the
Australian people for their help. The
acknowledgments arrive through Mrs.
Crivelli, president of the society here.
Major Charlie Mason has left for Eng-
land on his way to the ditches. He was
badly wounded in the Gallipoli landing, but
finished a good recovery here within the
last four months, and has been filling in his
later convalescence training other Austra-
lians for the job. Mrs. Charlie is waiting
in London for him.
At Cliveden on Wednesday Mrs. Loibner
had a large party at her flat to say good-
bye to some soldier boys, returning to battle
and all the rest of it after recovering from
their first helpings. Lieuts. Palastra and
Cartwright and Trooper Young were the
heroes of the afternoon, and two dimpled
little nurses from the Base were among the
guests. Lesley Madden came in her best
hat, built of tulle and shaped like a busby.
Muriel North, the Bigjohn Forrest niece,
looked captivating in forget-me-not blue mus-
lin and a muslin fichu, and apologised for
Auntie Forrest, who had stayed at home to
comfort her husband in a bereavement.
Captain Hilliard, in mufti, and his handsome
lady, in black velvet, were there. The Cap-
tain made speeches over the teacups and
presented Lieut. Palastra with an onyx ring
on behalf of the Leibners.
The Como Armytages have parted with
their little farm at Beaconsfield, where they
have been w.ont to week-end for many years.
The town house will not yawn so emptily
now that there are two unmarried sisters
with brother Harold to squeeze in. Cousin
Fred Fairbairn’s new white palace across
the road is calling loudly for a tenant and
not getting him. It’s a sad year for big
houses.
Red Cross Commissioners Norman
Brookes, F. Fairbairn, Heyward and J.
Murdoch have soared from civilians to
the honorary rank of lieut.-colonels and
are attached to General Ilowse’s medical
department. Now what about the Y.M.C.A.
men? The postal people have their ranks
and uniforms already. Oh, no, not merely
the postmen at the Front. There are people
who do letter-sorting or something of that
kind amid the perils of the city G.P.O. who
go in and out to their daily labor in mili-
tary uniform, badges and all!
Adelaide’s giddy whirl: —
This State has another D.C.M. and an interest-in
a Military Cross. The D.C.M. has gone to Lieut.
Cuthbertson, who was at Gallipoli before he was
wounded in France, arid before that was an accountant
and well-known athlete. The Military Cross has been
annexed by Major R. M. Williams, who is attached
to a British regiment. He is an ex-St. Peter’s boy.
The Howard Davenports are off to England, which
is sad news for the Red Cross Society, whose
treasurer he has been. Another good helper, Walter
Smith, of Angaston, has gone to Egypt, in the hope
of seeing a son who was wounded at Romani.
Poor old Michael Angelo! Millicent, a townlet
in the South-East, has refused to hang in the vesti-
bule of its institute a photograph of Michael’s
“David,” and the horror is to be relegated to the
obscurity of the subscribers’ reading-room. Mean-
while the jetty of Millicent’s nearest port, which
takes a good deal of the township’s trade, is in such
a rickety condition that coastal boats are boycotting
it. This makes things square.
If J. A. Southwood can get his way we shall have
another soldier M.P. Quartermaster of the 74th
Regiment as well as Labor member and journalist,
he has applied to his commanding-officer (who is
Major Smeaton, and also an M.P.) for a job on active
service.
News has come from London of the death of G. A.
Tomkinson, the eldest son of the late Samuel Tom-
kinson, the old M.L.O. who used to object to every-
thing. He was educated at St. Peter’s and in England,
and took his B.A. and LL.B. degrees at Cambridge in
’B2. Back in Adelaide after being called to the
Bar at the Middle Temple, he temporarily abandoned
law in favor of commerce; then he went back to
England and added an M.A. to his degrees. Ilis
widow and children are in England.
In a week or two the Destitute Asylum on North
Terrace is to be deserted in favor of the Old Folks’
Homes at Magill. These homes are planned on the
latest lines, and we are rather proud of them; but
it remains to be seen how the ancients will like the
change from the city to a place that means a long-
ish tram journey to visit friends.
H. S. Hudd, who put in three years as a Liberal
M.P. and enlisted as a private with the S. Aus-
tralian forces, has pushed up to a commission.
Friday, being a Button Day, was wet, but well over
£lOOO was collected and added to the Y.M.C.A.’s
“Active Service” fund. The Christmas Gifts Cam-
paign is in full swing, too, under the guiding hand
of the League of Loyal Women, which does the Aus-
tralian Comforts Fund work in this State, with a
huge depot at the Exhibition Building, and another
in one of the big Rundle-street shops.
Lieut. Harry Thomson, one of this State’s Rhodes
Scholars, is in the latest list of wounded. Among
the killed is Lieut. J. A. Raws, son of the Rev. J. G.
Raws, of Malvern, a promising journalist. His bro-
ther, also a lieutenant, was recently reported-miss-
ing.
There is to be a Navy League fair at the end of
next week, at North Adelaide, in Mrs. A. McCulloch’s
garden, as a preliminary to the big Naval and Red
Cross exhibition that is billed for October. And the
income-tax exemption is to be lowered to £156.
Soon it may soak into some- minds that the steady
demand for cash means something.
“Ruby Lind” is
the Australian girl
who was Miss
Lindsay—Norman’s
sister —and is Mrs.
Bill Dyson. “Mrs.
Bill” has been wor-
ried of late by the
imminent possi-
bility of hubby hav-
ing to take a gun,
although Bill’s car-
toons are being
used by the official
publicity men as
war posters. An-
other worry Rtiby
has encountered
arose from the
inconvenient prox-
imity of Zeppelins,
rendering it necessary for her frequently to
grab up husband and baby, and rush for the
bomb-proof coal-scuttle. These things have
interfered somewhat with the lady’s artistic
output, but she is still doing a little fine illus-
tration work. Bulletin readers know all
about the quality and charm of her drawing
and the extraordinary slickness of her line.
Bigjohn Forrest is a sorrowful giant these
days. The wholly Unexpected news of
brother George’s death in Westralia hit him
very hard.
F. S. Meyers, of the big blue car and the
London Stores, is marrying his daughter
Eva to liieut. B. Marks, son of Henry
Marks, of Fiji. My chief Fijian authority
has gone to the war, so I cannot say how
much of the island is his. Undoubtedly a
lot of property in this 'world is owned by
one Marks or another.
The William Gunns, of Armadale, have
been advised that the big list of missing,
compiled as a result of the doings in France
on the 19th of July, includes the name of
their son AEneas. Young Gunn made three
attempts to enlist before his final accept-
ance —an effort worthy a nephew of such a
woman as Mrs. “Never Never” Gunn.
The Bendigo Mackays have lost another
son. Angus, the eldest son of Angus, was
recently killed in France, and the news has
saddened a lot of people who know the
’Tiser family.
Now that Harold Hunt has married, the
most hardened bachelor might make an
altar-rail appointment with a girl without
startling Melbourne. Jolly little “Bobbie”
Collins, the bride, is the daughter of Dr.
Collins, of Auckland (M.L.), and once
toured for a while with the Julius Knight
Co. Harold the haughty said farewell to
his bachelor friends on Wednesday and was
married in Sydney on Thursday last. His
wedding trousseau is reported to be perfect.
The couple go to U.S.A. for a three months’
trip by the Niagara.
Hunt’s partner and closest friend, Jack
Dunlop, was married a few months ago to
the daughter of Mark Whitty. They also
went on a three-months’ voyage, and were
passengers on the last outward trip of the
Niagara. Now there is only one man of
this bachelor group left unmarried —wool-
buyer Gilman.
Mrs. Maberly Smith, who used to be one
of the Sandringham Hopes, has had some
18-carat cable news from her husband, last
heard from as leading his company in
France. He is now Major Maberly, M.C.,
which seems to indicate that he won his
cross and his majority at about the same
time. Maberly, the elder, who died not
long ago, was one of Geelong’s most-trusted
doctors.
Argus Mackinnon and his dame have
tracked up to Bananaland for a glimpse of
sun before the Cup carnival calls for further
social exertion. Daughters Barbara and
Nancy will just take a breather in the
country with their pal, Mrs. Ross Thomp-
son. Brother Lauchie is still busy pushing
army stuff on and off waggons somewhere
at one of the several fronts.
Fan Dango Mackay and her husband are
making a dash to Sydney for the horse sea-
son. They have sold their Berwick place to
Selby Paxton, who will ruralise there on
week-ends and holidays.
The daughter of the late Syd. Day was
married very quietly last Wednesday to
Arthur Riley, son of J. H. Riley, the rich
man you think of when you walk past
Inceley’s, Webb’s or Rocke’s in Collins-
street. The little bride, still sorrowing for
her genial dad and worrying about her only
brother in France, had everything done in a
minor key. She wore a lovely little cream
cloth coat and skirt and motored back from
the quiet old Scotch kirk at Brighton to
her old home for breakfast with her own
people.
Presbyterian parsons who branch off into
politics seem to have no luck. Alexander
Robb, who stood for Parliament after he
had abandoned the pulpit, sued this week
for divorce from his wife, now of Sydney.
The lady, for her part, asked for divorce
from Robb, also for alimony with custody
of the Robbins. Even a parson’s marriage
isn’t necessarily made in Heaven.
Paul Dufault began his season in the
Auditorium on Saturday night and gathered
a good audience in spite of flooding rain and
a dozen or less opposition first-nights. His
devoted people crowded the ground floor
and comfortably filled the balcony. Three
weeks —or a month at the most —will see
him through, for he is due for an American
tour and must leave Auckland on November
28. tie has to fit in a Maoriland trip before
then, and there is to be a farewell concert
in Sydney during the last week of October.
The star retains dainty little Pauline
Bindley as his soprano; besides having a
beautiful voice she has the great charm of
only reaching the star’s shoulder. That
means a lot to a short but glorious tenor.
On Saturday the little singer wore a cocoa-
nut-ice creation of white net garlanded with
graduated white satin bridal ribands; and
she indulged in a youthful pink silk sash,
pink stockings and shoes, a pink snood-
riband in her hair and side curls.
Melba is sending loads of Christmas-boxes
to the boys in the French trenches. When
they are opened a cunning little silver
mascot will be found in addition to the usual
supply of edibles, pipes, etc. The mascot
is stamped on one side “Melba’s Mascot,”
and on the other “Bless You.”
E. 11. Oliphant has carried out his threat
and leaves this week for America. His
trunk has a special water-tight compart-
ment for his plays.
Friends of artist Fred Leist, formerly of
Sydney and now of London, will be sorry to
hear that his daughter Dorothy was con-
cerned in a shocking misadventure recently.
They were giving a send-off to some soldiers
at her father’s studio. After dancing came
romping. Cyrus Cuneo, the well-known
artist, made a grab at Miss Dorothy, and
she, pushing him away, scratched one of bis
nostrils with her finger-nail. Some time
afterwards the little scratch became a
poisoned wound, and 10 days later Cuneo
died of septic pleurisy.
Jack Sommers, artist and poet, is in Lon-
don recovering from trench fever. At least;
he was in that city that swallows Zeppe-
lins, but just now the gentry of Hertfordshire
are showing him what English country life
is like.
George Chirnside, of Werribee Park, is
spending his time between Werribee and
private hospitals endeavoring to heal a
wandering’ knee-cap. Ilis appearance in a
motor-car with bandaged limb has led to
his being cheered as a returned-damaged
soldier. His decorative wife and daughter
Audrey are holding up the Red Cross in
London.
Henceforth the Red. Cross wants no frills
on its socks. Many sock-Susies have spent
time in doing fancy designs on their knit-
knitting forget-me-nots, “conversation”
mottoes and the like. It doesn’t necessarily
give a soldier that liome-like feeling to
march with a heart-and-arrow chafing his
instep or “A Message from Port Melbourne’’
rubbing inside the top of his boot. Hence
the Red Cross edict.
Haw-Hawthorn Anglicans are going to
drown their war troubles in a joyful noise
—£looo worth of it. A new organ is to be
built right away for well-fed St. Oolomb’s,
and the cash is ready. Maybe the expen-
diture won’t help to win the war; but at
least the money will be spent in Australia,
an Adelaide man having been given the job.
Wednesday was the Italian National Day,
but the Italians here felt that they could
not rejoice and make merry while their
people at home were so busy lighting. How-
ever, Consul Eles and a small band of
patriots met at Richardson’s Cafe for din-
ner, drank their country’s health and re-
joiced over the specially good Italian war
news just through.
The William Weatherly and George Kelly
widows are preparing a welcome for spinster
sister Maggie, who three "years back lit out
for California in search of health. Having-
found it, the little Scotch lady is coming
back to Australia for a three-months’ chat
before getting back to the pines that Bret
Harte advertised so well.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

WHIMf
The TOP-NOTCHSeotch.
ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“McNortli” : The hardest feet I ever met
belonged to Bill Merter, a long, loosely-built Aus-
tralian who hunted the nimble pennyweight bare-
footed for years in the rough country of Woodlark
Island (Papua). He had never been known to wear
boots, and he would use the side of his heel as a
block for cutting up tobacco or sharpening a stick,
like a native. One day while the mine tool-sharpener
was making drills Bill accidentally put his bare
foot on an inch or so of red-hot steel which had
just been cut off. Bill didn’t notice it; he just
stood there and asked: “What th’ hell’s that smell
of somethin’ burnin’ ?” It took fully half a minute
for the hot steel to eat its way through the hard,
leather-like substance that composed the sole of
Bill’s foot. But when it did there was an uproar.
It was many weeks before Bill put his foot to the
ground again, and when he did it was safely packed
in cow-hide.
* * * *
“YVada”: Re “Barcoo” and the homing
instinct of animals (B. 17/8/’l6). A party of
aboriginals from the Murrumbidgee near Hay went
to Melbourne accompanied by the usual dogs that
Binghi collects about him. After having seen the
sights they departed for home, taking train as far
as Goulburn and hoofing the rest of the journey.
The dogs were last seen in Collins-street, Melbourne,
but on arrival at their destination on the ’Bidgee,
the first to welcome the wanderers home were the
same mongs.
“Lowan”: Can the camel swim at all?
Some years ago my mate and I were trying to pilot
a couple of Abdul’s friends across the Murchison
(W.A.), when one of them slipped into deep water
and disappeared like a -politician’s pledge. He rose
again some little distance down stream ; but merely
to say “Kismet,” or something. Then he drifted away
around a bend without so much as making the
smallest dog-paddle for land.
* * * *
“Barcoo”: “Biddy B.” some time back
gave a list of the peculiar names tacked to the ships
of the interior. Years ago when the Duke of Man-
chester toured Western Bananaland the hostess at
one banquet was the proprietress of the principal
pub. Her speech of welcome was fixed for her, but
in her triumphal moment she flowed over and
supplemented the welcome with an invitation that at
some future date his Lordship might call again and
bring the “dear Dukess” with him. This so tickled
the local wheelwright that he straightway christ-
ened a new waggon on the stocks “The Dukess.”
All hands are dead now, and even the waggon has
senile decay.
“J.J.C.”: “Vigil ans” (B. 24/8/T6) 4s
correct when he states that the termites in the
Territory tackle live timber. The only sticks that
are immune are the teatree or paper bark, the Leich-
hardt pine and the bamboo. But I do not agree
that the pest has taken up permanent residence in
the Territory. It has been found in Queensland
that when they are disturbed they immediately leave.
If land is ploughed up before the crop is put in the
white ant does not molest it. But if the crop is
only “hoed” in it is devoured. This was proved
by experiment on the Government farms on the
Daly River.
* * * *
“Longy”: The Vic. Dept, of Agriculture
has a sure and simple cure for the borer that
attacks the sugar gum, and as many Western men
have planted the gums and had them ruined by these
sappers, I pass on the remedy. Pour a3O per cent,
solution of phenol into the crawler’s tunnel and
plug the entrance with disinfectant soap. If the
grub is making upwards a syringe will do the trick
nicely.
* * * *
“Hay”: Several Abo. writers have praised
the plain-turkey on a dish. But it is not as
toothsome as the wood-duck, known to Binghi as
“koonahli.” After rain, when water is to be found
in the clay-pans, wood-ducks arrive in thousands
and can be easily bagged with a shot-gun. They
flock to these feeding-grounds in great numbers and
their peculiar, harsh-sounding notes can be heard at
night in all directions. The bird is a size larger
than the teal and much brighter in plumage. A
lunch of cold roast wood-duck and johnny-cake is
a meal not easily beaten.
* * * *
“Granite”: The best sheepdog I ever
knew was a magpie. Fealy, who ran a pub at
Black Springs, near Beechworth (Vic.), also owned
about 30 sheep. The magpie kept them in order all
day, and at a signal yarded them at night. Mag
yapped and snapped just like a sheepdog, and any
jumbuck that didn’t do as it was ordered knew
what to expect.
* * * *
“Corio”: When Hogan’s store began to
blaze a stampede whs made with buckets to the only
available water in the creek some distance away.
But the best the water-carriers could do was to keep
the fire in check. At length Ryan hit upon a plan. He
harnessed his bullock-team to the building and pulled
it to the creek. After thiat, it was easy to extin-
guish the flames.

“Spreader”: Near Wilberforce, in the
County of Cumberland (N.S.W.), I have had an
experience growing cabbages for the city markets
which might set irrigation enthusiasts on a new
train of thought. We set aside four acres of ordi-
nary black-soil cabbage land, cropped the season
before with corn. When the rain came we ploughed,
harrowed twice, and rolled the ground. Two acres
we let stand as it was till the time came for plant-
ing out; the other two acres were harrowed and
cross harrowed twice a week, and at the end of
every fortnight we turned this portion over with
the double-furrow plough. Two months from start-
ing we got a fair fall of rain, and the whole four
acres were turned over, harrowed, rolled, drilled and
the plants put out. The dry-
worked portion was scuffled as
soon as the plants took, and the
other half was left as it stood.
We flooded the drills twice a
week witli water from an irriga-
tion plant, and in order to give
the matter a severe test we fer-
tilised this portion with bonedust
and blood. The two acres that-
had been dry cultivated were
scuffled in between the rows three
times a week, but got no manure
or water. Here are the results.
The two acres that were manured
and watered but not worked to
conserve moisture, and only
chipped twice with a hoe to keep
down weeds, netted £25 19s.
after all labor, manure and engine
oil had been allowed for. The
dry two acres netted £3l 4s. 6d.
Of course, the expenses were
heavier on the irrigated section,
but the fact that has the weighti-
est bearing on the case is that
the cabbages that Nature grew
(with a little assistance) grossed
£1 14s. Bd. an acre more than
those that were artificially grown.
Irrigation as the means of supply-
ing the future wealth of this
country, judged by what experi-
ence we have had of it, doesn’t
seem to be so promising as the
possibilities of increasing the pro-
ductiveness of millions of acres
of the best land that are not within range of artifi-
cial water. It is not to be inferred for a moment
that anything other than water is the prime factor
in growing crops. The point at issue is that by a
fair knowledge of dry-farming half our so-called
droughts would lose their significance.
“Dead Finish”: My queer pub-name is
the One Eye. It stands six miles from Maxwelton
Siding, on Queensland’s Great Northern Railway,
and a mile off the One Eye crossing in the Flinders
River. Two years ago one of the portable shearing
plants which peregrinate thereabouts was pitched
near this pub. Among the crowd were a one-eyed
cook and a ditto offsider ; two shearers had one eye
apiece, and two rouseabouts were gunners. The
penner-up’s dog had —-well, anyway, the pub had its
name years before.

soon. I’m having a red and white one made for
special occasions.”

THE RIME OF A CASUAL COVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The creek beside the tent went dancing down
And glancing down the granite-bouldered way;
A kookaburra, like a solemn clown,
Laughed in the face of day.
The mine was calling, calling me to graft;
But morning beckoned me and lightly laughed.
And through the scented bush I saw the bright
Dance of the silver sunlight on the ground.
A thousand birds were welcoming the light.
A never-slumbering sound
Thundered from where the headlong waterfall
Hung like a veil before the mountain-wall.
And in that ever-rumbling thunder-tone
I heard the tumult of a mighty host
Of white-maned ocean-chargers booming down
The sandy miles of coast.
It woke the follies in the heart of me,
And set me longing for the rolling sea.
Above the billy’s bubble-bubble song
Clattered the clanking engine at the mine;
But in my gipsy blood there ran the strong
Riot of wander-wine;
And still the willy-nilly ocean-call
Rang through the thunder of the waterfall.
The whistle blew. My thoughts went underground,
Along the tunnel that I daily trod ;
And then they hovered by the seas that sound
Like songs of some great god ;
And back to stopes of darkness down below,
And up to-day—and then I damned the show!
I damned it once, and if the truth must be,
I thought no more about the mine at all,
But strode away to seek the rolling sea,
And past the waterfall,
And down and down the mountain-track I went
'Swinging, and singing with a heart content.
Night, and the great grave stars that sauntered by ;
Night; and a droning lullaby of billow,
A bed of leaves beneath a tent of sky,
A continent for pillow.
That night the good-night kisses of the breeze
Lulled me to sleep beside the summer seas.
And there for seven days I lived the free
Life of a loafer in a lotus-land ;
A seven lazy, hazy days of sea
And sun and clean warm sand;
A seven days of drowsy dream-delights—
Marred by the blasted loneliness o’ nights.
The hags of solitude they slept by day,
But night by night along that silent shore....
Enough! At sunrise I was on the way
Back to the mine once more;
Ay ! back to drives and stopes, the plunger’s noise,
The murk and work, but—back among the boys!
Morning ; the plains ; the sunlight shimmering ;
Noon, and the ranges where the long climb starts;
Sunset, and gaudy parrots quarrelling
Like crowds in Asian marts;
Night, and the light o’ friendly six-by-eights;
Hey! and the hearty cheeros of my mates.
Lord ! it was jolly to be back once more
And yarning, yarning half the night away.
Then like a soldier coming home from war
I went to work next day ;
Blithe as the sunlight dancing on the track
I went to work —and found I'd got the sack!
MAORIIjAND.
BARTLETT ADAMSON.

“R.S.A.”: I will cheerfully uncover my
flowing locks to the quidnunc who will truthfully
tell me that he can tell the difference between iron-
bark and grey gum when the bark has been removed.
There is a most unjustifiable prejudice against grey
gum as firewood, though it is every bit as good as
ironbark, which realises anything up to 2s. 6d. a ton
more than its relative. If, however, you are chopping
amongst green timber it takes no time to remove the
bark from your grey-gum slabs, and then off to
market goes a truck-load of timber which the agent
himself will designate as “best ironbark,” and which
the buyer will have no cause to growl about when he
applies the good Australian match.

“Snell”: The long, thin man whined
for half a minute, and then Gunn parted up the price
of two beers. “You see that bloke sorter done me a
good turn once,” Gunn explained in apologetic
tones. “Fact is,” he said, “I was in a bad way,
out of work, hard up, neuritis in the neck, ’n’ the
girl I was shook on marryin’ another feller. I sez
to myself, ‘Jumbo Gunn, this earth ain’t no place
for you. You’d be more at home and happier some-
wheres else.’ I was that tuckered out and blue all
through I agreed unconditional, an’ takin’ all things
into consideration, plumped for drowndin’. I went
to the big rock where the river turns above Mac-
fadyin’s, an’ I prepared for the worst. 1 took off
me coat, ’n’ folded it up, laid me hat on top, an’
then waded in. Up to me knees I stopped t’ put in
a plea for a square deal in the hereafter, an’ just
chancin’ to look up I spotted a bloke at my coat.
He was turnin’ it over ; ’n’ presently he offs with his
own string iv rags, an’ ducks into my best beaufort.
’Struth ! I wasn’t goin’ t’ that. I fetched
a yell would have thrown a cow off her base, an’

“The Reefer”: The Purari Papuans are
dwarfs, but claim they could be giants if they liked.
The mere application of the juice of a plant that
grows in their back yard will considerably enlarge
any parts of the body to which it is applied. So to
become a giant it is only necessary to collect suffi-
cient of the elixir and have a bath in it.
“Sarto’s” tabloid melodrama :
Act I: Two robins build a nest in one of my
trees.
Act II: As many eggs therein.
Act III: Two young birds.
Act IV: Two amazed robins feeding two lumber-
some cuckoos.
Act V: Divorce.
“Ilex”: “Barcoo” (B. 7/9/’l6) should
take his jibbing steed out on a quiet road, and as
soon as he jibs hit him with the whip, -say, on the
shoulder. Then securely truss the nag with straps
and ropes, and keep him in this fixed state for about
half an hour after he wants to move on. He will
gee so disgusted at being made to stand still that
after three doses one flick of the whip on the
shoulder will remind him of the torture and prove
a cure for all I.WAV. principles.
* * * *
“E. 8.”: “Pick” (B. 24/S/’l6) asks if
any Abo. orchardist has tried camping sheep among
his trees to eradicate codlin moth. I don’t know of
any orchardist having done so, but I know an apple
orchard that was treated with sheep droppings with
complete success. About July the soil was taken up
to a depth of about 18in., leaving the surface roots
bare. The holes were then filled with sheep drop-
pings and the soil put back again. The apples proved
to be quite sound and free from the curse, but three
trees untreated were infected as in previous years.

“Eddyson”: “ ’Tis quare ’n’ multifarious
the way iv Fortune wid a man,” said Flynn.
“There’s Cornalius Crowe succeed in life be havin’
a tree fell on him. Cornalius is a man well
beknown. Can it be true ve’ve not heard iv the
fame iv him ’n’ him a number iv Parleymint wonst
’n’ a man wid a reputation fer ugliness mentioned
in the press rnanny occasions? Crowe is livin’ here
wid the rest iv us, bor-rn ’n’ bred in Bungaree, ’n’
till he is twinty-siven remar-rkable fer nothin’ but
ugliness. I’m admittin’ free he’s a fine figure iv a
man, tall ’n’ well shouldered, ’n’ wid an eye to
him like the bluebell, ’n’ a clane brown shkin t’ go
wid it, but fer the rest ugly ez the Devil’s self; ’n’
smit sore wid the dirty timper too. ‘Black Crowe’
the girls are for ever callin’ him, ’n’ he’s an open
shport wid them. There’s thim is irritated, wid the
presence iv a cat or any shmall har-rmless thing, ’n’
Crowe is that way with weemin. ’Tis plain they
irritate him all over; ’n’ when weemin is acquaint
wid that sor-rt iv man ’tis their nature t’ get to
him like blackbur-rds on one iv their kind wid a
white feather ’r some sich defor-rmity, ’n’ peck him
to death. There’s none more impish wid Cornalius
Crowe than Nora Conley, nineteen ’n’ a ha’f, ez
purty ’n’ fragrant ez a bunch iv honeysuckle in the
early day, God save her! Lor-rd knows there’s no
har-rm in her impudence wid Crowe; ’tis the mis-
chief iv a squir’l. ‘Good mor-rnin’, Black Crowe,’
sez she. ‘Will yeh never shmile at all, man, ’n’ it
the lovely gay day ?’ ‘Go Tong wid yeh,’ sez he;
never a wor-rd more’n that, ’n’ she’s hoppin’ to ’n’
fro, callin’ to him. ‘Yer face is like a broken pot,
Cornalius Crowe. Is there never a tradesman t’
mend it ?’ sez she. ‘Ah-h, go ’long,’ sez he. Well,
there’s wan day Nora Conley is cornin’ up frim the
crick wid wattle ’n’ wild geeraniums in her hair, lep-
pin’ this way ’n’ that, whin Cornalius Crowe is
lumberin’ down frim One Hut. She sings a rhyme
at him filled wid the ins’lence iv youth ’n’ the
glory iv a great day, V he answers nothin’ at all.
Lookin’ by chance she sees the grey iv his dead
face striped crimson wid the blood is drippin’ frim
his hair, V one ar-rm is hangin’, ’n’ he is stumblin’
ez he goes. She’s at him. She’s no more a girl at
all, but a strong woman. ‘Cornalius Crowe, yer
hurted, man,’ sez she. ‘Sit! sit, will yeh not?’
She pulls him down on a log, ’n’ is busy wid his
wounds. Her little round hands is ez sweet on him
ez butterflies’ wings ’n’ ez strong ez Billy the
smith’s, ’n’ all the while she’s murmurin’ at him
a way that takes pain from his hear-rt. She tears
the things she’s wearin’ t’ bind him, ’n’ minds it
not a bit. Then she lies him in the green shade
’n' runs fer cool crick water in his hat. Then sez
she: ‘Yi’ll lie here, poor man, till I’ll bring a
car-rt.’ ’N’ she was off on- her small swift feet ’n’
back agin soon, wid a car-rt ’n’ a bed tick to lit.
She’d spoke to none, ’n’ to none would she; ’twas
t’ be her own doin’. She brought him home, ’n’
she nursed him, ’n’ from the moment she found him
hurt be the hill she was his, she said, no matter
what. Cornalius wed wid her when well, ’n’ he
was like a man has received the benediction iv
Heaven ’n’ is fearful the joy of ut may flow through
his fingers away. Frim the day iv their marryin’
nothin’ wint wrong wid him. ’Twas like she’d put
her little cute brain into his great head. He
far-rmed wid more sinse than anny other man in
the disthrict, ’n’ then he wint t’ Parleymint ’n’

“Daybreak”: It’s waste of time to talk
in hundreds of how many posts have been split from
one tree. Here in Tassy they get ’em in thousands.
Quite recently I saw an old stringy bashed to pieces
that yielded 2500 posts and as many pickets. And
that was not considered anything out of the ordi-
nary ; there are heaps of other bird-rests bigger than
that. But if it comes to really big trees, well
When the railway was being built from Burnie to
Waratah (Tas.) there was a stick in the road that
took four men, on a platform, a fortnight to fall,
with two boys barrowing the chips away. AVhen it
did finally crash to earth the thump could be felt
all over the Speck. Hoary splitters swear that it
would have split enough posts and pickets to run a
fence across the island.
ART IS LONG, ETC.
VISITOR : “I suppose the most difficult part
in your work is to find the colors?”
ARTIST: “Yes, Miss —and to pay for ’em.”
“Tiaki”: Do forests suffer from epi-
demics? I knew a collection of sticks in Maoriland,
comprising rimu, totara, white pine and matai, which
fell a victim to arboreal phthisis, or something, in
1870, and 20 years later the greater part of the
giants were rotting in an early grave. Henare ex-
plained it by a prophecy that as the power of the
Maori faded, so would the big timber.
EXTENUATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They yanked him off to gaol because he took a
humble share
Of Wilson’s sheep (and Wilson had ten thousand
sheep to spare !).
The}' placed him in a felon's dock, with blue-clad
peelers near,
To give the Law its dignity and fill his heart with
fear;
To give the Law its majesty, its glory and its
force. ...
He pleaded guilty—but, my oath! that man could
ride a horse!
He broke Convention’s written code —he broke it into
bits
(And when they ran him into quod I guess the
twain were quits!).
He did the thing decreed against by men who make
the laws—
Queer coves who draw big screws to work their high-
falutin’ jaws.
You say: “He got his just deserts—the Law must
take its course.”
Well, p’r’aps it lias to—but, my oath! that man
could ride a horse!
N.s.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.

What a wonderful family it is! Rose,
Harriet and Sally Hyams, with a fourth
sister who is now Mrs. Laurie Abrahams,
started their pirouetting more than a
quarter-century ago. Though only a few
feet high and very broad, the family long
ago triumphed over such trifling obstacles
to success, and now has an apparently un-
warpable reputation as a toe-training com-
bination. Harriet Hyams, by the way, was
a piano-student at the Melbourne Conserva-
torium years ago, and was one of the little
bunch of that time who were regarded as
having a Future. But Harriet had her own
ideas and duly carried them out.
The Italian opera people complain that
they cannot find a cheap spaghetti palace in
all Sydney. Whilst in Melbourne they
browsed luxuriously at places where the food
was delivered the way they liked it, they
declare that Sydney serves it in an egg-cup.
As everybody who has travelled knows,
spaghetti should be served with a tub and
two toasting-forks.
Dorothy Brunton used to be a footlight
sister to Harrie Ireland when that red-haired
actress was a reigning Bland Iloltian. Doro-
thy Brunton, Daisy Ireland and Irma Caron
were just three ambitious youngsters then
and used to sit in a bunch at Harrie’s shoe-
tips. Clever Dorothy got a big share of the
attention. In those days the hair that is now
so golden hung down her back and was a
deep chestnut. It was so long that the
proud owner could sit on the further ends
of it. Now it is fair and fluffy like a baby’s;
but only half the length.
“K.” : After sitting out the first Sydney
performance of “Romance,” I have decided
not to begin any future plays of mine with
two gramophone selections. The gramo. on
the stage—even with a Caruso record on
sounds like a small child squeaking in a
back parlor, and the only person in the Cri-
terion who wasn’t fidgeting was the aged and
reverend Frank Harvey. But he was being
paid to keep still. When devoted grand-
daughter Alma Rock Phillips went to put on
a second disc, an agonised voice from aloft
yelled “Cut it out!” The polite but wearied
downstair people seconded the motion with a
sigh.
“Circle”: Within the next 20 years I
shall attend a performance of a Ned Kelly
grand opera by an Australian composer.
Unless the opera tradition alters, we must
have blood and thunder —and, as a wholesale
dealer in those necessary props., Ned ranks
after the tlieatrically-impossible aboriginals.
In Italy our most eminent bushranger will
be disguised as Nedda Kelli, and La Scala,
where the old traditions roost high, will
put him into a Fauntleroy plush suit and a
lace collar. If he is extremely unlucky he
will also have a light court sword and black
cotton gloves.

ing whether “Edson’s” pertinent remarks on
the flabbiness of dramatic criticism in Mel-
bourne (B. 24/8/T6) have anything to do
with it, but it is noticeable that the Argus
has since become quite dashing—not to say,

Two low comedians who seldom rose above the

noun !
Or this :—•
The chorus have been chosen for qualities obvious
rather than vocal.
I shall not be surprised if we get some real
criticism shortly.
“J.F.11.” (London) : The Bishop of
London’s crusade against the promenades in
certain variety houses has been successful at
last. Such pressure was brought upon the
London County Council that word was
passed round to the managers to afford no
further facilities to Tottie if they wanted
their licenses renewed. So Alfred Butt, of
the Empire, and the lesser lights of other
houses have announced that the promenades
will no longer be open to women. Evidently
Melbourne man Oswald Stoll smelt some-
thing. The Alhambra promenade was one of
the roomiest and most largely patronised of
all these places ; but he promptly closed it
on taking over the management a few weeks
ago.
Business, Bobbery, etc.
“The Hum” : Shave, sir? Then allow me
to introduce Miss Ainsworth, of Narrandera
(N.S.W.), who has handled razor and
scissors for the past couple of years. Her
saloon is a joy for ever, because so many
familiar things are absent. No beery breath,
no advice about the races, no futile argu-
ments —only peace, clean work and courtesy.
I move that barbering be put on the pro-
hibited list for men,
* * * *
“Quidam”: The Drouin (Vic.) butcher
who has the motto “Live and Let Live” over
his shop is not original. Twenty-five years
ago a butcher at Bundaberg (Q.) had the
same words on his crest. I remember it par-
ticularly well, because I was driving down to
Burnett Heads with an old drystick of a
man I was canvassing for pictures with,
time-payment made-in-Germany stairs that
an Australian house made a big fortune out
of. I remarked upon the singular inappro-
priateness of the motto, but my old anti-
derrink confrere didn't see any point in the
remark. Some weeks later, when we struck
Howard, a coal-mining town outside Mary-
borough, just as it was celebrating a football
match by the most whole-souled drunk I ever
saw anywhere, my teetotal friend got so
overcome that he became intoxicated for a
fortnight, and his most consistent topic of
conversation was the humorous inappropri-
ateness of that butcher’s sign. I have ever
since refused to listen to arguments about
liquor making a man dull. In such matters
you mustn’t generalise.
“Condenser”: The Bulletin of July 20
refers to a mineral called lenzile which the
Yanks are marketing at a high price for use
in wireless telegraphy detectors. The par. ven-
tures the opinion that at the price probably
Australia could supply a rival from its own
minerals. Right! It can. lam using for
the same purpose a mineral which Australia
can supply in large quantities, and which
will give six times the distance of any stan-
dard Marconi magnetic detector, and to lit it
up as a detector costs about the price of a
whisky and soda. In good weather I can
receive readable signals at a distance of over
4000 miles. Its name is galena, and the best
of it comes from Geraldton (W.A.).

Concerning the splitting of an old hair: —
An interesting point arising out of the law as to
infants was decided by the Tasmanian Practice Court
lately. The Public Trustee asked for directions in
the administration of the inter-State estate of an
“infant” —a storekeeper who, at the date of death,
owed divers merchants £350 for goods supplied, of
which some £7O worth was still unsold. The next-of-
kin claimed these unsold goods on the ground that
their deceased relative, being under 21, wasn’t liable
for trade debts. The Court held that, under the law,
an infant’s contracts other than for necessaries
were void, and as bills for trade goods weren’t run up
for “neoessaries” the creditors couldn’t recover any
cash. But the creditors could recover the goods
themselves, provided they could identify them. So
it amounts to this—if a creditor “gets fly” to the
fact that an infant debtor isn’t likely to pay him he
can demand, and if necessary sue for, the recovery of
goods supplied, providing that the infant isn’t “fly”
enough to get rid of the stuff before the creditor
“gets fly” as aforesaid. The bird that flies quickest
wins.
* # * *
To “rob a poor man of his beer” takes a
lot of doing. Premier Peacock, very busy
just now with the Six O’clock Closing Bill,
circulated the other day some illuminating
statistics regarding the shutting-up of Vic-
torian pubs. The figures show that since
Sept.. 1906, pubs have been reduced from
3448 to 2505, while the liquor purchased over
the counter has frothed up from £1,607,760
to £2.021,766 worth. The average sale by
each of the 3448 pubs for the year ended
Sept. 30, 1906, was £470; the average for
the 2505 for the year ended Sept. 30, 1915,
was £S00!
* * * *
“Clxloe”: We ought to investigate the
methods and materials used by the
women of Timor (Malay Archipelago) who
extract from their own flora by hand-press
the colors they use in the staining of
their rich and beautiful sarongs. I have two
hand-made pieces of cotton from the house-
hold of the Rajah of Gabenzoes, Koepang.
These have been for five years in constant
use as portiere and table-cover respectively,
and have not lost color nor become in any
way shabby. The colors are orange, red,
black, a rich emerald, a maroon and a gold,
as well as a pale daffodil. They were spun
on old-fashioned distaffs, woven on curious
hand-looms worked by six women at each,
the patterns being painted on by hand with
a dye-brusli as the material passed through
the loom. Each rajah has his own color
and pattern, in the manner of the Celtic
tartans. Sarongs such as those in my pos-
session last half a lifetime. If they don t
the grand-daughter of the lady who made the
disappointing garment is remonstrated with
by the grand-daughter of the owner. It’s a
slow business.

Out of the battle-smoke comes a joyous
letter from a lad who tells how he
“again got a pair of Australian boots. They
are second-hand, but they fit me all right.
The Tommy boots have neither shape, make
nor size.” But we must go on importing,
gentlemen. It stands to reason that the
foreigner can do the thing better.
The Ellerman liner City of Hankow stag-
gered away from Williamstown (Vic.) the
other day with what is reputedly the big-
gest wheat cargo ever lifted in Australia by
one vessel. She had on board 126,000 bags,
equal to 10,500 tons. The next heaviest
wheat cargo is said to be one of 123,600 bags.
In the Sydney District Court the other
day Judge Murray jumped vigorously on one
of the snide practices :of some cos. that deal
in Workmen’s Compensation insurance. A
workman had had his eyes damaged while in
the employment of the defendant firm. At
first he thought that only one eye was hurt,
and was induced, for the sum of £4 10s., to
sign a document purporting to release the
firm from further liability. Subsequently it
was found that both eyes were affected, and
the damaged person sued for adequate com-
pensation. When the paper he had signed
was produced against him, it raised the
anger of Justice, which sometimes manages
to get angry in the right way. Having
snorted that insurance cos. which took over
employers’ liabilities apparently issued forms
like this in order to entrap the unwary, the
Judge snapped out a verdict that meant £2OO
and costs.
