“Unba”: “F.N.T.” (B. 7/9/T6) is no
doubt quite right in supposing that brown
paper could be made of bracken-fern. You can
make paper out of nearly all our native
plants, from the bluegum down to the com-
mon “sag” rush. The Melbourne Botanical
Museum and the Technological Museum con-
tain specimens of paper from dozens of
different shrubs and plants. But whether it
would be a commercial proposition is an-
other matter. “F.N.T.” commends the ex-
periment to the Federal Council of Science
and Industry; but it is surely, like the
making of glues and gelatines and other
things already turned down, too sordid for
that exalted body to soil its fingers with.
“D.F.”: Some time ago I mentioned the
apple-store dug out on a hillside by It. It.
Bealey, of Kangaroo Valley (Vic.), who
was thereby enabled to keep his fruit in
good condition right through the year with-
out bothering about cold-storage. __ This
evoked a snort of disbelief from “Kopje,”
who said if there was anything in the idea
everybody would be doing it. Some of them
are: a Vic. orchardist is now selling at a
good long price Jonathan apples which he
has kept through the winter in such a store.
* * * *
“Harrow”: Some dreadfully superior
people came to live in our street a few weeks
ago. A mutual butcher told my wife that
they were “from home.” The housefather
mostly wears a golfing suit, though there is
no evidence that he golfs. Of course, the
family does not know us, although now and
again, when I have been catching an early
train, I have met the daughter—an anaemic
girl who carries her head at an angle of 45
degrees —cycling homewards, accompanied by
a violin-case. I was mildly curious about
these early music-lessons, until the other
morning, when a dog got in the bike’s way
and brought the rider down. The violin-case
flew open, tossing on to the roadway—a
priceless Strad. ? Not a bit of it. Some
carrots, turnips, beetroot and a parcel of
what were probably sausages. The girl obvi-
ously was uninjured, and I was chivalrous
enough not to assist in the work of salvage.
After all, there is something Spartan in the
snobberv which will send a haughty damsel
marketing in the early morn with a violin-
case in which to conceal her guilt.
“Cam” • At church the other Sunday mora-
ine I sat in the same pew as a friend of
ffie named Catter. When the good man
was reading out the names and addresses of
those about to wed I saw Catter, who has a
time Davment furniture arcade, rapidly mak-
ing notes in a pocket-book. He told me later
that he posts business-cards and catalogues
every Monday to the prospective customers,
and ‘does a number of churches in rotation.
He says religion pays.
BEAUTY’S BOOTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Just now the Australian woman, consumed with a
desire to make herself look like a darkey’s bride,
decorates the ends of her legs with arrangements
that simply yell for inspection. — Bulletin 21/8/’l6.
I made it my biz ev’ry dear girl’s phiz
To see as she passed on the street,
But of late I lower my eyes to quiz
What the boj's call her “plates o’ meat.”
No more I’m cowed by her frozen frown ;
For her glacial look who cares?
I don’t see her face for I’m glancing down
At the .wonderful boots she \years.
I no longer offend any lady friend
Or deserve the rebuke “What cheek! ”
“Glad eyes”—like winks —are now at an end,
Direct ones or oblique;
Her gaze I meet no more, for I
Move along with downcast lids ;
I may look devout, but my glance is sly
At her gaitered tans or kids.
Though ever so fair her face and hair,
What matter ? I’m gone on her boots !
Her spats are her fortune now, and rare
Is the sight of her “daisy roots.”
I shall fall to the most attractive brace,
And the owner, where’er we may meet,
She may have any sort of a foolish face —-
I shall throw myself at her feet 1
VICTORIA.
THE BOOT-LOVER.
THE SAINTLY “TRAY.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I am sure the threepenny-bit will go to Heaven.
It comes to church twice every Sunday.”
—Rev J. Snell (Ballarat).
To church proceeds the saintly tray
In answer to the bell.
While half-crowns roll in wanton play,
It keeps the Sabbath well,
And lies both morn and eve in state,
An offering upon the plate.
But is it not in Scripture writ
More joyous pseans roll
Where angels bright in Glory sit
When some poor, spotted soul
Is rid of its unrighteousness,
Than o’er ten-score that ne’er transgress?
And though the pastors praise it so,
How often they have craved
To have the thripny here below
By frugal laymen “saved,”
While they employed bell, book and gown
To salve the reprobate half-crown!
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.
HIS DRAMATIC END.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Old Bronk, the great tragedian,
Was superannuated. He
Was very old and weak, poor man,
And near his finish seemed to be.
We knew, though, if he had to go,
He’d pass out like a gallant pro.
He acted in that close retreat
Like one upon a spacious stage;
He’d enter, exit, drink, or eat
The greatest actor of his age.
In bed, approaching his decease,
He was the hero of the piece.
One night he strode into the storm,
Declaiming with his final breath,
With lifted brow and swaying form,
Some splendid periods from “Macbeth.”
Long thunder tore the heavens wide -
He bowed to God’s applause and died!
VICTORIA.
STARGAZER.
THE DRINKING STAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a star whose brightness naught could dim;
He blazed and blinked for many hundred nights;
Mere men of earth could scarcely glance at him
Who trod the firmament in costly tights.
But all lights fade and ev’ry star must fall,
Leaving all black when Hope and Power have fled ;
We found our dark star lying in the hall
And, panting, bore him up the stairs to bed.
Like Caesar’s corse was he when We came in ;
Rum was his posture but his breath was gin.
N.S.W.

SAFETY
*J[ Enjoyable touring and efficient commercial transport
are alike impossible without the sense of security imparted
by good tyres. The toughness of Avon rubber and the
strength of Avon casing reduce to a minimum the risk of
punctures or bursts, and the sure grip of the “ Sunstone ’
ladder-grooved tread makes you as skid-proof as it is
possible to be —whatever the weather, whatever the road.
COMFORT
*H" Superior resilience alone can reveal the maximum com-
fort of motoring on pneumatic tyres. Many a car owes
its reputation for luxurious springing and silky running to
the buoyant liveliness of its Avon Tyres.
ECONOMY
f The same qualities of strength and resilience that
achieve safety and comfort necessarily cut down ultimate
costs —of petrol, repairs to chassis and tyres as well.
Avon economy is the economy of endurance —the typically
British sort of tyre beauty that is more than tread-deep.
The claim that Avon Tyres show lowest cost per mile rests
upon disinterested records of average mileage in everyday
service, open to investigation and capable of demon-
stration on your vehicle.

POLITICAL
POINS
IN TOPHET.
HIAWTHAUS : “The war seems to have
broken the astrologer’s heart.”
CHIBIABOSUS : “Yes; he tells me he’s had
more hick picking Melbourne Cup
winners than peace, dates.”
The Federal Government is itself tremen-
dously to blame for the most serious criti-
cism with which the Referendum Bill has
been raked. The Government shut up Par-
liament, and while it had things its own way
it muddled along and prevented the exposure
of wrongs and grievances. No sooner had
Parliament resumed and members’ mouths
were opened than the most hateful of the
restrictions on free speech were withdrawn.
This is a pretty good indication of what
ought to be done —so long as there is one
soldier, and especially one conscripted sol-
dier, in khaki, so long should Parliament
remain in session. It is not necessary that
it should meet every day—one day a week
might be enough. But this great protection
ought to be afforded; and if Hughes is wise
he will promptly undertake that it shall be.
Treasurer Higgs has struck so many snags
in connection with his War Time Profits Bill
that the measure has been held up. He had
no sooner made up his mind on a general
principle of assessment than a dozen cases
were cited to show that that principle would
produce injustice. It does not look as
though it will be possible to lay down any
hard and fast rules; and there is no good
reason why anything should be attempted
except in a very broad way. If we have tri-
bunals to say which men should and which
should not go to war, it is no less reason-
able that we should have tribunals to declare
what profits are due to the war, and being
due to the war should belong to the nation.
It is a big job, perhaps; but men like H. Y.
Braddon, of Sydney ; George Swinburne, of
Melbourne; and Chief Justice Murray, of
Adelaide, could do it with far more equity
than it could be done by any hard-and-fast
method laid down by elaborate legislation.
REDUCTION OF MEMBERS. —
AUSTRIAN METHOD.
Berne, Thursday.—Thirty-five seats in the Aus-
trian Parliament have been vacated since 1914.
Twenty of the former occupants were hanged and
others imprisoned for treason. — Cable.
Hughes can thank Andrew Fisher in the
first place and himself in the second for the
whole of the trouble he is now in, and for
the turmoil of the last few weeks. Over two
years ago Fisher specifically promised to in-
troduce “as soon as possible after Parliament
meets” an Initiative and Referendum Bill.
He had ample time to do so, as had Hughes
since: but neither attempted to keep the
election pledge. Had they done so somebody
else would long ago have taken the onus of
demanding a Conscription Referendum, and
Hughes, by thus making that possible, would
have got the credit for its acceptance. That
chicken wasn’t long in coming home to roost.
“F.0.E.”: At Garden Island, Sydney,
where they fit out the transports, they make
bonfires of the partitions, etc., taken out of
converted steamers. It is material that could
be put to good use; but it isn’t even heating
a boiler or crackling under a widow’s kettle.
Hundreds of thousands of pounds’ worth of
expensive fittings have been simply smashed
to pieces—fittings, including carved wood,
that could have been taken down in sections,
and replaced later on. The wicked waste
still continues, and apparently will continue
until the Government calls in half a dozen
business men to form a sort of roaming
commission' whose reports will be presented
fortnightly to Parliament. No good will be
done if the reports are merely made to the
Defence and Naval Departments. They are
simply pigeon-holed there.
McKissock, in the Senate, declared himself
against “conscription and cheap labor.”
Germany’s idea of what is a fair thing for
the North was shown by the fact that
when the Australians took Rabaul they found
the best-paid native labor there getting 10s.
per month. If McKissock knows any way
of preventing conscription and lOs.-a-month
labor than by fighting Germany, which stands
for these things, he ought to tell us.
To merely yell that he is against conscrip-
tion and cheap labor is just as sensible and
effective as it would be if he roared at an
oncoming mad bull that he was against being
gored by mad bulls.
[S. H. Pri®r, 214 George Street, Sydney.]
Givens, president of the Senate, took the
high road when the Referendum Bill was
before the Chamber last week. It is unusual
for the President to take part in a debate,
and with leading men in his party being
hurried to the guillotine for favoring con-
scription there was strong temptation upon
him to sit tight and express no opinion by
voice or vote. But that is not Givens’s way.
The occasion demanded personal decision,
and looking Labor between the eyes he made
an impassioned appeal for universal service.
Norman Bayles, M.L.A. for Toorak, asked
a sensible question the other day and
got laughed at for his pains. When Labor-
ite Prendergast’s Bill for the abolition of
aldermen was brought up and the Liberal
Ministry had given its blessing, Norman
arose and asked what was the use of passing
the Bill in the Assembly, since “everyone
knew it would be thrown out by the Coun-
cil.” Well, what is the use? But surely
Norman doesn’t propose to leave it at that!
If it’s no use the Assembly doing anything
of which the Council doesn’t approve, then
plainly, if we can’t spare the Council, the
only rational thing to do is to abolish the
Assembly.
The agreement made by the Tas. Govern-
ment with the Amalgamated Zinc Co. should
be satisfactory to the co., but is a very
doubtful one for the State. Carried off its
feet by the size of the business the Govern-
ment gave the co. a first call upon 50,000
h.p. at a price at which it is doubtful
whether current can be supplied: it is all a
matter of estimation, with very little to go
upon. The co. is given the right to sell
power to other people, and although it is
debarred from charging more than it pays
there are plenty of other ways in which the
co. can make money at the expense really of
the State. As the Baillieus’ co. has prac-
tically a monopoly of all power likely to
bo available for some time, any other co.
with a zinc process or other patent rights
requiring electrical energy would be at the
mercy of this powerful organisation. _ The
State has given it a splendid opportunity to
squeeze. The Labor Government purchased
from the original co. in order to prevent a
power-monopoly, and the present Govern-
ment has now handed over to a much
stronger co. practically the whole of the
available power.
A fragment of Pitt-street, Sydney, was
bought last week by an insurance co. for
£2OO a foot —bought from the family whose
Australian founder got it from the Govern-
ment as a grant. The family has added
about 2d. to the land’s original value. The
community has added the other £199 19s. lOd.
per foot. And now the community, having
created the £199 19s. lOd. worth, has handed
it all over to the people who' did the two-
penn’orth. It is a very mad community.
Efficiency and scientific training are fav-
orite catchwords nowadays; but the Vic.
Ministry does not confine itself to talk and
to getting Yankee and other “experts” to
give lectures. It shows the value it places
on knowledge by an advertisement in the
Government Gazette, offering £72 a year
to a man with “a sound training in theoreti-
cal and practical chemistry,” one who holds
certificates from a University or a recognised
technical school preferred. The maximum
pay for the job is £144 a year, or nearly £3
per week, and no mere scientist could want
more. Yet some people let their children
drift into jobs as wharf-laborers, where they
are harassed with as much as £1 a day.
In the Yie. Assembly a sensible proposal
by Toutcher to allow Australian wines to be
served with meals in hotels and restaurants
between 6 and 8 p.m. was brought for-
ward during the discussion on the new Early
Closing Bill. But Toutcher’s proposal was
trodden on with heavy feet, and Victoria was
told to go and tan its undigested meat with
tea and become a nation of dyspeptics.
TOO MUCH COOK.
New York, Wednesday.—A despatch from Berlin
to the United Press Agency says that the German
people are disappointed because there is no improve-
ment in the food situation. They declare there is
so much organisation that the food spoils.— Cable.
FRITZ : “Ach, such a big cook for so little
broth. ’E vill taste id all avay und I
ged noddings
To help Westralia struggle out of the pit
which Scaddan digged during his live years
of office, the Wilson Government proposes to
tax amusements. The first £15,000 or so
will be earmarked for the Repatriation Fund.
At latest, the show people were sharpening
their teeth on the property grindstone. But
it is too late. And, anyway, they benefited
as much as anybody by the bogus prosperity
which Scaddan’s borrowings created.
THE GLORIOUS LIBERTY TO BE A
COWARD.
“You little fool, ivhat did you want to fight,
for?”
“I didn’t want to fight, hut he hit me sister.”
“Hit your sister! Hit your sister! What
sort of an excuse is that? Don’t you
grow up to he a slave, my hoy, and
think you’ve got to fight just because
somebody’s hit your sister, or kicked
your mother or been a brute to your
wife!”
Victorian Statist Laughton has struck a
remarkable coincidence. Before the war
Australia got about £20,000 worth of cement
per year from Germany. She does not get
any now; but the imports from Denmark
have bounded up from £261 worth in 1914-
1915 to £20,502 in 1915-1916. Denmark is
next door to Germany. It will also, by the
way, be next door to Germany after the
war is over, when we shall be vainly trying
to shut out German goods by imposing
duties on goods imported from Germany.
“Croson”: While we are looking for “in-
creased national efficiency” what about adopt-
ing the decimal system? It would save years
of time in schools, offices, factories and else-
where. In fact, to a commercial office its
adoption would probably mean the saving of
the labor of one man or woman in ten. The
sole objection is the difficulty of inaugurating
it. But Japan did it, though its system of
weights and measures had been till then
almost as cranky as our own.
In Melbourne, at any rate, a section of in-
dustrial labor has something else to think of.
When, early this year, the State Government
gave the Eight-Hours’ Committee permission
to conduct its usual abominable Art Union,
it stipulated that “at least 33 per cent, of
the gross proceeds should go to charity, and
to this condition the committee agreed. The
gross proceeds were £4994, but the manage-
ment, thinking it didn’t matter, has got only
£663 available —exactly £lOOO short. And
now the pesky Peacock Government refuses
to wink its eye, and wants to know how the
committee proposes to make up the balance —
in notes or coin ?
Some people are quite candid, anyhow.
One Rhodes, the general manager of the big
Waihi mine, was before the maoriland Arbi-
tration Court the other day; and this was
part of his evidence :—•
Rhodes: We just consider the workers a piece of
machinery, something to buy and sell.
Then you would use the worker just as the car-
penter would his hammer or his chisel?
Rhodes: Precisely.
For an hour or two, and throw him aside without
further consideration until wanted again, just as a
carpenter would?
Rhodes: Exactly.
Then there is no sentiment as far as you are
concerned ?
Rhodes: Not a bit.
Some Labor people are saying that it would
be a dreadful thing if “conscription of
labor” by the State became a reality, and
men are asked not to let in the thin end of
the wedge. Well, the laborer has to work,
and The Bulletin has a notion that it
would sooner be obliged to work for the State
than for a person called Rhodes.
THE LAST OF THE MONMOUTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Knowing our chance of victory to be hopeless we
asked the Monmouth whether she could escape by
steering north-west. Captain Frank Brandt, her
gallant commander, replied: “My ship is making
water badly forward, and is unable to get away, so
I shall attempt to ram or torpedo one of the enemy.”
... .By this time night had come, and we could only
see the foe when the moon shone. Shortly after turn-
ing away we counted the flash of some 70 guns, all
directed at the gallant little Monmouth, and after
this we saw her no more. —Story of the Coronet action
by one of the crew of the Glasgow.
At the end of that dread day,
Rolling wounded in the sea,
Like some forest-creature grey,
Spitting bravely at ' on Spec—
That was what the Glasgow saw
Ere the night came down to hide,
And each gun-mouth, red and raw,
Cursed the Germans on the tide.
“Cannot run, so we shall ram
Or torpedo one,” they said ;
Then towards the wolves the lamb
Turned its battered, dauntless head;
Seventy guns spat out their hate
As the Glasgow turned to go,
And the Monmouth met her fate
With her bows towards the foe.
Sea and furrow, trench and wave,
Hide our brothers stark and stern !
To each land or ocean grave
We must adamant return,
As the gallant Glasgow did,
Leading out her sisters grey
In her cunning smoke-clouds hid
From Port Stanley’s sheltered bay.
Put the trumpet to the lip!
It will call us from our toil,
And the men for trench and ship
Will arise from out the soil.
Little voices call and craze,
But Australia’s eager ear
Waits impatient through the days
For the Great Call, brave and clear 1
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Last week’s expectation of this page that
Dan Seaton’s colt Eastcourt would do something at
an early date was translated into fact on Saturday
at the Hawkesbury. The Earlston youngster landed
the Guineas, with Agathon next. The difference
between the pair at the finish was a neck; at the
. start Earlston was favorite, and Agathon at 20’s.
Both are in the A.J.C. Derby on Saturday. Neither
indicated that the extra distance will worry them
much. This is going to be a great Derby. In
Wolaroi and Eastcourt, Seaton has a couple of good
strings to his bow. Of course, Wolaroi will be
favorite, but Isaac is not likely to open his face too
wide if anyone inquires about Eastcourt’s price. Many
shrewd judges give the Maorilander Kilboy a big
chance. One of them informed the writer the other
day that “this Kilboy’ll beat all the Wolarois.” As
it happens, there is only one Wolaroi. But there is
Bucklaw to beat, and Thana is a rare good filly. Also,
there are J. Wren’s Mudros, which is said to have
been backed, and one or two others. If Wolaroi
misses the ribbon, this page wiU be short a dollar.
“Mujik”: Blague, Hopfield and Polycrates
are on recent running the best-handicapped horses
in the Epsom, and in spite of all the yarns about
gallops on the track and all the rest of it, form
shown in public is always the best thing for the
punter to take notice of. This holds good even
against such reversals of form as a Sydney nag
lately showed within a week by running last and then
first. It is because he is usually a rotten judge of
form and has a wide ear for stories about some-
thing else that the average punter gets rid of what
he toiled for. In the Tramway Handicap at Tatter-
sail’s meeting Polycrates made a run over the last
furlong which stands out by itself as the most
brilliant hurry-up seen on the Randwick course for
a long time. He carried Bst. 111 b. In the Epsom
he has Bst. 61b. Hopfield was up with Woorak at
the mile post in the Chelmsford Stakes with 9st. lib.
aboard. In the Epsom he has Bst. 131 b. Blague
(9st. 31b.) was doing well when Cetigne (9st.)
ended the argument about the Spring Stakes at
Rosehill. In the Epsom he comes down 131 b., and
Cetigne goes up 61b. The person who can sort out
this lot of form and lick it into proper shape is
going to feel pretty good for at least 2sec. while the
Epsom is in progress. If the handicap for the race
had to be made to-morrow none of these nags would
be on the mark they are.
* * * *
It is more likely the Metropolitan Handi-
cap run at Randwick on Monday will be won by
something unthought of than that the same thing wiil
happen rin the Epsom. Some of the prads engaged
are going well. Prince Bardolph is one ; Cagou, who
has already won the race, showed such good form at
Rosehill that he cannot be overlooked. William the
Silent has had so much rest to have a leg repaired
that he may not get on to the course as satisfied with
himself as he should be. It is a race that has in-
. finite possibilities, and The Bulletin merely re-
marks that Prince Bardolph and Cyklon and Kings-
burgh are in it, and that the punter who happens to
get on to the winner will have a lot more luck than
if he backed something else. The best way to pick
the Metrop. is to toss the names of the likely-looking
into a hat, draw one out, back it for eighteen-pence,
and hurriedly take refuge from temptation in the
grandstand, where the worst can be waited for with
confidence.
When this page was hurrying to press
there was much wonderment about how many horses
would get to the post at the A.J.C. meeting with four
legs or without something wrong with their innards.
There has been much mystery. Woorak was disin-
clined lately to finish his breakfast, and Ikey was
ready for a day or two to do business on that basis,
but as the chestnut flier hopped over a mile on Tues-
day in “41J” it seemed that he was feeling reason-
ably well. Polycrates had the brand of suspicion
dangling to his tail, and on Monday and Tuesday
took things very easy. And Costello, having col-
lided with some timber last week, was missing on
Tuesday from the tracks. Garlin has undoubtedly
got a leg, but so far as could be seen early in the
week he didn’t appear to know it. And on top of
these incidents the weather prophets have beer, lugu-
briously talking about copious rain at the end of
the week. A softer track for the Epsom would be
joyful for some owners, but only Ikey knows what
the backers of Woorak would do- if they saw him
going out with lOst. 41b. to run in mud. Most of
them would wave a sad farewell to their quids to
start with, anyhow. The nag on which Luck perches
behind the jockey in this year’s Epsom is going to
land the bacon.
* * * *
There was a whole heap of merit attached
to Lady Minnie’s success in Hawkesbury Spring Han-
dicap on Saturday. As the distance, li furlongs, was
considered beyond her, particularly as she had tired
to nothing at the close of the recent Tattersall's
Spring Handicap, mile and a quarter, won by Fan-
tarie, Ikey became absolutely reckless and offered to
lay B’s. The mare slipped away from the bunch
going out of the straight, and was not caught until
50yds. from home. It was Fantarie, the favorite,
who ranged alongside; but the mare stuck to him
and had her head in front at the post. The incident
revealed a gameness in the lady hitherto unsus-
pected. Minnie lost nothing by having Foley to
assist in keeping her together. There were some
Metrop. candidates behind the pair, but for all the
chance they have at Randwick they might as well
be kept in their stalls.
* * * *
Sydian, one of Baronet Cooper’s imports,
won his first Australian race on Saturday. It was
not the first race he had been expected to win, and
it wasn’t much of a race,-anyhow. At one time the
Rowley Mile at the ’Awkesbury was regarded as an
Epsom trial, but it was not that last week. With
7st. 131 b. aboard Sydian hopped away at the start
and beat a-moderate to third-rate field in “40J.” In
the Epsom Bombita has 7st. 91b., in the Metropoli-
tan 7st.. 61b., and an entirely different class to battle
with. On the Hawkesbury running the chances of
Inia and La Balfour in the big races at Randwick
look about the same as the Hun’s hopes of salvation.
Sydian won in the only way he apparently likes to
do it —with no other nag interfering.
“Carfax”: It is a national misfortune
that William the Silent happens to be dumb. A
speech from William in the present crisis would be
as important to Australian punters as orations from
various other Bills. William was lame after the
Rosehill meeting, and since then various stories about
him have been authorised in reference to the Metro-
politan Handicap. Also he has been swimming.
William seems unlucky. Something of the same kind
occurred to him just before last Caulfield Cup. He
went swimming then, too, and wasn’t going to start.
But he did start and ran a great second to Levando
in the fastest time for the event. Swimming may
be good for William.
* * * *
The Sydney tracks have been as hard as
flint these last few weeks, and Victorian trainers
are wondering how many of their horses are going
to pull through the A.J.C. meeting without crutches.
The people behind the Melbourne Cup fav-
orite, won’t risk it, so he has been sent south to
get something softer for his feet than can be secured
north of the Murray. Lingle is apparently very well.
He is not being hurried in his preparation, which
is at least something for horse and backers to be
grateful for. But the habit of winning races on the
training track and losing them when there is cash
at the end seems likely to last for more than another
season. Heaven knows what the cost of it is to
owners and the public, but it must be something
fairly solid.
* * * *
It isn’t often that E. A. Connolly, the
Melbourne trainer, ventures far from his own dis-
trict with a team of “good things,” everything of
Connolly’s being popularly suspected of goodness.
But he crossed over to Sydney a month ago in charge
of Costello, Meroo and Grand Fleet, and his Mel-
bourne followers are anxiously awaiting develop-
ments. Up to time of writing two of the three
aforesaid had been backed down to 5 to 2 for races
in which they ran badly. Costello and Meroo may
assert themselves at Randwick, but it looks as though
Ilcey Mo has lost some of his respect for the English
horse, at least.
AT FLEMINGTON.
0.K.” : The gees that went from Mel-
bourne with designs oil the A.J.C. meeting seem to
have lost many of their friends of a month ago. They
went across to complete their preparations, and the
awful hardness of the tracks has given some of them
pause and knocked others clean out. At time of
writing the hopes of Victoria are clinging desperately
to Costello for the Epsom, and Bucklaw is said to
be a promising Derby colt.
Bob Ellis, an old-time jockey who had a
big innings when James White’s Kirkham stable was
gathering up the plums, weighed out for the Final
Handicap lately. He won the A.J.C. and V.R.C.
Derbies on Nordenfeldt, the A.J.C. St. Leger on
Melos, the Ascot Vale Stakes on Uralla and Penance,
and ihe.Maribyrnong Plate bn lolanthe. Notwith-
standing all his Wins; Bobby went on taking ordinary
size in hats.
* * * - *
The September blood stock sales in Eng-
land realised 86,863 guineas, or £36,000 more
than in 1915, the average per nag rising from 196
guineas to 302. A filly by Spearmint topped the
list at £4200. -Of course it is argued that the prad
buyers are betting on an early peace. At the 1913
sales £225,000 was extracted from hopeful pur-
chasers ; and if buyers didn’t know then (as evi-
dently they didn’t) when war was going to start,
they are probably not much better informed now
about when it is going to stop.
* *
Kitchener was fond of a mount on a race-
horse on big occasions. At the Delhi Durbar, on
King Edward’s coronation, he rode the American-bred
Democrat, which as h*tWo-year-old had pretty well
swept the board IS England for Lord William Beres-
ford. At King George’s coronation procession in
London he rode Moit'aa, the Maorilander which won a
Liverpool Grand National for Spencer Gollan, and
was sold by him to King Edward. The Calcutta
statue of Kitchener shows him astride of Democrat.
There were 103 applicants, one of the
number being a woman, for the recently-vacant posi-
tion of V.R.C. stipendiary steward —-or stewardess.
The choice fell on a Sunbury man, W. H. Johnston,
a relation of the County Court Judge. He used to
be an amateur rider, one of his mounts being the
well-remembered mare Phyllis. The screw is £7OO
a year.
* * * •*
The solemn people who want to “put
down gambling'” might find some exciting occupation
in France just now. Especially among the Tommies.
When a regiment is going into action the men form
groups, and each group puts its cash belongings into
a pool. The money is left behind in the care of
someone who doesn’t happen to be fighting, and after
the battle is split up among the survivors. This is a
surer way of making money than backing favorites,
for you draw a dividend every time you ask for it.
The heavyweights had a night out at
Sydney Stadium- on Saturday, Les O’Donnell and
Tom McMahon, of U.S.A., providing the gore. There
was plenty of it, for early in the proceedings Thomas
commenced to bleed copiously. He didn’t look much
of a boxer at the start, and had frequent collisions
with a straight left. But he had a powerful wallop,
and when he started to deliver it on O’Donnell s
diaphragm the receiver felt like the walls of Jeru-
salem when Marcus Superbus or someone dashed
forward with a battering-ram. Les stood these
bashes with the fortitude of a conscientious objector
until he couldn’t stand them any more, and then he
commenced to peter out. He was all to loo’ard by
the tenth collision. The Yank then obligingly
“handed over a swipe on the ear, and O’Donnell’s
agony was over.
* *■ * *
N. S. Wales pug Jack Darcy had a fur-
ther argument with the Speck’s best heavyweight,
Court Oakes, at Hobart the other night, and it
wasn’t long before streams of gore added to the
landscape that touch of realism which always de-
lights the anaemic onlooker. Oakes stopped some-
thing sudden in the second round, and rested for
nine. Thenceforth he was mostly scattered about the
floor in more or less untidy attitudes. After 10
weary rounds of sloganbash he ran up against one
that caused him to lose all further interest.
* * * *
Fred Kay and' Art Magirl exchanged
pleasantries at Melbourne on Saturday night. Kay
had built up lOst. 131 b. to meet the broadside of
list. 2Jib. The extra cargo slowed him down. For
eight rounds he performed his elusive act and Magirl
bashed vigorously at the enemy’s last-known address.
Then the Yank took a derry, forced his attentions
on the wanderer’s head and body and endeavored to
administer the anaesthetic at close quarters. Kay
stuck out the 20 rounds and just missed first place.
* * * *
There seems little doubt that Freddy
Welsh is the topnotch performer among the world’s
lightweights. His last two fights suggest that the
young man had been foxing. For instance, in a pre-
vious bout with Benny Leonard it looked almost a
certainty that the Hebrew was Welsh’s master, and
their coming together again put a big supply of cash
into the ticket office. Apparently that is what the
guileless Welsh had been waiting for. But he made
it as plain as a dollar that Leonard was not in the
same class. Yankee-paper reports of the conflict
show that Welsh was on top all the way. Leonard’s
Claim to be looked on as a possible winner of the
championship fell like the price of ice in winter.
* * * *
The late B. R. Wise was at Rugby when
he became notable amongst athletes. His first feat
in England was to win the School Distance Diving
•Competition in 1873, his brother being second. In
1876 he developed first-class ability as a runner and
next year won the mile in 4min. 44sec., the half-mile
on the flat and the half-mile over hurdles. He
then went to Oxford, and in the Pembroke College
Strangers’ Race, with 180 yards start, he won the
mile easily in 4min. 26sec. In 1878 he was hard at
work for a University prize, which he ultimately
gained, and had to train at the same time for the
annual sports. That time he overdid it and had to
lay up for nearly four months. In 1879 with 100 yards
start he won the three miles handicap at Cambridge.
C. N. Jackson, of Hertford College, had Wise nomi-
nated to compete for Oxford against Cambridge in
the Inter-University Sports. He started for the mile
race ‘and, making all the running, won by four yards
in 4min. 34 4-ssec. On the following Monday he
started for the One Mile Amateur Championship of
England, and beat 11. C. Jenkins and C. J. Johnstone,
winning easily by 10 yards in 4min. 29sec. In the
following season he won the half-mile at his own
college sports, and also the One Mile Race at the
University Sports, leading from start to finish and
winning in 4min. 26see. He also ran against Cam-
bridge, and was successful in the mile race, time
4min. 28 4-ssec. In 1881 he again competed for
the Amateur Mile Championship of England, and
this time was opposed by the celebrated W. G.
George, who afterwards went to America and
defeated Myers. Several others started, but the
finish was left to Wise and Georg'e, the Australian
winning easily by five yards in 4min. 24 2-ssee.
This was his last and certainly his best performance.
Mr. Wise was also a good cricketer and played for
his college eleven in 1880.
* # * •
Amateur athletics in Sydney owed some-
thing to the late Bernard R. Wise. Concealed in
knickerbockers, boots and stockings, Wise was among
“the pack” which followed a trail laid by Dick
Coombes and' J. Martin for the N.S.W. first cross-
country championship in ’B9. Alas, many of those
who started in that event are frisking round in
another sphere. Wise’s sympathies were always with
amateur athletics, and his advice was behind many
of the steps taken to put the A.A.A. on its feet.
• * • *
W. P. O’Neill’s success in the 10-mile
cross-country race the other day dispelled all doubts
about his right to be labelled Victoria’s distance
champion of the season, even though the champion-
ship for that distance has bee,n in the ice-chest since
Wilhelm broke loose. O’Neill spent 65min. 43sec.
of his life on the journey, and had a quarter of a
mile to spare at the finish.
Another of England’s old-time cricketers
has just been stumped—Alfred Lubbock, one of four
brothers who did things with bat and ball. He was
71. Half a century ago he hit up a century for an
English team against Middlesex, IV. G. Grace, then a
boy of 17, assisting with 75. In the following month
he put 107 on the slate for the Gentlemen against the
Players. Another Cold Land cricketer to go is
Kenneth Hutchings, but it was a shell that ended
him. Hutchings was a remarkably fine bat. He was
in Australia with Jones’s Eleven of 1907-8, and his
29 innings showed an average of 34.03 runs. His 126
in the second test, played at Melbourne, was the
greatest individual accumulation of runs made by an
Englishman in tests during the tour. Hutchings was
34. He went to the Front as a ranker and earned a
star.
* * * *
Norman Broughton, killed in France, was
a fine athlete. After leaving Sydney Grammar he
shifted to the ’Varsity, and there took the “blue”
at football and cricket. He had only had 28 years
of life when he crossed the line.
* * * *
Most of those Australian cyclists who are
pursuing the elusive dollar in Yankeeland have so
dug themselves into America that their claims to be
regarded as Cornstalks are beginning to evaporate.
McNamara, Alf Grenda, J. Clark and E. Pyo have
achieved families as well as currency, and Clark has
annexed a pub as a side-line.. As it will be a few
weeks before a crust can be captured in Australia
by professional wheeling, these ex-Australians _ are
not likely to hurry back to look for what isn’t
available.
* * * *
W. Abotomey put up the next-to-impos-
sible in winning City Tatt.’s (Sydney) billiard tour-
nament last week. Nobody imagined that he had
better than Buckley's chance when the handicapper
isolated him on the “600-behind” mark; but by
mixing grit and high-class play he defeated D.
Totten (received 85) in the final and took the dross.
Abotomey gets 800 start in 4500 from Frank Smith
in a match at Sydney this week. Whoever runs out
first overtakes £ 50.
* * * *
Ben Thoroug'hgobd, who got a well-bulged
purse pushed into his hands at Newcastle on Satur-
day, has been dabbling his oar in N.S.W. water for
more than half of his 47 years. In “best boats”
Ben has had some fine successes, two of the best
against Fogwell and Whelch (1908) ; but it was in
the more-recently-fashionable “heavy boats” that he
excelled. When Big Ben, in a 22-footer, went after
the Australian championship he was a certain winner.
In winning the Australian Rules goal-
kicking championship at Sydney on Saturday, Jim
Ashley (Balmain) gave a glimpse of that booting
which won him many cheers last season in S. Aus.
He sent the bladder on a journey of 63yds., the
nearest effort to this being 61yds. 7in. by J. Taylor.
The League “challenge final,” decided the same
afternoon, was a bitter struggle, in which Padding-
ton defeated Balmain by 32 points to 14. As Pad-
dington also defeated Balmain in the “first” final the
previous week-end by 50 to 22 there’s not much
doubt about the pennant.
* * * *
White Wings were unfurled on Sydney
Harbor for the first time this season on Saturday,
when the Flying Squadron and Sailing Club
—which have the same fleet —held a combined race.
There were two heats and a final, over the usual
triangular course. Mississippi in the first scramble
looked like being home and dry before the others
reached half-way, but did so badly near Shark
Island that her chance disappeared. A like perform-
ance in the final ended this speedy craft’s
hopes. Rosetta romped away with both the second
heat and the final, again proving what a fine hard-
weather craft she is. H. Collins, a former dinghy
skipper, landed Sunny South into second place in
the second heat. But, though beaten this time, the
Coonland craft should be heard from later.
* * * *
Few of Adelaide’s lost men will be more
missed than “Pepper” McKail, dead in France. He
was a prominent North Adelaide footballer after
leaving St. Peter’s School, where he had captained
both football and cricket teams.
N.S.W. Fisheries Department lias rolled
back its sleeves in an effort to keep trout-fishing
from petering out. This year Prospect hatchery
received 274,350 trout eggs, and the distributions
total 89,000 —Southern 46,200, Northern 19,400,
Western 16,000, North Coast 1400, hatchery 6000.
Last season there was a welcome slump in trout-
murdering, thanks chiefly to the.call of serious busi-
ness with Johnny Turk and Wilhelm. Another
“dead” season will give the trout youngster a chance
and make it unlikely that the rivers can be fished,
out for many years.
“W.A.”: The crowd was rushing witli
cash to the bookies when an able-bodied youth
knocked against an old chap and bowled him over
in the dust. The ancient picked himself up and
rose to heights of eloquence. “You etcetera,” he
yelled, “I’ll be even with you yet! I’ll vote ‘yes'
at the referendum.”
Mrs. Henrietta Anne Foott, who died
a few days ago at Woollahra (Sydney),
aged 94, arrived in 1853 with her husband
and spent many years pioneering ’way back.
She was the author of a book, “Sketches
of Life in the Bush,” published in Sydney
in IS7B. Another of the Foott family,
Mary Hannay, author of “Where the Peli-
can Builds” and other poems, assisted the
Rev. J. S. Hassell with his now-valuable
book, “In Old Australia.”

Including Governors there are about 75
Knights in Australia. The great Griffith
is tue senior of them all, though there are
a number of older men in that branding
yard. Octogenarians who are knights are
Henry Stephen of Sydney 87, Arthur
Snowden (Vic.) 87, Edwin Smith (S.A.)
85, Philip Fysh (Tar.) 81, Justice
a’Beckett (Melb.) 80, J. R. Fairfax
(N.S.W.) 81, and Samuel McCaughey
(N.S.W.) 80.
One duchess of Toorak rolls up her expen-
sive sleeves and acts as scrub-ladv for the
Empire. It’s not brilliant scrubbing re-
garded from a trade standpoint; but the
private hirer of domestic labor couldn’t get
her for a fee of 100 guineas per half-day.
Of course such dear things honestly believe
they are “doing their bit,” and they would
merely frown if you told them that under
real national organisation the citizen would
be put on the job that he (or she) could do
best.
Some of the white-handed patriotic
workers engaged on light honorary duties
are more amusing than the current vaude-
ville shows. They will tell you “the work
nearly kills us”; but let a willing volunteer
who hasn’t dukes in her family-tree try to
come in and she will be beaten back like
the Prussian Guard. There are probably
soldiers’ wives and mothers to be found who
would be very grateful to anybody who
would give them a hand with the youngsters
or the housework once in a while: also,
there is regular money to be earned for
patriotic funds in well-to-do households that
are short of help. But you don’t catch any
she-patriots fighting round that sort of
thing!
An Inspector-General has to take a more
or less intelligent interest in everything he
rakes with his eye. One night lan Hamilton
was inspecting the Electrical Engineers at
Hobart. When he arrived in one of the
searchlight emplacements he glanced about
him, cleared his throat and bent a piercing
gaze on a sapper. “Now, sapper, what would
you do if —er —supposing—er—supposing the
—er —light failed suddenly?” The mere sap-
per wasn’t half so overcome as he ought to
have been. “AVell, sir,” he replied smoothly,
“I’d try to get it going again.” “Yes, yes,”
said Hamilton, hastily searching himself
for something penetrating. “But, what
would you do to get it going again?”
“Well, sir. that would really depend
on what went wrong.” “Ahem ! Ahem !
Quite so, quite so.” said General lan hur-
riedly. “Er —that’s all I wanted to know” ;
and followed by his staff he hastened off to
complete his military education elsewhere.
“Marianne” : We still teach French from
German text-books, and at least one univer-
sity in Australia specifies a French grammar
compiled by a German, in which German
and not French nomenclature is employed
in dealing with verbs. English French is
bad enough, but German French just now
is bitter irony.
From the Office Curate :
Bishop Golding-Bird, of Kalgoorlie (WA.), who
has been giving this country the usual bad adver-
tisement by moaning in the London press about the
poverty and godlessness of his diocese (B. 14/9/’l6),
was 10 or 12 years ago a curate at All Saints’,
Margaret-street, London —one of the very “highest”
of fashionable churches in the West End, full of
chasubles and incense and other things that keep
Archgaiters Wright awake o’ nights. From there
Golding-Bird tried on his first ecclesiastical spats
as nominal Dean of the Falkland Islands—in reality,
a sort of pannikin boss over the chaplains of
English congregations in the Argentine, Chile and
Peru. As Anglicanism claims to be part of the
Catholic Church, it doesn’t appoint rival bishops in
Catholic countries or proselytise the Catholic popu-
lation, but only provides parsons for its own adhe-
rents. There being no British possession in the
lower half of South America, the bishop and dean
who look after Anglican stragglers halve to take
their titles from the Falklands. Bird was imported
in 1909 to Newcastle (N.S.W.) as dean of the
local cathedral. As a lot of the miners used to roll
up at his men’s services on Sunday afternoons for
want of something better to do on the day of gloom,
Bird began to fancy that he had a great influence
with them, and tried to interfere in the big coal
strike of a few years back, but was promptly told
by Peter Bowling that he was “a bumptious new-
ehum” and should mind his own business. He was
chosen to wear the gaiters at Kalgoorlie when that
see was formed in 1914. As to “J.F.H.’s” remarks
on establishing bankrupt bishoprics which have to
live on the cadge, pretty well every Australian
Anglican knows why there was such hurry to start
extra bishoprics in W.A. The idea was to make a
“province” with four sees, thus causing Perth, under
Anglican law in the Commonwealth, to become an
archbishopric; and the hurry was to get the title of
archbishop for Dr. Riley, of Perth, before the Pope
gave it to the R.C. Bishop Clune. After all the
scramble, Arcbgaiters Wright held up the scheme
for some months on a legal technicality, and “Rome”
got in first. Now two worried bishops—Trower at
Broome and Golding-Bird at Kalgoorlie—have to
keep the hat constantly circulating in an effort to
save their jerry-built sees from collapse.
IN THE OTHER MOTOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Whirring along the narrow road,
Our flier hooting close behind it,
The slow old “model” with its load
Rolled on and didn’t seemed to mind it.
A scrap of veil outside the hood
Signalled a welcome. AVas she pretty ?
I tried —as any traveller would —-
To see as we raced from the city.
Then as we passed them with a roar
I saw her face. Yes, pretty— very!
I wondered if we’d met before
And why her eyes were bright and merry.
I wished her car a turn of speed,
And longed to find it singing past us.
No luck ! We sizzled in the lead.
Off that straight road they’d never blast us.
They never caught us up. I looked
Behind and saw it take a side-road ;
My sentimental goose was cooked
AVhen we raced past them on the wide road.
All ways branch off. Mere speed and fuss
Are not the assets I can treasure
AA'hen Beauty loiters in a ’bus
And bids you woo her at your leisure.
N.s.w.
E. O’F.

How to Treat
Your Hair and
Complexion.
A Few Simple Beauty Hints.
By Mdlle. GABY DESLYS, the well-known Parisian Actress.
YOU ask me for a few hints on the
treatment of the hair and complexion.
Well, the less “treatment” you give
the skin the- better. I do not -
believe much in massage, but a
little cream to the face is neces-
sary to counteract the effects of
wind or sun. What cream
would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to use a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after
washing the face. Rub it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. You will find that
this will be the only “treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-
3$
Photo: Wrather & Buys.
thing is a good shampoo. Never use any-
thing inferior to wash the hair with. Get
some good stallax from your chemist and use
a teaspoonful in a cup of hot
water. Then rinse the hair
well and it .will look bright and
glossy.
A tonic is necessary when the
hair is inclined to fall out more
than it should, and is always
good to use during the spring
and autumn. Then the hair
needs a little —what do you call
it? —stimulant, and for this I
would advise you to get a packet
of boranium and mix it with
some bay rum ; dab this into the
roots and it will not only stop
the fall, but make your hair
grow long and thick. Give your
hair a good brushing every night, and that
will be all that you need do.
looking for all your life. The mercolised
wax removes all the dead outer skin, so
that you have always a fair, fresh com-
plexion like a girl’s.

Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. This
simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old=fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
There are plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite and mix it with
three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair with a small sponge for a few nights
and the greyness will gradually disappear.
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the
formula.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
The most Effective Formula ever Discovered,
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove ih<> hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
applied directly to the objectionable hair
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair but also to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Good News for Fat People.
Something New in Obesity Cures.
A London chemist says: “The latest
method of reducing obesity certainly is far
more pleasant and convenient than all pre-
vious methods. It consists merely in eating
clynol berries. The fat person who wants
to reduce without the usual rigid diet, exer-
cise, sweating baths, etc., now puts a few
of these little brown berries in his or her
pocket and eats three or four each day.”
Clynol berries not only eliminate fat from
the body, but also correct the tendency,
which is usually constitutional, to create
fatty matter. No discomfort whatever is
caused by their action; in fact, except for
the loss of superfluous fat and the feeling
of “fitness” so created, you would not be
aware that these little berries were doing
their work.
Local inquiry shows that clynol berries are
not very well known in this country, but
the demand is increasing daily,. and any
chemist can quickly procure them if specially
requested to do so.

“Pint Pot”: George Dibbs is not the only
Australian public man who has been mis-
taken for one of his own employees (B.
14/9/M6). Judge Fitzhardinge whenever
possible gets into his oldest clothes and
works in his orchard at Pennant Hills
(N.S.W.). A traveller humping Matilda
hailed him across the fence one day, and
taking him for a gardener asked him. “Any
chance of a bit of tucker'?” “Oh, I think
so,” said the Judge, “go up to the house
and ask the missus.” A while later the
traveller reappeared, and holding up a \tell-
filled bag called out, “You must have a
pretty fair thing on here, mate; the old
girl’s a good sort. Been with her long?”
“Yes,” said the Judge meditatively, “a good
while now. She’s not a bad sort.”
From “The Oldest Inhabitant”
Another of the many buildings erected by Lachlan
Macquarie 100 years or more ago is in the hands
of the destroyers. In its earliest days'it was the
residence and office of the “Secretary to the Gover-
nor” ; later it became the Colonial Secretary’s Office;
yet later it was the headquarters of the Public In-
struction Pep* irtment. Its first occupant was pro-
bably .1. T. Campbell, picked up by Macquarie at
Capetown, and on the recommendation of the ; Gover-
nor there appointed Secretary. Subsequently he
became one of the founders of the Bank
of N. S. Wales, which celebrates;: its cen-
tenary next year. Following Campbell, at
some distance —Major Goulburn in between—
was Alexander Macleay, succeeded by Edward Deas
Thomson, who held the post until Responsible Gov-
ernment made the Colonial Secretary a political
office with a portfolio. Colonial Secretaries from
Stuart Alexander Donaldson down to Henry Parke*
had their dens there until the new buildings on the
Macquarie-street corner were raised. In front of the
old building, before the erection of the present
Education Buildings in Bridge-street, were two huge
Moreton Bay figs. These in long years ago were
prepared for the axe, when old Jock Robertson came
along and in lurid language threatened the life of
the man if a limb of the tree were disturbed. But
when Jock was dead and Labor came to power, the
ancient trees were axed at a cost of ,L9O. and the
Wade Libs, made the matter one of their “texts”
against the Government.

'T'HIS charming selection perhaps not intrinsically a very
A great composition —has spread the sweetness of its
haunting strains over the whole world. It possesses a depth
of feeling so elusive that frequently the finer effects are
lost. In its execution the expression is its all.
With a Beale Player-piano you will
find that it is not at all hard to play
music with characteristics similar to
the “Barcarolle.” It is an instrument
essentially responsive to one’s best
musical thoughts, and is so sympa-
thetic that complete, soui-satislying
rendition becomes almost instinctive.

THINNESS IS DANGEROUS.
EMINENT SPECIALIST TELLS HOW
THIN PEOPLE MAY PUT ON FLESH
AND BECOME STRONG, PLUMP AND
HEALTHY.
Thinness is unnatural and dangerous. It
is not necessarily the cause of consumption,
indigestion, liver or catarrhal trouble; but in
nearly every instance it is an indication that
something is radically wrong. Very thin
people may feel that they are perfectly well;
but after thirty years of active experience I
can truly say that I have never seen an
extremely thin man or woman who was really
healthy. Thin people are thin because they
do not assimilate their food properly. They
may eat heartily, but their food does uot
make flesh, simply because the assimilative
organs are lacking in nerve force. This is
why thin people are so often nervous, sleep-
less, and become addicted to the use of drugs.
To supply this nerve force a nerve food must
be taken, and I have had remarkable results
in nearly every case from the use of a tonic
nerve-builder which contains no harmful
drugs, consisting of ioz. Tincture of Carda-
moms Compound, 8.P., 2oz. Salrado Com-
pound, 3oz. Elixir Simplex, and enough
water to fill an Boz. bottle. One to two
tablespoonfuls should be taken before each
meal, or whenever excessive fatigue or depres-
sion is felt. Any chemist can readily prepare
this formula, and as it is widely recognised
for its unusual efficacy many chemists now
keep it in stock ready for use under the name
of Salrado Tonic. The increase in weight
following the use of this tonic is remarkable,
the nervousness, sleeplessness and other
unnatural symptoms all disappear; dull eyes
become bright and pale cheeks glow with the
bloom of perfect health. —A. E. M. —Advt.

IN BANANA LAND
Premier Ryan recently said there was
“so much money lying in the English banks
that the directors do not know how to get
rid of it.” And Ryan apparently reckoned
from this that anybody who looked like a
borrower was simply chased. As a matter
of cold fact, there is a lot of money in Eng-
lish banks, and that is so simply because
investors are not lending. If he wants con-
firmation he’d better ask Holman.
According to family records, John
Connolly, the grand old man of Gayndah,
has just climbed into his second century.
John has put in 64 years in Australia, and
has been, by turn, storekeeper, station-
owner, copper dealer, clerk of the Shire,
J.P., returning-oflicer and mayor of the
town. He wrestled with the blacks in the
early days, but he now devotes his time to
chopping wnod and wrestling with the
climate.
So much as 50 years ago Bananaland was
paralysed by a strike. About 100 navvies
came down from Helidon, attempted to
broach the commissariat stores in Brisbane
and created a small riot. A Courier reprint
throws light on the rumpus:—
It has been found that members of the so-called
unemployed recently rioting in Brisbane and Ipswich
are persons possessed of property or other means,
placing them above the need of obtaining aid from
the Government. Perhaps this fact will account for
so many being sticklers for high wages. One indi-
vidual seeking relief work was found'to own nearly
half a street in Fortitude Valley.
Fat has been up to every form of villainy
in his day. Here we see him rioting for
higher wages while drawing rents from
streets of houses. But luckily there was a
paper called the Courier to show him up!
The luck of a Chow ! A soused Yangtze
person recently strolled along the corridor
of a Western mail train travelling at 20
miles an hour and walked right out. When
the police went to pick up the corpse a
couple of hours later China came joyously
to meet them. He was hardly scratched.
“Norbell”: Touching the copper-cadging
proclivities of the Anglican Church, I notice
that at the various rest huts for soldiers in
Queensland those of the C. of E. are the
only ones that have collection-boxes pro-
minently displayed. They are painted a
glaring signal-red. and are adorned with an
appeal in white letters begging Billjim to
contribute to the cost of the pen, ink and
paper.
“M.A.W.”: Now that the magnitude of
the prickly-pear problem is beginning to be
realised, it should not be forgotten that the
proper people to blame for its spread are
the party politicians. The leasehold squatters
may have been negligent, but even then it
was the landlord’s business to look after his
estates. Some of the runs repurchased in
recent years have not a pear on them, whilst
the Government land, roads and reserves
around are covered with the curse. The
fact is, no votes were to be got by attending
to the spread of the pear until the vegetable
had grown big enough to be a public ques-
tion. The Lands Department has the man-
agement of the public estate; but it is bossed
by a politician, so of course its energies are
absorbed in promoting party ends.
“Malbon”: What tough ’uns the Back-
blocks breed! W. W. Thomas was “Old
Bill” when I worked for him in Victoria
over 30 years ago. He was a grandfather
of a man when later on he was “leaded” at
Broken Hill. A wanderer, too, I found him
next at the Zapapan, in the Northern Terri-
tory, where the white man can’t etc. Where
he was meantime I don’t know, but die was
my boss afterwards at Chillagoe (Q.) ; and
when I moved south and west he was at
Cobar, and then became the first manager of
the Budgery mine. That petering out, he fled
north again to the place where the white
man can’t etc., and I heard of him manag-
ing the Daintree, 50 miles from the Einas-
leigh, which is 100 miles beyond the Sunset
in North Queensland. And I now strike
him as manager of the Consols mine at
Friezland. one of the Elliott Co.’s remote
ELOQUENCE IN THE DROUGHTY WEST.
“Yes, my friends, no rain has fallen for months, and the Heavens still frown down upon
us with a — er—with a smiling face. With no prospects of rain above us, we
meet to-day under a — er—heavy cloud”
shows —still “Old Bill,” still holding up his
head and drawing deep breaths of the Out-
back air, which is the only air such a man
can breathe in comfort.
“Fitzroy’s” view of it: —
“Dead Finish” (B. 28/9/’l6), holding forth about
the Bananaland Government classing and selling
small lots of wool for the grower, is barking up the
wrong sapling. One of the chief snags met by the
Dept, of Agriculture in showing that it is not neces-
sary to have a big holding to successfully go in for
jumbuck-raising has been the wretched price the
man with only a few bales of wool has had to
accept from private buyers. He has had to take 3d.
and 4d. per lb. for wool which if properly cata-
logued in a larger consignment would have brought
lid. and 12d. To beat the robbers the Government
now proposes to take over all these small lots of
wool, advance 50 per cent, of their value, pool the
lot, class it and give it a run for its money at the
big sales. There is to be no pooling of “star” lots,
but rather of “bag and butt” lots. The wool is to be
classed at the Brisbane Technical College, but the
classing will be done by men who know their job.
Moreover, the cocky is not to be charged 10 per
cent., but 10s. per bale, and even “Dead Finish,”
who apparently prefers to see the small cocky getting
a fourth of the true value of his wool, will admit the
reasonableness of that charge. As a fact, this move
of the Government is going to mean a big thing for
the small woolgrower, and a much bigger thing for
the wool industry generally.
Executive Gardens, Brisbane.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Slate-colored pigeons, pink of foot,
With pecking motion of the head,
Strut up. and down the sweep of shade
Where burn geraniums hot and red,
In striking contrast to the mood
Of grave Victoria the Good,
Erect in bronze of greyish-green,
Cold contemplator of the scene.
From the green square, the freezia flings
Bouquets of perfume down the wind;
A rose “Inconstant,” gold and flame,
Casts petals on the grass, to find
Perchance as other beauties may,
The permanency of decay ;
And challenging the placid street
Are captured guns, grown obsolete.
A sunless" seat against a wall —
The steps a rebel much prefers;
The great queen cannot block the sun,
Though sway across a world was hers....
Sometimes a cab rolls, rattling past,
Sometimes a motor in full blast,
And sometimes in the garden place
Love rests proud eyes on young Love’s face!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 23, 1916.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
The rival “queens” who seek coronation
on Carnival Day, in connection with the
Residential Club for Returned Soldiers, are
providing the only social excitement these
days. Last Tuesday night a bridge-and-
euchre party and a dance brought in about
£4O to the fund for the “Soldiers’ Queen.”
The “Sports Queen” (Mrs. Blume) began
her dash with a dance at Albion. An ama-
teur entertainment at 11. M. Theatre, a
shivoo on the Sandgate pier, and a night at
the Dandies are threatened for the near
future. The “Allies’ Queen” (Mrs. Giraud)
has been a dark horse until this week. Her
supporters will open with a cafe chanta,-t in
the Majestic on Friday, September 29. His
Ex. and his wife will pay homage. Miss
M. Brennan organised a euchre party for
the “Citizens’ Queen” (Mrs. Iletherington)
in the Temperance Hall on Tuesday night,
and the Japanese tea-house in Market Square
continues to steadily pile up cash and votes
for the lady. Dame Morgan is “Charity
Queen.” She will hold a reception at John-
son’s Cafe on October 4.
Miss May Power, hon. sec. of the Babies
of the Allies Clothing Fund, is on the look-
out for volunteers to make garments for the
toddlers. There has been so much talk of
what women could do if they were given a
chance that it seems a paradox to have to
appeal for volunteers for this good cause.
Yet willing workers have been scarce lately.
Little Grace Hughes, of Wooloowin,
murmured the usual oaths of allegiance to
W. T. Russell last Wednesday. Belgian
lace and satin were used for the bridal gown.
Two sisters, May and Annie Hughes, were
bridesmaids, and they wore palest pink and
blue. The newly-weds are honeymooning in
Sydney.
A big patriotic entertainment happened
at the Stadium on Wednesday night. Lena
Hammond’s contralto and Olive Clare
Brown’s soprano, Fred Watson’s basso and
turns from the Empire and Tivoli Theatre
filled up a good musical programme. Gladys
Frost was a useful and ornamental accom-
panist.
Last Tuesday Mrs. ' Ryan tea-ed' Mrs.
Holman and Louise Mack (Mrs. Creed) at
the Art Gallery. Our pretty Premieress
was in violet taffetas, with a bodice of
ninon of the same shade. A pale pink hat
lined with black made a fetching top-piece.
The Holman, lady was in navy blue and
white stripes over naVy blue taffetas, and
Mrs. Creed wore pale blue and white stripes
and white fox furs.
Lady Goold-Adams opened a new kinder-
garten at Paddington to-day. The school is
built on the summer-house plan, verandahs
all round, and walls consisting chiefly ol
doors and windows. Bishop Le Fanu, Home
Sec. Huxham and Donald Gunn, M.L.A.,
were among the prattlers, and Lady Goold-
Adams made one of the pretty little speeches
that the public expects.
Arthur Morgan to-day opened Bowman
Park, a beautiful reserve at Ithaca named
after the late David, once the big-hearted
leader of the Labor party. The Governor
was to have presided, but illness prevented
him doing so.
An “All Khaki” concert, at which every
performer was a volunteer, was held in the
Exhibition Hall to-night in aid of Y.M.C.A.
funds.
Mrs. E. L. Turner, of Auchenflower, has
just lost a brother, Sergeant Tom, at
Salonica, and a nephew, Arthur Watts, at
Ypres. But only a miracle could save Mrs.
Turner from mourning, seeing that she had
six brothers and ever so many nephews on
the big job.
Ipswich is doing itself proud to celebrate
the Military Cross won by Lieut. Trevor
Francis. He was a student at the Queens-
land Uni. when he enlisted, and like his dad,
J. Y. Francis, is an athlete of some tonnage.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Bimbashi”: Yet another Anzac, junior.
A number of Rockhampton youngsters un-
hitched a coal truck, pushed it up an incline
and then had the ride of their lives. Finally
one of the boys slipped and was crushed
between the buffers when the trucks crashed
together. For some time he lay in agony,
imploring his mates not to send for ( the
ambulance, but to bring him water. “And
don’t you tell me mother,” he begged. At
last he was able to walk home, helped by his
companions. He died next morning, after
suffering the agonies of ruptured kidneys and
spleen.
From an old Northerner: —
Buchanan, of Brisbane Daily Mail,, is making the
Courier sit up and look to its laurels. Buchanan’s
prompt and courageous dealing - with the Fihelly inci-
dent and his alertness and his determination not to
be tied to the wheel of any party-car are making
the Mail a power.
Fihelly, acting-Minister for Justice, let
himself go at a recent Irish Club meeting ;
and of course Tolmie had to play the party
game with a no-confidence motion. The
immediate result was seven hours of uproar
ending in the Government securing “confi-
dence” by 42 votes to 24. But the Cabinet
is sizzling, all the same; and between'
Fihelly and Adamson, who takes up a do-
your-worst stand in his advocacy of con-
scription, Ryan’s pillow is stuffed with dis-
comfort.
“Norbell” : They say it takes a ton of lead
to kill one man at the Front, and I believe
it. The recruits at the Rifle Range camp,
Brisbane, the other morning were held spell-
bound watching the gambols of a dog in
front of the targets. About 50 men were
training their Lee Enfields on those targets
at 500yds., and the bullets were clickety-
clacking on them like a score of typewriters.
But the dog frolicked up and down, snap-
ping at the whizzing bullets, and enjoying
himself thoroughly. After having the time
of his life for over half an hour he left the
targets and raced down the range towards
the firing party, under a curtain of Mark YI.
lead. Then he sat down derisively in
front of the shooters and joyously barked
his opinion of their marksmanship.

LL the bottles which carry “Light Crystal” are
thoroughly washed and sterilised in this room. First

soda. They remain in this some forty minutes. From here
they pass into clean water, J where they are thoroughly
scrubbed. The rinsing-machine follows, and then the
conveyor-belt brings them to the filling-machines. There
are th ree of these —with a capacity of 500 dozen an hour.

eminently satisfactory Summer beverage.

Ages of S. Aus. Judges: Chief Justice
Murray 53, Justice Gordon 65, Justice
Buchanan 68, Industrial Court Judge Jethro
Brown 48. Westralian Judges: Chief Jus-
tice McMillan 58, Justice Burnside 54,

If all the Tasmanian apples in London
brought the same price as a case sent over
by Percy Sargant there would be no grum-
bling. Percy used to be on the London Metal
Exchange before he took to apples, and he
shipped a case to be sold amongst his old
friends for the Red Cross. It fetched £125
55., the case brought £6 65., and the wrapping
paper £5.
Neville, of Westralia, is the most Chester-
fieldian Protector of Aborigines that ever
was. A stock-rider who followed in his
Kimberley trail relates that as he left each
cattle-station the aborigines were lined up
and Neville shook hands with the men and
raised his hat to the flabbergasted gins.
One of the last social accidents that hap-
pened before war began was the falling in of
Brassey during the jollification at the com-
pletion of the Kiel Canal. Brassey took the
old Sunbeam across to show the German
plotters that Englishmen were willing to be
friendly. But when he was keeping off his
rheumatism by pulling round the harbor one
morning in his dinghy, a German harbor
policeman arrested him for approaching for-
bidden waters. The mannerless Kaiser
chuckled over the joke that night at dinner;
whereupon the old ex-Governor up-anchored
in the grey dawn of next morning and fell
out altogether.
The Cataract Dam catchment, which tries
to fill Sydney's cup, isn’t the only area in
N. S. Wales that has missed the rains. The
gauge at Burrinjuck dam has registered
over 24 inches of moisture during June, July
and August; but on most of the 5000 sq.
miles of catchment the fall was from three
or four inches to a few points, and the
Federal territory, where Canberra lan-
guishes, is in the driest corner.
John Laver, a Melbourne timber broker
who for many years has had his eye to the
market keyhole, broke the news the other
day that for the year ended June 30 Vic-
toria’s timber-importations—largely soft-
woods —were nearly 2,000,000 super feet
more than the previous year’s. As the 1914-
1915 imports reached 86,000,000 ft., the State
Government had better throw its fear of
Socialism boldly out of the window and
reach after this trade by planting young
stock wherever it sees room to grow a tree.
At present it seems to hang an apology on
every seedling it trowels in.
“F. Born” : The Government proposes to
disfranchise, for referendum purposes, the
“German or enemy-born” residents of Aus-
tralia. Why? Because it is assumed that
they will vote anti-conscription, or against
the’ true interests of Australia. If this be
a valid reason for disfranchisement, why
should it not be applied to the 126,000 single
eligibles who wrote on their cards that they
were not willing to come forward to fight
for Australia?
The protection given by abnormal freights
has enabled the Bendigo potteries to extend
their business to things besides drain-pipes,
and they are now turning the Bendigo
“slimes” into teapots and coffee-jugs and
casseroles and a heap of other things. The
few people who went to a little pottery
show which the Vic. Arts and Crafts Society
held the other day in its loft at the top of
a Collins-street skyscraper got their eyes
opened to what Australia can do in pottery-
ware; and Miss Boyd, the enthusiast who
worked the wheel, does not see why she
should not produce even superfine china-
ware. Neither, by the way, did the
Bakewell firm in Sydney. But after it had
lost, according to faithful report, £25,000,
it decided that in Australia you can no
more make chinaware without a high tariff
than you can do it without clay.
“Billy T.”: Symons was expatiating on
the servant-girl probleln. “These girls drive
my missus mad,” he said. “We’ve had
about 20 in two years, and out of the lot
only one was even pretty good. Her name
was Millie. She did her work well, but was
peculiar. She would get, as it were, trans-
fixed in the middle of a job, and go on think-
ing till she was shook up and started again.
One evening I heard the‘missus give a yell.
The trouble was in Millie’s room. There
the girl was sittin’ on the side of the bed,
cuttin’ her throat.” Symons paused, exas-
perated. “What did you do, Symons?” I
asked. “Do?” he snorted. “I gave her a
week’s notice, of course !”
From “The Oldest Inhabitant”: —
An old Sydney estate comes under the hammer
at the end of October. In and before the ’2o’s one
William Macdonald was in the hardware line in
George-street, where he erected some shops and
dwellings known as “Macdonald’s Buildings.” The
western end of the Strand occupies the site. Nowa-
days 76 feet to George-street is let on building lease
for a term of 50 years from 1890 at £3OOO per
years and taxes. Another lot is right opposite—32
feet 8 inches to George-street, let on building lease
of 50 years at £5OO per annum and taxes. A third
lot is 249 George-street, “looking up Bridge-street,”
22ft. x 80ft., a 50 years’ lease from 1891 at £4OO
a year. But the best-known lot is Roberts’s Hotel,
on the north-east corner of Market and George
streets, let on a 50 years’ lease from January 1, 1885,
at £lBOO per annum and taxes. The Roberts
corner in the ’3o’s was occupied as an ironmongery
store. One .of the proprietors was John Thomas
Wilson, at whom an actress and vocalist of those
days cast the glad eye with the usual results. She
went to Calcutta and died three days after landing
there. Dr. Lang in The Colonist lampooned Wilson
in some stinging jingle, which caused Wilson to
horsewhip Bull, the publisher. Wilson then pur-
chased on credit a shipload of goods, chartered the
Nereus, rode down to the wharf, boarded the ship
and never returned. Dr. Lang said that he fled to
Java. In 1850 the Roberts family got possession
of the Market-street corner and opened a pub under
the name of Crown and Anchor, previously borne
by a hotel opposite the Post Office. The license has
been held by one or other of the family ever since.

The Story of the Lamp
cvs>
tm
1
l
/ ,77 1
Pi
First “Improved” Lamps,
1783 A.D.
In 1783, M. Argand, a Swiss chemist,
introduced a glass chimney and burner
with a circular wick, which supplied oxygen
to the flame and prevented smoke. One of
Argand’s workmen was holding a bottle
over the flame when the bottom of the
bottle fell out. He dropped the hot bottle
over the burner and a better and steadier
light was the result.
Successful lighting depends on the
steadiness with which a lamp burns.
Laurel Kerosene
The soft white light
being specially manufactured by white
labor for Australasian conditions, gives the
ideal light. Laurel burns without a flicker
and is brilliant, but restful to the eyes. For
lighting, heating and cooking, it cannot be
excelled.
Laurel is our new trade mark name for
the highest quality American Kerosene
ever sold in Australasia and replaces our
old brand, White Rose.
