The Inky Way.
Died the other day in London, George
Dull, a bright Australian journalist who,
20 years ago, while doing dramatic criticism
in Sydney, fell in love with a Gaiety Girl and
married her. After that Bull went to his
home-land, where he was a popular press-
agent for various theatrical managements,
and found time to write mumming and other
notes for the smart little London Opinion.
* * '* *
The austere Chief Justice Murray gave
stern sentences of three years and six years,
at the Criminal sittings in Adelaide, for
unnatural offences of various kinds. Next
morning the Register had an article—in its
agricultural columns—on “The Value of
Rape.”
* * * *
The other papers put it this way :
The report of the U.S. Bureau of Crop Estimates
says there is barely enough spring wheat for home
consumption.
But when they reached the S. M. Herald
an e and an l got tangled:—
The report of the U.S. Bureau of Crop Estimates
says there is barley and enough spring wheat for
home consumption.
And that made an e and an l of difference.
* * * «
“J.F.11.” from London:—
An interesting journalistic jubilee we’ve been cele-
brating is that of the Tailor and Cutter. Founded
50 years ago by a young Scottish journeyman tailor
named Williams, it was really inspired by Charles
Kingsley’s “Alton Locke: Tailor and Poet.” That
story revealed the terrible sweating conditions under
which the trade was carried on at that time —the
horrible dens in which women stitched day and night
for a few shillings a week, and men were paid tup-
pence-ha’penny for making a pair of pants with
tuberculosis germs in them. Williamson reinforced
Kingsley’s story with a pamphlet of revelations based
on his own personal knowledge, and followed it up
by organising a trade-union, with the Tailor "and
Cutter as its organ. The paper has alvvays been
solid on the “educational” side, publishing 'lessons on .
all departments of the business by experts. Shalc-
speare’s theory that “the apparel oft proclaims the
man” it holds to be incomplete. Always, not
often, says the Tailor. So Gladstone, although the
Liberal leader, was claimed as a Conservative on the
strength of his stand-up collars; and it wouldn’t have
believed he wasn’t a Tory even if he had blown up
the House of Lords.
* * * *
Randolph Bedford in reply :—■
Tardily, and in foreign parts, I read the talk of
the correctly-named “Copyboy” (B. 24/8/T6). This '
know-all know-nothing remarks that my qualities
as a writer emphasise my unfitness for a job with
a travelling commission which is still an unknown
quantity. He knows all that the job doesn’t ask for,
but apparently not what it wants. I say that the
job was cut and dried for a Melbourne reporter
weeks before the proposed appointment of the Com-
mission was published, and that nobody’s qualifica-
tions or disqualifications count for anything.
* * # *
''Kodak” : Latest Greek cable headlines—
Dimitrakopoulos—Looks Before He Leaps.”
Yes, and we journalists have to look before
we even spell! Lobsters to him!
* * * *
“First Old Man”: After all, you fellows
have nothing to complain of; the Turk in
Ins time has suffered quite as much. When
Sara Bernhardt visited Constantinople in
IJO4 she took with her half a dozen plays,
three of which were by the daddy of French
dramatists, Sardou. Five out of the six, in-
cluding ail Sardou’s, were prohibited by the
authorities—“La Tosca,” because a prefect
of police is killed in it; “Fedora” because
the subject is Nihilism ; and “La Sorciere”
because the Koran is mentioned in the text.
Of the others, Racine’s “Phedre” was for-
bidden because it was a Greek drama, and
Rostand’s “L’Aiglon” on the ground that it
was calculated to give the Sultan’s subjects
a false idea of European politics. The only
piece that passed the Censor was the old
Flossiedrama, “La Dame aux Camglins” !
* * * *
“J.F.11.”: Will Dyson is now entitled to
wear a halo. The London Christian World
hails him as the “most Continental and tech-
nically brilliant of all our cartoonists.” By
way of saying “Amen” London Daily
Chronicle declares every cartoon “a sermon
by a pitiless Calvinist.” The religious train-
ing these young fellows get on The Bur-
letin will tell.
CONTEMPORARY PORTRAITS: J. F. ARCHIBALD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He summed the world and all its din
A sing-le paragraph within.
A message from the dead
He- wouldn’t use, he said,
Unless sub-edited.
The Angel Gabriel sending in
An “Abo.” to The Bulletin
Would be blue-pencilled, too.
No words could be too few:
Most adjectives he slew.
He flayed the cuticle from Pride,
And paunched Pomposity he fried.
Neatly beneath the rib
Of Cant and Falsehood glib
He dug his polished nib.
He knew, but wouldn’t always pen,
The private life of public men.
Not with a club he smote,
But just a two-line note,
Or stinging anecdote.
Perched in his cafe window bright,
He saw all life as words to write,
This gallant Gallic wit,
This sword that neatly slit,
This butterfly that bit.
He brought to us the soul of France,
Her lucid look at Circumstance ;
And when he dies, the last
Bohemian here outcast
Will, with a quip, have passed.
There’ll be no weariness in Hell:
The anecdotes that he will tell—
From fear of libel freed—-
Of Henry Parkes and Reid !
(Who will the laughter lead!)
And Lucifer will pay his sub.
To be a member of his club.
And in that, causerie
I’m sure Eternity
Will pass .quite pleasantly!
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

Appassionato.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I.
IFEEL the fine wind on my cheek;
I turn to meet it with my mouth.
O wind from out the South,
Thou art his messenger
Upon my mouth !
I have been lifted up as a bird
On his wing
Because of a word;
Because of a whisper I heard.
The trees bent down to cover it,
The deep of the night to hover it;
Love spelled it, heart held it;
Now as a bird on the wing
In the Spring
Am I stirred.
Glad, glad is my heart!
(Sing, morning time, sing!)
Last night at the pane I heard the rain—
Rain that came from the South !
I gave it my cheek, and I gave it my mouth,
Because in the South were you.
Now morning is blue ;
And I, I sing like a bird on the wing!
I sing, and I call on the sky, and the sun
and the morning to sing;
And out of the window where slanted the
rain
I lean for the sun to lie on my heart
(Oh, close to my heart!),
The sun that is shining on yon.
Oh, blithe on the wing of delight
I soar, higher and higher,
Where-dreams give wine to desire —•
Wine to a mouth
Tingling to drouth
For one who is turning her face to the sun
and the wind from the South !
11.
Sun, let thy light go by!
Sky. lean down and hear my cry!
Darkness is on me deeper than night.
He is away.
He is awa3% and my hands have forgotten to
play:
My eyes that were bright as the light of the
stars in the night
Are wells of despair;
The surge of the blood
Is drowned in the dirge that is all I can
give to the day.
He is away.
O song that is measure of power! O grief
that darkens the hour !
Shall power go empty, for ever and ever,
Beating its cry on the air?
Song that is measure of me
Call to him tenderly,
Call him so longingly;
He will not stay
Always away.
N.S.W.
MART GILMORE.
The End of the Feud.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O one hut _ the two old men knew
I exactly how the feud began. It
had lasted for more than twenty
years, gaining intensity with every month
that passed, and so deen was the feeling
aroused that many outsiders feared it would
come to bloodshed in the end. They had
visions of a stray shot fired from behind a
patch of sandalwood, or a stirrup-iron
swung viciously when the two men met in
the road.
Denison owned four hundred square miles
of the best grazing country along the creek,
and he had worked most of his life to add
value to the place. A lean, grey-bearded
bushman of the old school, he was tough
as greenhide and active as a rock-wallaby,
and at sixty-five was still a hard rider
among timber. And though the place
turned over five thousand yearly he never
changed his moleskins more than once a
week. From the overseer down to the half-
caste horseboy, the men regarded him as a
good boss on the camp or in the yard, but a
hard nut to deal with in the matter of
money. It pained him to write out a
cheque or kill a fat bullock for beef.
On the edge of the run, by the stock-route
gate, lived Larry Burke, the mail-contractor.
He had been a mate of Denison’s thirty
years before, and had helped him sink tanks
along the droving-routes from the Dawson
to the Bree, working hard on contracts that
were little more than tucker-jobs. But
neither of them talked much of those days.
Larry was a thin man of Denison’s own
build, but without his extraordinary energy.
Most of the real work of the place, getting
meals ready for the coach passengers and
attending to the mails, was done by his
eldest daughter, Delia, while Larry pot-
tered about the swamp with a shot-gun in
search of ducks, or sat on the rails of the
yard with the Whelan boys, talking horse.
But in his gloomy moments he talked of
nothing but Denison, growling a little into
his beard and looking with sullen, vindic-
tive eyes at the wire fence that marked the
boundary of Maloola run.
“Luck, pure luck,” he would say. “That
and the instinct to grab. If he hadn’t
started by putting his brand on better men’s
cattle he’d be a poor man still. I could tell
you yarns about moonlighting.”
It was the same old story, over and over.
“Don’t you worry, Larry,” Andy Whelan
would assure him, “we’re getting some of
our own back now.”
Andy was a big fellow with a sun-cliapped
face and a good-humored grin. Somewhere
in the. ridges at the back of Denison’s run he
and his brother Steve lived by shooting and
trapping, their horses feeding on Denison’s
grass and drinking from his gilgais. But
on Sundays when the coach came in they,
were generally to be found at Burke’s, along
with a casual drover or teamster who hap-
£ en i , to be camping along the creek.
Burkes was. the centre of all the floating
I ire of the district, and there was always a
place for anyone at the table, with someone
in the corner playing the accordion, and
Delia and the other girls bustling round
and blushing at the men’s repartee.
But the feud was never entirely forgotten.
At Burke’s everyone was against Maloola
as a matter of principle, and if Denison’s
name was mentioned Larry always rose and
paced the room like a caged tiger, growling
into his beard. It was as if he felt that his
life at some point had gone unaccountably
askew. Why should he, who had been free-
handed and generous all his days, be in-
habiting an old bark shanty with hardly
enough grass to feed a herd of goats, while
Denison, whose meanness was notorious,
could run his five thousand cattle along
twenty miles of creek frontage and order
overproof rum by the case?
Sometimes Denison, riding out from the
township in the dark, heard the music and
laughter coming from Burke’s shanty and
was affected by the same kind of irritation.
There had never been any children in the
big, pise homestead at Maloola, and that
was a raw spot. And none of the stockmen
played the accordion in the Maloola hut at
nights.
Denison thought of the two girls, and the
Whelan boys, and all the casual bushmen
who hung around the place, and somehow
the blood grew hot about his temples.
“A crowd of lazy loafers!” he thought.
“I’ll get no peace till I’ve rooted out the
whole damn lot* of them.... I wouldn’t
wonder if they’re eating my beef now.”
But the process of rooting-out was a
long business. He had “pounded” the
Whelan boys’ horses, and they had retali-
ated by cutting his fences and letting three
hundred head of cattle out into the next
run. He had dammed a little creek that
ran through a corner of Larry Burke’s
selection, and Burke had shot one of his
finest bullocks and left it lying in the road.
There had been a system of mutual reprisals
extending over many years, each man
pressing others into his service and growing
more bitter with advancing age.
_ In the middle of the mulga, two days’
ride from the nearest township, the two
houses stood confronting one another over
10. miles of bush as if they represented every-
thing that was eternally opposed. The
teamsters, the drovers and the casual, float-
ing population sided with Burke; the other
station-people and the stockmen of Maloola
just as automatically took the part of Deni-
son. Yet no one knew what it was all
about. Even the teamsters who brought
the bulk of the Maloola stores (and inevit-
ably left the flour behind just when it was
needed) had no excuse for their partisanship
except that Larry was a good fellow and
that the girls always got together a good
meal when a man was hungry.
“We’ve got to stick to them,” they said
dogmatically.
Denison, on the other hand, talking to the
neighboring cattle-men, emphatically reiter-
ated that their interests were the same as
his own.
“We’ve got to get rid of the whole bunch
of them,” he repeated. “If they get to kill-
ing my cattle for beef they’ll kill yours, too.”
Camping out with the overseer and stock-
men his talk was always the same, and he
continually endeavored to wring from them
new ideas of retaliation. Yet he was dis-
tressingly conscious that he himself was
the only one whose heart was really in the
business. . Lying awake at night, with the
trees tracing fantastic patterns above his
head, he was haunted by an absurd feeling
of isolation in a hostile world. He saw
himself as a powerful old bull stricken down
by accident, and tormented by a swarm of
crows with faces like those of Larry Burke
and the Whelan boys.
“I’ve got to do something,” he said, rising
and flinging his nap away from him. “Damn
it all, I’m not going to be beaten by an
old waster who’s never owned a decent
horse or a fat bullock in all his born days.”
His chance came one morning when, rid-
ing out to the back of the run, he stumbled
upon four young steers lying all in a heap,
with an empty cyanide-tin in the middle of
them. He sat in the saddle looking at
them, the veins standing out in cords upon
his forehead, and his fingers clenched tightly
over the handle of his whip. It was hardly
a mile from the Whelans’ camp, and he had
heard that they had been illegally using
cyanide to poison ’possums.
Denison did not even wait to go back to
the homestead. It was the first time he had
got a clear opportunity to use the law with
effect, and he was going to lose no time in
geting out a warrant. Andy Whelan was
going to marry Delia Burke, so the overseer
said. Well, if he could get him and his
brother sent down for a good stretch, he
would be going a long way towards rooting
out the whole crowd.
He rode across country to the stock-route
gate, his long, lean face seeming to grow
more hatchet-like in its keen concentration,
and his eyes burning with an almost fanatic
intensity under the shaggy brows. On the
rails of the yard Larry Burke and the
Whelan boys were perched like gaunt birds,
and the sight of the familiar bay horse was
enough to bring a growl from Burke’s throat.
The very hair on his head seemed to bristle
with animosity, and he threw a chip
savagely at a mongrel cattle-dog that was
lying near a post.
“Git to him, Boney,” he hissed. “Git to
the old waster!”
Boney was a malicious dog and that was
the one kind of order he could enjoy. His
aged teeth sank home in the bay’s heel just
as its rider was leaning over to shut the
gate, and when the horse plunged Denison
fell like a sack of flour. A little later Burke
and. the Whelan boys were bending over him
feeling his arms and legs. “
“A bad break,” grunted Larry at last.
“It’s up to someone to get a doctor, I sup-
pose. .. .Damn that blasted dog !”
No one saw any irony in the curse, least
of all Denison. When he came round two
hours later he was lying between white
sheets and. feeling a little muzzy and senti-
mental. His head seemed to be comfortably
filled with cotton wool, and he only remem-
bered vaguely what had happened, but his
eyes, wandering vacantly round the room,
fixed on the bald spot on Larry Burke’s
head.
Larry was standing 10 feet away, with
his back turned and his hands in his pockets,

looking stolidly out of the window. There
was something about the hang of his shoul-
oeis. that was extraordinarily familiar to
enison, yet it was over 20 years since he
naa seen him at such close quarters. He
remembered, _ though, that that was always
;. ie attitude m which Larry had stood while
the billy was boiling at nightfall, his back
to the fire and his eyes looking into the
darkening timber.
Denison s mind worked round to the morn-
mg on which he and his old mate had last
words. It was in the horseyard
at Maloola, and the two of them had nearly
come to blows about the ownership of an
eight-year-old mare that had been found in
the ndges with no brand upon her. With
the overflowing amiability of the utterly
helpless he felt an extraordinary impulse to
1 the age-old controversy once more.
‘Larry!” he said.
Burke turned on his heel.
“Weil?”
‘That mare,” Denison fumbled. “I’ve
been thinking about her. I’m not sure she
belonged to Maloola after all.”
The admission caught Larry Burke in an
unprotected spot.
“Rot!” he grunted. “Who else would she
belong to?”
“She might have been yours,” said Deni-
son.
There was a short silence in which both
men were thinking 20 years back.
111 tell you what we’ll do,” said Denison
at last. “I got seven quid for her. We’ll
toss to see whether I keep it or pay it over
to you.”
He groped ineffectually for a shilling,
and then saw that his trousers were hanging
over the bed-rail. Larry brought out a
battered sixpence.
“As you like,” he faltered.
“It’s the only way out,” said Denison. “I
wonder we didn’t think of it before
Heads!”
VANCE PALMER.
Stiff Luck.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ABDI L handled No. 074 very roughly
on the Peninsula. He filled his left
leg with machine-gun bullets and
chipped off a portion of his right shoulder
with shrapnel. Afterwards No. 074 was
my closest neighbor for five weary weeks in
an Egyptian hospital; and during that time,
while other men cursed Abdul, the heat, the
flies, their wounds, the war, and the
monotony of inaction, he proved himself a
hero, as not one complaint was uttered by
him.
On my last day in the hospital, No. 074’s
leg formed a bone of contention between
three surgeons. After 15 minutes of exami-
nation and discussion, the conference decided
on a majority vote that on the morrow the
lead-filled limb would part company with
its owner’s body.
Stiff luck, old man,” I said to No. 074,
after the surgeons had passed on.
He looked across at me with a grin. “Don’t
mention it, mate,” he said. “Stiff Luck and
me s been keepin’ company for a long while
now. It was through Stiff Luck I lost me
greatest cobber an’ a ’eap of money. It
was Stiff Luck wot got me into this.
‘Squeaker Kelly was me cobber. A fair
dag Squeaker was, an’ clever too. Never
worked in ’is life, an’ never seen the inside
of gaol, though that wasn’t the police’s
fault. I run up against Squeaker first
"™ n , I was tonsorial artist at Soapy
Li hot s Elite ’airdressin’ saloon. Two quid
a week I was gettin’ for shavin’ the ’ardest
dials you’d find in Orstralia an’ asking ’em
whether they’d like their ’air cut Noo York
or fashion.
“ W ell, Squeaker keeps whisperin’ every
time we go down to the Arms of Bacchus
for a tid, ‘Wot’s the good of workin’?’ he
sez. ‘Work’s a evil, that’s wot it is. You
oughter put it in the same class as John
Ops, an’ plagues, an’ wowsers. You cut it
out an’ come along with me, an’ I’ll show
yer ’ow to write Gentlem’n of Independent
Means after yer name for life.’
t 4 I was just tliinkin’ of slingin’ the job,
’cause the manners of the blokes wot patron-
ised Soapy’s didn’t give the joint a charnce
to live up to its name, when one day Soapy
takes stock, an’ ’e makes some nasty remarks.
You bet yer life I’m not standing that, so
I picks up me lid, gives Soapy notice and
dodges the spittoon ’e ’urls at me as I walked
out.
“After that I slings in me fortunes with
Sq,ueaker for weal or woe, as lie sez.
Squeaker an’ me has a good time on the
whole, an’ we most times ’as a pocketful of
cash. ’Strewth, he was that clever tliinkin’
out schemes he oughter been a politician or
racin’ ponies. Our luck was bonzer. The
mugs we struck was dead easy. It was just
like blin’foldin’ them, an’ talcin’ the dough
outer their pockets. We made money, too,
runnin’ a two-up joint, but the Johns got
too inquisitive, an’ they put the ’unt on us.
Then we struck old Stiff Luck, an’ ’e never
left us. Stiff! If it was to rain quids
Squeaker an’ me would hav’ been struck
with paralysis so we couldn’t pick ’em up.
All the mugs seemed to have cleared outer
town, an’ it looked as if all the police force
had been given orders to let the criminals
go an’ keep their eyes on Squeaker an’ me.
“All the splosh Squeaker ’as is one and six,
a crook half-quid, an’ a double-headed penny.
My wealth’s down to a couple of sprats.
‘Look’s as if we’ll have to enlist or work,’
sez Squeaker, and if you’d ’eard him groan
when he said ‘work’ you’d thought there
was a lost soul floatin’ round.
“But I didn’t lose me faith in Squeaker,
and when I meets ’im the next night ’is
eyes is shinin’ bright, as if ’e’d picked up a
fiver. I tips he’s got the dinkum oil some-
where. I was right. Squeaker tells me
lie’s got a scheme to save us frim the rocks.
It’s a bit desperate, but he sez when a man’s
got an empty stummick an’ is down to his
last couple of bob it’s no time to be carryin’
kid gloves. Seems Squeaker’s been hangin’
round a big bettin’ club, where he’s pally
with one of the stewards, and he’s found
out that one of the big bookmakers is in
the ’abit of playin’ cards pretty ’eavy at
the Club after the races, and leaves in the
early hours carryin’ his takin’s on ’im through
the Gardens. Squeaker’s idea is we meet ’im
in a dark spot in the Gardens, give ’im a
gentle tap on the ’eadpiece, pinch the cash
an’ fade away, an’ get our beauty sleep while
the dorn’s paintin’ up the sky.
“ ‘Sounds easy,’ I sez, ‘but supposin’ the
bloke’s been skinned that night. Wot’d we
git for the risk?’
“ ‘ ’Strewth, but you make me tired,’ sez
Squeaker. ‘Carn’t you use yer imagination?
Ain’t ’e got a gold watch an’ chain, an’ don’t
’e wear as many diamond rings as a pawn-
broker’s missus?’
“That clinches it; so we goes an’ blues
in our capital on a supper. A bloke’s no
good if ’is stummick is a dead marine. Then
we ’angs about the Club, dodgin’ to beat the
Johns.
“It must have been about three o’clock when
the bookmaker bloke comes out and mooches
away towards the Gardens. We shadders
’im, ’n’ when lie’s goin’ along a dark path,
we creeps up. Squeaker gives the word, an’
we’re on to ’im. The light only lasted a
minute, but that bloke squirmed like an eel,
an’ bellowed like a bull, an’ the three of u*s
rolled over an’ over like seftls wrestlin’. At
last we gits ’is ’ead down on the grass, ’n’
puts a foot on ’is face, an’ our ’ands go
to ’is ’ip pocket simultan’ous. ’Strewth, but
the feel of them notes —Cripes! While
it ’ad me dazed, Squeaker jerks the roll outer
me ’and, and with ’is other ’ook he has the
bookie’s gold watch an’ chain.
“Just then we hears a sound like an ele-
phant rushin’ through the grass. ‘lt’s a
John! Iluck different wa'ys,’ Squeaker sez.
But I’m not losin’ sight of Squeaker when
’e’s got that roll of notes. So I toilers ’im,
an’ we races along the path, an’ outer the
Gardens "opposite a big ’ouse with two
motors standin’ in front. The John is cornin’
on about a ’undred yards behind. Squeaker
rushes across to one car, an’ tells me to ’op
in. Then he winds her up, climbs in, an’
with a jump we’re off, just as the John
dashes across the road. Motor drivin’ was
one of Squeaker’s old games.
“We got a fair start, but that John wasn’t
wastin’ time. He must have woke up the
driver of the other car, because when I
looks round the other car’s cornin’ on be’ind
us. In a couple of minutes we’re dashin’
through the city, an’ I can see ’em slow-
in' down once or twice an’ taking on a cargo
of Johns. Then we shakes the old hinjun
up ; but the other lot keeps gainin’ on us.
Squeaker has to turn off the big streets, an’
when we git twistin’ round the side streets
I wonder whether we ain’t blarsted avia-
tors. We slides round lamp posts, jumps
across gutters, pulls down a verandah post,
an’ —then dashes into a small street with a
dead end ! Squeaker sees it, puts on the
brakes, an’ the car pulls up in a yard. In
the centre there’s a big black buildin’ with
a tall chimbly. The Johns’ car comes snort-
in’ up to the gate, so we rushes across to
the buildin', an’ looks fer a place to nit.
There’s a row of tip-carts propped up
against the wall. Squeaker crawls under
the cover of one of ’em an’ I gets under the
cover of the next one, and we lies quiet.
“That cart might have been a ’appy ’ome
for a rat, but it was no good for a ’uman
being. It was full of rubbish, an’ I couldn’t
move, ’cause I was frightened of the tins an’
broken glass maltin' too much of a row. Me
’eart was thumpin’, an’ when I heard those
Johns searchin’ round the carts cold shivers
was runnin’ up ’n’ down me back.
“ ‘How about havin’ a look in them carts,
caretaker?’ sez one of the Johns.
“ ‘No charnce of ’em gettin’ in there.
The covers is fastened down to keep the
rats out, an’ no ’uman bein’ could live in
them carts,’ the bloke sez.
“He was right about them carts. The
stink was fair sickenin’. I catches meself
tliinkin’ how long a man’s supposed to live
in ’em. It must have been an ’our before
the Johns give up searchin’. When things
was dead-quiet I begins to wonder wot’s
’appened to Squeaker. He’s got the roll of
notes an’ the watch an’ chain. I was just
goin’ to drop out of the cart an’ have a
look to see whether Squeaker had died of
the hum when some blokes comes inter the
yard and begins to shovel coal, so I reckon
it’s time to see wot’s ’appened to Squeaker.
I lifts the cover an’ looks out, and then I
’ears footsteps cornin’ round the buildin’, an’
blarst me if two blokes don’t come an’ stand
beside a cart.
“Strewth, then they lets down a iron
door in the side of the buildin’, and I can
see flames leapin’ up inside, an’ I ’ears a
roar. The next thing they does is so sudden
that a lump comes inter me throat. ' They
goes to Squeaker’s cart, whips off the cover,
an’ gives the front of the cart a ’eave with
their shoulders.
“There’s a clatter o'f bottles an’ tins, an’
all the rubbish goes slidin’ inter the flames.
But I ’ears somethin’ else. It’s a screech—
somethin’ awful it was. Squeaker, I knew;
and wot I seen an’ heard fair paralysed me
for a couple of minutes.
“ ‘Must have been a dorg in there,’ sez
one of them, an’ they walks to another cart.
“That’s me charnce. I shins out an’
creeps across to a ’eap of stones against the
fence, an’ drops inter the street.
“ ‘What’s the tall chimbly?’ I sez to a
cove. ‘That’s a destructor,’ he sez, ‘where
they burn the rubbish.’
“ ‘lf a dorg got in there, wot chance would
he have?’ I sez.
“ ‘Buckley’s!’ he sez.
“Old Stiff Luck’s got our two hundred
lovely quid—it couldn’t ha’ bin less!
“Of course, I never told a soul *ow
Squeaker passed out, an’ 1 suppose ’is cob-
bers is still wonderin’ ’ow ’e did the dis-
appearin’ trick. But if old Stiff Luck lets
me git back to Melbourne again I’ll put up
a notice in the papers on the annivers’ry
of Squeaker’s death :
“KELLY—WiUkm.—To the memory of William
(.Squeaker) Kelly, who was cremated on 21st April,
1915, at Melbourne. (Inserted by a sorrowing
friend.) Stiff luck.” *
W. K.
